==PAGE:3==
III
Abstract
To meet the 12th sustainable development goal; ensure sus-
tainable consumption and production patterns before 2030, 
new solutions for minimising the use of resources are needed. 
During the Covid-19 pandemic, the takeaway market has ex-
panded which has resulted in increased amounts of resources 
used for single-use packaging.  
This master thesis has investigated how to develop a Reusable 
Product-Service Solution for The Delivery and Takeaway 
Food Industry, with a User-Centred Design Approach. This 
project has aimed to reduce the consumption of resources 
used for packaging for takeaway meals, as well as creating 
an innovative and user-friendly solution, which serves as the 
favoured alternative to single-use packaging. 
The scope of this project has been to develop a product-ser-
vice solution to be implemented in canteens. Here the canteen 
company Kokkenes Køkken has served as a case-partner. Af-
ter establishing a proof of concept and infiltrating the canteen 
market, the vision is to expand the reusable container system 
to the restaurant takeaway market. 
Through extensive research, including literature studies, 
market analysis, explorative user studies, ideation, concept 
development and evaluation phases, a final product-service 
solution, ROTINE, has been developed. Throughout the de-
sign process methods and approaches from circular economy, 
product-service system design, participatory design and be-
havioral design have been used to explore, analyse and eval-
uate. 
The ROTINE system consists of a reusable container and a 
service platform. The ROTINE container is designed for cir-
cularity and emotional durability. With its organic shape, the 
design is unique and aesthetically pleasing but also function-
al. The service platform includes a website for employees of 
canteens to manage menus, orders, containers and statistics as 
well as an app for consumers to view menus, order takeaway 
and manage their containers. 
Furthermore, the platform aims to motivate both employees 
and consumers by providing insights into the system’s sus-
tainability benefits. Before implementing the ROTINE system 
to Kokkenes Køkken’s canteens further work must be done, 
such as implementing final touches to the container before 
manufacturing and developing the service platform. 
The ROTINE container will break even in terms of CO2 emis-
sion compared to single-use at six uses. After the six uses the 
ROTINE container will become a more and more sustainable 
alternative to single-use packaging.

Preface
This report is created by Astrid Anna Toftegaard, Mike Slots-
borg, Sneaster Asrat and Signe Friis Schack and serve as 
documentation of the master thesis project Developing a Re-
usable Product-Service Solution for The Delivery and Take-
away Food Industry, with a User-Centred Design Approach. 
The project accounts for 30 ECTS points and has been con-
ducted as the final project on the master of Design and Inno-
vation at the Technical University of Denmark.
Reading Manual
This report consists of 13 chapters,
01/ Introduction 
02/ Literature Study
03/ Design Process
04/ Market research

Not all chapters are equally important for gaining an under-
standing of the project and its outcome. Chapter 02/ Literature 
Study can be used as an overview of methods and approaches 
used throughout the report, which can be consolidated while 
reading about the design process. Most chapters include an 
introduction and a summary or conclusion, which highlights 
the most important aspects from the chapter. The concept cat-
alogue concludes chapter 07/Ideation and Concept Develop-
ment and the solution catalogue concludes chapter 11/Detail-
ing the Final Solution. 
Three types of boxes are used throughout the report; notable 
information (blue boxes with blue icon), reflection (red boxes 
with red icon) and key insights (yellow boxes with yellow 
icon), see below. Key insights summarises the most important 
outcomes of either an activity or Section, and is therefore es-
sential to read. Information and reflection boxes include addi-
tional insights, which are not a part of the main report.  
Note that this report is prepared for print. Therefore it is rec-
ommended to use a two-sided view (with frontpage), if read-
ing this document digitally, as some figures are spread over 
two pages (if reading in Adobe Acrobat reader choose Show 
coverpage in two page view).
In addition to the report a Concept Catalogue and a Solution 
Catalogue are developed, which are handed in as separate 
documents. These represent the results of respectively chap-
ter 07/ and chapter 11/ and 12/ and are therefore essential to 
the project. 
Furthermore, there are two types of appendices; A and B. 
Appendix A serves as the main appendices supporting the re-
search and documentation of the project while Appendix B 
consists of original documents such as excel files and unpro-
cessed user data.
The appendices are in the report referred to as: A or B followed 
by the relevant chapter number and the chronological number. 
An example: A08.02 is the second A appendix relevant to the 
/08 chapter. Some appendices might contain several pages.
  
Tables, Figures and Pictures are referred to using the same 
logic: Section number, followed by the chronological number. 
E.g. Figure 04.6.1, meaning the first Figure in Section 04.6. 
All Tables, Figures and Pictures are created by the project 
group unless otherwise is stated.
Furthermore abbreviations are used throughout the report. 
These include;

01.1 Introduction
Climate change is one of today’s greatest challenges. For 
many years convenience has been one of the biggest priorities 
in a world of consumerism, causing a “take-make-dispose” 
mindset towards resources. This linear economy has espe-
cially been dominating when it comes to the takeaway food 
industry, where meals are purchased in single-use packaging. 
Furthermore, the Covid-19 pandemic has caused a significant 
increase in the takeaway food industry, as restaurants have 
been forced to close down for inhouse customers and only 
sell takeaway meals. A study estimates that 2.025 million sin-
gle-use containers are used for food in the European Union on 
a yearly basis (Gallego-Schmid et al., 2019). Another reason 
behind the increase in the takeaway food industry is due to 
the changed perception. Consumers have become aware of 
the possibility to purchase a good and healthy meal from local 
restaurants instead of the common fast foods. This was stated 
in an interview with Sisse Fagt from the National Food insti-
tute of Denmark, see Appendix B01.01 
If the 12th Sustainable Development Goal; ensure sustainable 
consumption and production patterns, set up by the United 
Nations, is to be met by 2030, new solutions rethinking pack-
aging for the delivery and food industry need to be developed 
and implemented (UN, n.d.).
Many initiatives have been made nationally as well as inter-
nationally to help minimise the use of resources and transition 
to a circular economy. Most recently a new waste directive 
has been approved by the European Union, which will be-
come effective by 2025 (EU Commision, 2018). New targets 
include recycling, packaging and landfilling as key elements 
in the Circular Economy Action Plan. The plan states that all 
packaging should be reduced by 65% in 2025. It is expected 
that the new legislation will provide an economic incentive 
to an Extended Producer Responsibility, entailing that the 
producers responsibility, which might also include producers 
of food, is extended to the post-consumer phase of the prod-
uct life cycle. This can potentially have consequences for the 
restaurant industry, if they do not accommodate for a more 
sustainable transition. Therefore the delivery and takeaway 
food industry needs to take action in implementing new al-
ternative solutions for their takeaway to mitigate this future 
legislation. 
01.2 Kokkenes Køkken as a Key 
Partner
Prior to this project a contact has been established to the can-
teen company Kokkenes Køkken (KK) through the special 
course Solution for handling dishes in canteens managed by 
Figure 01.2.1 Luups Bakken
==PAGE:13==
5
01/ Introduction
Kokkenes Køkken and concept solution for packaging of their 
takeaway (see Appendix B01.02). KK manages 25 canteens 
located in different areas in Denmark and are firstmovers 
when it comes to implementing innovative food solutions, 
while they aim to fight food waste and be sustainable. 
KK has developed a concept called SANS! (Danish abbrevi-
ation of: Smag - Ansvarlighed - Neurogastronomi - Sammen-
hæng), which among other things replaces the conventional 
buffet with pre-served meals, creating a smaller variety while 
increasing the quality of the dishes (see Appendix B01.03). 
An important aspect of the SANS! Concept is the implemen-
tation of neurogastronomy, which emphasizes the importance 
of all senses in an eating situation, including the surroundings 
and the sense of touch. Thus, aesthetics as well as the tactile 
feel of tableware is important to the overall eating experience. 
Elements from the SANS! concept have been implemented in 
all of KK’s canteens, and food waste has been reduced to less 
than 10% compared to 30% for traditional canteens according 
to the CEO of KK Maj Toppenberg. 
The output of the special course project was a product design 
of a tray (referred to as Luups Bakken) to make it easier for 
consumers to carry the pre-served meals. Luups Bakken has 
later on been produced in large scale to KK’s canteens. 
As KK wishes to be more sustainable, they have a vision to 
replace single-use packaging for their takeaway. A collabora-
tion for this project has therefore been established with KK, 
ensuring valuable insights to the canteens workways as well 
as experiences with serving food. Furthermore, KK allows the 
project group to test solutions at their facilities. 
The four stakeholders, that have been involved throughout the 
project are;
Maj Toppenberg: CEO of KK and founder of Luups 
ApS (selling Luups Bakken)
Teddy Rosé Losé: Head Chef at the canteen in Edison 
Huset
Rune Skov Petersen: Operations Manager  
Asmus Thorsøe Hamann Quality Manager 
01.3 Motivation
The execution of this project is motivated by the urge to de-
velop a sustainable and circular solution to replace disposable 
takeaway packaging. Choosing to order takeaway is a highly 
convenient choice for consumers, and changing the conven-
tions of the current use-patterns is both necessary in terms of 
reaching the 12th SDG but also highly challenging, as this 
requires a shift in user behaviour. 
As design engineers we are highly motivated to solve this 
wicked problem, by designing an innovative PSS with a us-
er-centred focus, that includes both industrial product design 
as well as holistic system design. Furthermore, working as a 
consultancy closely together with the canteen company Kok-
kenes Køkken is highly motivating, as a real life implementa-
tion of the final solution is feasible. 
01.4 Aim
This project aims to reduce the use of resources used for pack-
aging of takeaway meals to ensure sustainability and circu-
larity. The goal is to design a product-service system, that in-
cludes an innovative reusable container to replace single-use 
packaging, as well as a service platform, that facilitates and 
enhances the use of the container. The ultimate goal is to de-
sign a solution that is not only the most sustainable alternative 
to single-use packaging but also a favoured solution amongst 
the users.

02.1 Introduction
Several design practices have to be explored in order to incor-
porate relevant methods and approaches in the further work. 
The circular economy (CE) framework has been investigat-
ed in order to identify relevant design strategies and business 
models for developing a sustainable solution. A method for 
evaluating the environmental impact of the final solution is 
defined as well. The definition and methodologies related to 
developing a product service system have to be mapped out in 
order to act as a baseline throughout the development phase. 
Theories regarding behaviour and behavioral design tools are 
important to account for in order to apply behavioral termi-
nology and design approaches. Lastly, participatory design 
methods are defined and two different approaches highlighted 
to include user in the design process as a part of the user cen-
tered design approach. 
02.2 Sustainability, CE and LCA
Sustainability can be measured and evaluated using a range 
of different frameworks. Some of these are focused on de-
tailing and accounting for environmental impacts that can be 
attributed to products and systems, while other methods are 
more concerned with the broader picture and focus on the 
way resources flow through many different systems. In this 
report two systems are used to evaluate sustainability; circu-
lar economy is looking at the overall structure of the flow of 
resources, business model and product design, while Life Cy-
cle Analysis (LCA) accounts for the environmental impacts 
in more detail. In order to use the two frameworks to evaluate 
the environmental sustainability of takeaway packaging it is 
first important to determine how circular economy and LCA 
are related to environmental sustainability, which is the aspect 
of sustainability that is focused on in this project. 
Circular economy - a regenerative system 
Geissdoerfer et al. (2017) has investigated the differences 
and similarities between sustainability and circular economy. 
They define circular economy as a  
“(...) regenerative system in which resource input and waste, 
emission, and energy leakage are minimised by slowing, clos-
ing, and narrowing material and energy loops. This can be 
achieved through long-lasting design, maintenance, repair, 
reuse, remanufacturing, refurbishing, and recycling”. 
It provides an idea of how the economy (understood in a broad-
er sense and not just monetary) could be organised in order to 
minimise resource input and provide guidelines for how to do 
so. The motivations within the circular economy framework 
are clear: To make better use of resources by moving from a 
linear model to a circular one. One of the core elements in a 
circular economy is that economic actors are prioritised. They 
are the ones who drive the change and the benefits for busi-
nesses and governments have been stressed the most. Societal 
benefits are seen as a consequence of the beneficial changes to 
the economy and the environment (Geissdoerfer et al., 2017). 
The goals and motivations for sustainability are less clear and 
depend on the perspective of the user of the term and their in-
terests. Within the sustainability framework the societal bene-
fits are just as important as the economic and environmental. 
Sustainability is generally easier to fit into a wide range of dif-
ferent contexts and goals, as the frase is much looser defined 
than that of circular economy. The responsibility for carrying 
out the change is not placed on businesses and governments, 
but is diffusely divided between everyone in a common effort 
(Geissdoerfer et al., 2017). 
Circular economy does not encompass all the elements of sus-
tainability, but the relationship between the two is not wide-
ly agreed upon in literature, according to Geissdoerfer et al. 
(2017). In some of the literature circular economy is described 
as one of or the main conditions for a sustainable system. Oth-
ers view it as one solution out of many which is beneficial 
when building a sustainable system, but not the only way to 
go. Finally there are those who are more skeptical towards 
connecting circular economy with sustainability, either be-
cause they believe there are a lot of trade offs or because they 
emphasise that circular economy does not encompass all of 
the elements related to sustainability. 
The fact that the circular economy has clear goals and moti-
vations in line with the ambitions for producing a sustainable 
product system in this project, makes it a suitable framework 
for evaluating environmental sustainability as the design 
work progresses. Concrete design tools and business models 
for achieving a circular economy are described by Bocken et 
al. (2016). 
One very relevant business model for slowing down resource 
loops (keeping resources within use for a longer time or 
creating a more efficient use of resources) is the access and 
performance model. The aim of this model is to deliver ac-
cess to a product rather than ownership. A similar term for 
this kind of business model is a product-service system. The 
circular economy strategy is highly relevant for this project, 
as creating a product-service system is the aim for this de-
sign process. Creating a product-service system contributes 
==PAGE:17==
9
02/ Literature Study
to a circular economy, not only because it reduces the need 
for owning physical products. Businesses providing a prod-
uct-service system have a financial incentive to make long 
lasting products and to repair them in order to keep them in 
use for as long as possible, which slows down resource loops 
(Bocken et al., 2016). 
Design strategies for a circular economy includes design for 
trust and attachment. This strategy is focused on creating 
products that the user will love and therefore keep using and 
take care of. Another design strategy is designing for dis- and 
reassembly, which ensures that the product can stay in use 
for as long as possible (as fragile parts can be replaced= and 
effective recycling (parts made of different materials can be 
separated into different fractions). 
Another relevant design strategy described by Bocken et al. 
(2016) is designing for a technological cycle. This strategy 
is primarily focused on recycling materials, either through 
primary recycling (the recycled material has properties equal 
to the original material) or tertiary recycling (materials are 
broken down into their chemical components and remanufac-
tured). 
The circular economy framework provides tangible business 
models and design strategies, which can be used in the de-
velopment of the product service system. It is, however, not 
a framework which can provide exact measurements for en-
vironmental performance, which is why LCA is needed as a 
supplement.  
LCA as an evaluation tool
LCA is a method used to determine the most eco-efficient 
solution among alternatives, as stated by Moltesen & Bjørn 
(2018). It is focused solely on the environmental aspects of 
sustainability, and does therefore not comply with the under-
standing of sustainability as consisting of environmental, so-
cietal and economic dimensions. Moltesen & Bjørn (2018) 
stress that the LCA methodology has been developed in order 
to evaluate technological progress within manufacturing and 
determine the environmental impacts of different alternatives. 
LCA differs considerably from circular economy in the way 
they view the economic dimension of sustainability. The eco-
nomic dimension is prioritised in circular economy as it is the 
driver for change, but Moltesen & Bjørn view this differently. 
They stress that economic sustainability is only relevant when 
any gains are to the benefit of the poor, not just strengthening 
businesses. 
Moltesen & Bjørn see LCA as a valuable tool, but one that 
cannot stand alone in the efforts to transition into more sus-
tainable systems. There are no considerations as to whether 
the product or service is really valuable or necessary in a giv-
en system. 
In conclusion, both circular economy and LCA can be used to 
evaluate a sustainable product service system. They address 
some, but not all of the sustainability dimensions, environ-
ment, society and economy, though both of them address 
environmental sustainability, which is the main focus of this 
project. Design principles and business models aimed at cre-
ating a circular economy can be used in the design process. 
LCA is great for evaluating the final concept in order to deter-
mine whether the designed solution is preferable to existing 
alternatives in regards to environmental sustainability. 
The two frameworks will compliment each other in designing 
and evaluating the solution. 
sLCA - a simplified version
Conducting an LCA is a great way to evaluate the environ-
mental impact of a product or system, but it can be a very 
cumbersome task. The goal of this project is not to investigate 
the environmental impact of a new packaging solution in de-
tail, but rather to provide an estimate of whether the solution 
uses less resources than disposable packaging and roughly the 
order of magnitude. Therefore a simpler version of the LCA 
called a sLCA will be conducted as described by McAloone & 
Bey (2008). McAloone & Bey describes a seven step process. 
In this case four steps have been selected for the analysis: (1) 
describing the  use context, (2) gathering an overview of the 
environmental impact, (3) identifying hotspots and (5) quan-
tifying the environmental impacts.

A PSS concentrates on minimizing the environmental impact 
of consumption by e.g., closing material cycles, minimising 
consumption by changing how products are used, or/and 
increase the overall resource productivity and efficiency of 
PSSs (Mont, 2002).
Archetypes - classification of PSSs
There are many different classifications of PSSs. This project 
will use the eight archetypical PSS business models (Tukker, 
2004) as the main framework to analyse and develop the PSS 
(see Figure 02.3.1). The model shows the interval from pure 
products to pure services, with the three subcategories in the 
middle being a mixture of both products and services.
Figure 02.3.1 - Main and subcategories of PSS (Tukker, 2004) 
The sub-categories consist of:
•	
Product-oriented; the business model is mainly focused 
on selling products but with some extra services. 
•	
Use-oriented; products are borrowed, rented or leased. 
The provider owns the product. 
•	
Result-oriented; the end product is a result rather than a 
physical artefact. 
The solution will be evaluated based on the three sub-cate-
gories in order to determine the main orientation of the PSS. 
Barriers/limitations related to Product Service Systems
A PSS can demand both tangible and intangible sacrifices of 
the user, by implying the user would have to invest both time 
and effort into the PSS, e.g., learning how to use Bycyklen, 
which could end up lowering the potential market value of the 
PSS (Tukker, 2004). Furthermore, external barriers are often 
related to disagreements between stakeholder, suppliers, pro-
viders and/or customers, which can lead to distrust. Whereas 
the internal barriers often are related to barriers within an or-
02.3 Product-Service System 
(PSS)
Developing product-Service Systems (PSSs) has been a trend 
during the last decade. This trend is seen in systems such as car 
and bike-sharing systems, where users buy access to a service 
which allows them to go from A to B. The reason for the in-
creasing interest over the last couple of years is due to various 
benefits that PSSs provide, such as shifting towards sustain-
ability by applying changes in production and consumption 
patterns (Mont, 2002). As mentioned in the previous Section 
the PSS business model is one strategy for developing a circu-
lar economy, as described by Bocken et al. (2016). 
The most common motivational factors for choosing to devel-
op a PSS business model is to increase focus on sustainability, 
accessibility, availability, and ease of use, which is crucial in 
the conquest of developing a sustainable, reusable solution to 
the delivery and takeaway food industry (Mont, 2004). Other 
drivers would be related to legislation because of the rising 
focus on environmental impact, circular economy, efficient 
use of resources and new market possibilities (Mont, 2004).
This project seeks to utilise already existing use patterns to 
promote a more sustainable and profitable solution which 
counteracts consumerism. Additionally, our society and the 
rest of the world are finally starting to invest in the environ-
ment and sustainable initiatives. This will move markets away 
from costs and focus on quality and added value for the rel-
evant stakeholders (Mont, 2002). Therefore, by utilising the 
potential PSSs offer, it is possible to create value for both cus-
tomers, suppliers, users, the environment, and the rest of the 
entire system of interaction.
Tangible products and intangible services
As of now, there is no consensus on the definition of a PSS 
system, however, several prominent authors have come up 
with their definition, where one definition by Tukker and 
Tischner has been chosen as a starting point for this project. 
 
“Product service (PS): a value proposition that consists of a 
mix of tangible products and intangible services designed and 
combined so that they are jointly capable of fulfilling integrat-
ed, final customer needs. Product-service system (PSS): The 
product service including the (value) network, (technological) 
infrastructure and governance structure (or revenue model) 
that ‘produces’ a product service.” 
(Tukker & Tischner, 2006)

02/ Literature Study
PSSs provide. 
For companies, PSSs provides new business opportunities, 
hence staying competitive in an ever-evolving market. More-
over, PSSs can enhance and maintain the customer relation-
ship between customer and provider, increase affordability, 
and disconnect needs from material consumption. Further-
more, the economic improvements are related to a constant 
cash flow and closer contact with the customers during the life 
cycle (Mont, 2004). Therefore, it is a way for companies to 
compete on other value parameters than manufacturing cost 
alone (Kjaer, Pigosso, & McAloone, 2017)
Furthermore, manufacturing companies’ service components 
will have the possibility to add extra value to their products, 
by refurbishing or upgrading. It will also help them under-
stand the consumer preferences. 
For service companies, product components will extend and 
differentiate their service and make their service more tangi-
ble for common users (Mont, 2002).  
Lastly, the interesting aspect for companies to buy into prod-
uct service systems is that they have the ownership. Therefore, 
they would have to manufacture and develop solutions, prod-
ucts etc, which last longer and utilize energy and resources. 
For the government and society, PSSs will demand new pol-
icies due to the increased awareness and promote sustainable 
initiatives of consumption. Additionally, it could assist in cre-
ating new jobs due to new markets, and therefore less unem-
ployment (Mont, 2002). 
ganization, such as costs, conceptual design etc.
It is also necessary to find a social system or infrastructure 
which accepts or supports the initiatives of a proposed PSS 
solution. Finally, the most common barrier is to get customer 
acceptance of service-oriented solutions, such as Bycyklen 
(Mont, 2004). Furthermore, consumers might not be enthu-
siastic about going towards ownerless consumption (Mont, 
2002). This might be a hurdle in the future development of a 
use-oriented PSS for the delivery and takeaway food indus-
try. It is therefore important to have a user centered approach 
when developing the PSS. 
The potential barriers of the final solution will be evaluated in 
Section 12.6 (implementation plan). 
Sustainability benefits related to Product Service Systems
The sustainable benefits of PSS systems are described by 
Bocken et al. (2016) as a part of the Access and performance 
model (see section 02.2) from a circular economy point of 
view. The sustainability aspects are however investigated in 
the PSS literature as well. 
Consumers/users have the possibility of choosing between 
a wider variety of services and products, e.g., leasing, rent-
ing, maintenance and repair services. Therefore, the user has 
more options and can decide how much ownership they want 
through subscriptions- and payment-based models. This will 
make expensive things more accessible, provide the con-
sumer with new high combinations of products and services 
(Mont, 2002). Together, all this will add value to the user ex-
perience and motivate users to enter the market and choices

When aiming to replace single-use packaging with a reusable 
solution, it is essential to understand the underlying factors 
that drive human behaviour to ultimately design for sustain-
able behaviour. Behavioural design is therefore examined to 
explore how frameworks, principles and tools can be used to 
persuade and design for a desired behaviour. 
Behaviour
The dual process theory (DPS) states that there are two cog-
nitive states of the human mind that controls decision making 
and behaviour; system 1 and system 2 (Miltenberger, 2011 
and Nielsen et al., 2017). 
•	
System 1 (unconscious) are the automatic, autonomous 
processes that ensure fast decision making. 
•	
System 2 (conscious) is the rational mind, that requires 
working memory and decision making processes are 
therefore more deliberate and slower (see Figure 02.4.1)

Figure 02.4.1 - characteristics of system 1 and 2
The behavioural design consultancy firm KL7 describes two 
states of the cognitive mind as the monkey and the business 
suit (Mølgaard et al., 2015), where we as humans generally 
perceive humans as business suits, with the ability to make 
reasonable and well argumented decisions. However, in re-
ality it is the monkey that controls most of our behaviour 
and habits. The monkey is controlled by external or internal 
triggers, e.g. needs, situations, senses, rather than rational ar-
guments. The two states of the cognitive mind help describe 
why human behaviour is oftentimes difficult to change, as it is 
seldomly enough to target the rational and logical mind. 
Scoping the system
As stated by Kjaer, Pigosso, & McAloone, 2017, there are 
three ways to scope the development of a new PSS; PSS con-
sequences, PSS comparison and PSS optimisation, where the 
PSS consequence and optimisation both have an external ref-
erence system and the PSS optimisation has an internal refer-
ence system. In this project the scope is to go from a product 
reference system to a PSS and the scope is therefore PSS con-
sequence (see Figure 02.3.2). 
The Proteus handbook by Finken et al. (2013) will be used in 
the further development of the PSS and supplemented by an 
environmental evaluation in the form of an sLCA (see section 
02.2).
The methods and framework will help aid the understanding 
of all relevant customers/users, the key elements of the com-
pany’s technology offerings, and the value delivery process 
from supplier to final receiver of the PSS (Finken et al., 2013).

02.4 Behavioural design
When developing a reusable product-service solution for the 
delivery and takeaway food industry, it is relevant to exam-
ine behavioural design, as such a solution will most likely 
require a change in the user behaviour or habits. The current 
use patterns of the takeaway industry is to buy ready made 
==PAGE:21==
13
02/ Literature Study
Besides targeting either unconscious or conscious behaviour 
change (implicit/explicit), Nielsen et al. (2017), suggests fur-
ther elements to consider when developing a strategy; 
•	
The three factors: Motivation, Ability and Trigger 
(this will be further described in the Persuasive design 
section)
•	
Whether the solution should either positively encour-
age the new behaviour or negatively discourage the old 
behaviour. 
Furthermore, strategies can be either structural or informa-
tional. Structural strategies aim to change contextual factors, 
by e.g. limiting access to products that facilitates a certain 
behaviour (Cash, et al., 2017). Where informational strategies 
are more focused on changing knowledge, awareness, norms 
and attitudes (Abrahamse & Matthies, 2016). Structural inter-
vention strategies can be very efficient, however, removal of 
choice is often perceived as negatively encouraging. 
Figure 02.4.3 suggests a way to categorise four types of inter-
vention models according to whether it is targeting the uncon-
scious or conscious cognitive state of mind and whether it is a 
structural or informational intervention. The four intervention 
models are described as following (Michie et al. 2011): 
•	
Coercive: creating expectation of punishment or cost
•	
Decisive: deciding by removing choice or ability
•	
Seductive: unconsciously and gradually affecting be-
haviour
•	
Persuasive: using communication to induce positive 
or negative feelings or stimulate action (persuasive 
approaches are well researched and described in liter-
ature, and are therefore investigated further in the next 
section).
Khadilkar et al. (2020) claims that most behavioural design 
strategies have a complex mix of behavioural design elements 
to enable a target behaviour, and a robust behavioural design 
process includes the exploration of different strategies and in-
tervention models. 
Persuasive design
Persuasive design is described by Fogg (2009), who states 
that there are three factors that are essential to affect a per-
son’s behaviour; motivation, ability and triggers/prompts. He 
affirms “that for a person to perform a target behavior, he or 
she must (1) be sufficiently motivated, (2) have the ability to 
Behavioural design
Khadilkar & Cash (2020) propose the following definition for 
behavioural design: 
“Behavioural design has the goal to explicitly and ethical-
ly realise positive behaviour, desired by both the individual 
and society; the object of design is behaviour itself, which 
is explicitly understood and designed for using behavioural 
theories and brought into effect by actively changing user 
psychology with the help of artefacts.”
According to Mølgaard et al. (2015), the main purpose of be-
havioural design is to bridge the gap between intentions and 
actual actions. 
When looking at a behaviour as an action, this can be de-
scribed by the ABC model, as consisting of three linear steps 
(Miltenberger, 2011); 
Antecedent (trigger)
Behaviour
Consequence (result)
The antecedent is the step where the mind is triggered to be-
gin a certain behaviour, and the consequence is the outcome 
of the behaviour. Interventions to change behaviour can hap-
pen in all three steps. 
Furthermore, behavioural design strategies can target either 
the unconscious state of mind (system 1) - an implicit inter-
vention, or the conscious (system 2) - an explicit intervention. 
Implicit and explicit interventions can through an artefact 
be coupled with one of the ABC steps. Strategies can also 
combine multiple steps of the ABC model (see Figure 02.4.2) 
(Cash, et al., 2017).

3.	
Physical effort; if too much physical effort is re-

4.	
Brain cycles; thinking in new ways or of difficult 
issues requires ability, and can therefore be a barrier

The “simplicity profile” is different from person to person, as 
some people have more money than others, while some have 
more time, etc. 
The third factor in the FBM are triggers, these can be divided 
into three different categories; (1) spark, (2) facilitator and 
(3) signal. Spark is a type of a trigger that sparks users with 
low motivation provoking any of the core motivators. If the 
user has high motivation but low ability a facilitator can be 
used, as facilitators are triggers that make it easier to perform 
the action. Signals as triggers are most efficient when users 
are both highly motivated and have high ability, these mainly 
serve as reminders. 
perform the behavior, and (3) be triggered to perform the be-
havior.” All three factors must furthermore occur at the same 
time. The Fogg Behavior Model (FBM) describes the rela-
tionship between the three factors (see Figure 02.4.4). 
If the user is highly motivated, he or she is more likely to per-
form the target behavior (go above the action line), whereas if 
the user is not motivated, it becomes more difficult to perform 
the action. The same goes for the ability, the easier it is for the 
user to perform the action, the likelier it is for the user to cross 
the action line. Thus higher motivation and ability increases 
the likelihood for the user to perform a target behaviour. How-
ever, high motivation and ability is not enough, as the user 
also needs to be triggered for a behaviour to occur. 
There are three main motivators; (1) sensation: pleasure/pain, 
(2) anticipation: hope/fear and (3) social cohesion: accep-
tance/rejection. Ability refers to how easy it is for the user to 
perform the action. Humans are fundamentally lazy, and are 
generally resistant to learning new things. Therefore simplici-
ty is key. Fogg describes six elements of simplicity; 
1.	
Time; if a target behaviour takes too much time, the 
behaviour is not simple 
2.	
Money; if people are financially limited this can

02.5 Participatory design
This project aims to understand the user experience when in-
teracting with takeaway packaging. In order to map out the 
full experience it is important to not only listen to what users 
have to say, but actively involve them in the design process 
as well. Participatory design methods aim to uncover the 
latent user experience and behaviours and will therefore be 
used in the research, design and evaluation phase of this proj-
ect. Working with participatory design methods is one of the 
methods chosen for developing a user centered design. 
A wide range of participatory design methods exists (see e.g. 
Hansen et al., 2019), resulting in a variety of different methods 
and approaches. The participatory design approach applied in 
this project is based on elements from Sanders & Stappers 
(2012), who call their approach generative design, and Beyer 
& Holtzblatt (1998) contextual design approach. How the two 
approaches define participatory design, how they understand 
the user experience and which methods they apply throughout 
the design process will be outlined below. Additionally the 
co-creating method is introduced and its relation to participa-
tory design defined.

Understanding the users experience - design research
As the users are experts in their own experience the challenge 
is to gain the insights that they can provide. Both Sanders & 
Stappers (2012) and Beyer & Holtzblatt (1998) agree that deep 
understanding cannot be gained through interviews alone, as 
the users will seldom be able to express their emotions, de-
sires and knowledge. Important elements of their experience 
are hidden from the conscious mind. How these latent parts of 
the experience are accessed differs within different participa-
tory design methods. 
Beyer & Holtzblatt (1998) emphasises the importance of be-
ing present with the user as they are in the context of the ex-
perience. Their approach is focused on designing IT systems 
for work practices, so they are stressing the importance of the 
designer actively participating in the work practice through 
the master/apprentice relationship, as described in the previ-
ous section. The context itself will probe the user to reveal 
much more details about their experience, as they are able to 
express tacit knowledge to the designer by actually playing 
out their experience in the familiar context. 
Sanders & Stappers (2012) have a slightly different approach. 
In their approach the experience happening now can not be 
separated from the memories from our past, which should be 
investigated before moving forward towards the dreams for 
the future. This framework, which Sanders & Stappers call 
the path of expression (2012) is based on the perception of 
creativity introduced in 1926 by Graham Wallas in The Art of 
Thought. Wallas states that it is essential for creativity that the 
mind is introduced to work that focuses on the problem and 
then has time to work with the problem in the unconscious 
mind (see Figure 02.5.1). 
Explore the present
Think about the 
past
Find the 
underlying layers
Move towards 
the future
Figure 02.5.1 - Sanders & Stappers framework for using the present 
and the past to imagine the future (adapted from Sanders & Stapper 
(2012)) 
Definition of participatory design
Sanders & Stappers (2012) describes participatory design as 
“an approach to design that attempts to actively involve the 
people who are being served through design in the process 
to help ensure that the designed product/service meets their 
needs”. 
Participatory design can be seen as a way to ensure a more 
democratic design process, as users are actively engaged in 
developing solutions that benefit them (Hansen et. al, 2019)
An important element in participatory design is using phys-
ical artefacts to communicate with users and organise user 
insights (Sanders & Stappers, 2012). 
It can be used primarily for the fuzzy front end of design as 
Sanders & Stappers (2012) do it (though it is argued that gen-
erative design approaches can take place at any point in the 
design process), but tools and approaches from participato-
ry design can also be used throughout the entire design pro-
cess and even as an evaluation tool, as suggested by Beyer & 
Holtzblatt (1998) or Hansen et. al (2019). 
The role of the intended end user is an important character-
istic of participatory design, but what exactly the role is can 
differ within different approaches. In the participatory design 
approach described by Beyer & Holtzblatt (1998) the rela-
tionships between the user and the researcher/designer should 
be characterised as a master/apprentice relationship. The mas-
ter is the user, who will show and explain their actions while 
the apprentice (designer) listens and asks questions.
Sanders and Stappers (2012) describes this relationship a little 
differently within generative design research. They define the 
role of the researcher as being a facilitator, where the main 
job of the researcher is to allow the user’s creativity to be 
expressed by introducing them to tools that allow them to do 
so. The designers role within workshops is to bring all of their 
expertise within their field and practical experience with pro-
totyping. 
Despite some differences in the way the role of the user and 
the designer/researcher is described one thing is widely agreed 
upon: The user is the expert of his/hers own experience. This 
mindset is at the core of participatory design. 
Beyer & Holtzblatt do not explicitly state that they view their 
practice as participatory design, but it makes sense to include 
their methods and approaches here, as the user is actively in-
volved in the contextual design practice. 
==PAGE:25==
17
02/ Literature Study
In practice, sensitizing is often done by asking users (who are 
later participating in a generative workshop session) to docu-
ment their experiences with the wider research topic for a pe-
riod of time before the workshop (Sanders & Stappers, 2012). 
This is typically done by asking them to write a diary or fill in 
a workbook provided by the research team (which can include 
prompts, placeholders for pictures, stickers, etc.). 
Observation - what users do
Both generative design and contextual design highlights that 
observations of the users actions is an important part of un-
derstanding their experience, but active observation through 
the master/apprentice relationship is at the very core of the 
contextual design approach described by Beyer & Holtzblatt 
(1998). 
The master/apprentice approach to design research has the 
observation of the experience as the starting point for all in-
teractions. The goal of the observation is therefore in line with 
the generative design approach, which is also aiming at un-
covering tacit and latent knowledge. 
Observation in the contextual design framework should not 
stand alone, as continuous questions from the apprentice are 
essential for uncovering further details and encourages the 
user to talk about previous experiences. The uncovering of 
Sanders & Stappers furthermore stress the importance of us-
ing three different types of research tools, which are distin-
guished by what kind of activity the users are participating 
in: Do, Say and Make. The three categories are addressing 
different parts of the timeline, as do techniques are concrete 
and focused on the present (could be enactment or roleplay-
ing exercises), make techniques can be more abstract and are 
good for retrieving memories or imagining the future (e.g. 
prototyping) and say techniques can assist in covering the rest 
of the user experience (including more traditional methods 
such as interviews, surveys and storyboards). 
Participatory design applied
Participatory design can be applied to all parts of the design 
process to ensure that the user is included. Figure 02.5.2 be-
low illustrates participatory design methods that are applied 
from the early research phase to the evaluation of a prototype. 
Sensitizing
This step is an important part of generative design research 
(it is not a part of the contextual design approach), as it intro-
duces the users to the context of a problem and allows the un-
conscious mind to work with the subject matter before being 
creative in a generative session.

feel, they are objects that are meant to be interacted with and 
actively used in a conversation with the user. It is important 
that this prototype does not look finished or unchangeable, as 
the goal is to invite the user to make changes and suggestions. 
The user is encouraged to act out the intended scenario and 
use the prototype actively in order to reveal how the changes 
in design might affect the interaction. 
Sanders & Stappers agree with Beyer & Holtzblatt that tra-
ditional prototyping activities are not enough to explore the 
more complex design challenges we face today. They argue 
that the way a designer bridges the gap to the user is by mak-
ing, telling and enacting. Making activities is e.g. to build tra-
ditional prototypes, but also props that can be used to make 
stories and enactments more realistic and detailed. Enacting 
focuses on the actual interaction and therefore enactment with 
props gives a much deeper experience than simply looking 
at a prototype. Storytelling allows the designer to place the 
design in the emotional context. 
A combination of the methods described by Sanders & Stap-
pers and Beyer & Holtzblatt will be used throughout this 
design project. The main focus will be on generative design 
research, including activities such as sentisising and do, say 
and make activities. Elements from contextual design will, 
however, be applied as well, with a special focus on active 
observations through the master/apprentice relationship. As 
described in the previous sections the two approaches have 
many things in common, and are therefore easy to combine. 
Co-creation
Co-creation methods are closely related to participatory de-
sign, as it is a way to engage users in the design process. 
Mattelmäki & Visser (2011) describe the relation between 
participatory design and co-creation as tight, as both design 
approaches are concerned with empowering the user to take 
part in the design process. 
Co-creation practices are characterised by changing the role 
of the designer into a facilitator, who provides tools for the 
user and prompts them to come up with their ideas and con-
cepts (Mattelmäki & Visser, 2011). Generative design has 
co-creation elements in the workshop formats, as participants 
are asked to prototype and come up with new concepts with 
the help of probs delivered by the design team. 
Co-creation practices will be implemented throughout the de-
sign process. 
previous experiences is in line with the generative design ap-
proach as well as described above, as the memories of the past 
are processed before imagining the future. 
The generative design approach does not have rigid require-
ments for where observations should take place, as it could be 
both in the context of the experience or within a generative 
workshop session. 
Analysis
All of the user data collected in the previous phase needs to 
be organised in an effective manner in order for it to become 
useful during the design phase. 
In the contextual design approach analysis is done by creating 
five different workmodels, each focused on a specific aspect 
of the context: physical, artefact, sequence, flow and cultural. 
The models map out important elements in the user context 
and forces the designer to notice details within the five areas. 
Notes taken during the interaction with the user will inform 
the models and all of the information gathered will contrib-
ute to an affinity model, which is the first point where the 
designers gather all observations and quotes into meaningful 
statements. After creating the affinity models (which is an ac-
tivity that all members of the design team participate in) the 
work models are consolidated and updated based on the new 
collective insights. 
Generative design has the same basic approach to data han-
dling. It is stressed by Sanders & Stappers that the extent of 
the analysis depends on how big the project is, what the re-
sources are and what the designers want to gain from the user 
data. One option is to do an analysis on the wall, which is very 
similar to the affinity diagram created in the contextual design 
analysis. The data in the generative session is organised into 
themes, rather than statements. 
Bridging the gap
Effective communication with the user is an essential part of 
maintaining user involvement when progressing from the re-
search phase to the design phase. How this process is facil-
itated and how much the users are engaged depends on the 
approach to participatory design. A traditional way to achieve 
this is by making prototypes.  
In the contextual design approach described by Beyer & 
Holtzblatt (1998) the designers will make the first prototypes 
based on all of the insights they have gathered in the initial 
research phase. These prototypes should not, however, be 
viewed as models for how the end product should look or

02.5 Summary 
The literary study forms the foundation for the methodologi-
cal toolbox used throughout the research and design process. 
Designing for sustainability can be done by applying ele-
ments from the circular economy framework such as business 
models and design strategies. A simpler form of the life cycle 
analysis called sLCA can be used for evaluating the environ-
mental impact of the final solution. 
The PSS framework is closely linked to the circular econo-
my framework, as creating a PSS is one of the strategies for 
achieving a circular economy. Designing a PSS is related to a 
wide range of methodologies, which can be used in creating, 
describing and evaluating the solution. 
Behavioural design methods can be used for changing be-
haviour by introducing coercive, decisive, seductive and per-
suasive intervention models. Ability, motivations and triggers 
will determine whether the user will perform the targeted be-
haviour. 
Participatory design methods empowers the users to share 
their experiences and thereby affect the design methods. Gen-
erative design and contextual design are both forms of partic-
ipatory design, but represent different approaches, which can 
supplement each other in the further design process. 
As the theoretical foundation has now been established the 
rest of the design process can be approached.

03.1 Introduction
To develop a reusable product-service solution for the de-
livery and takeaway food industry, a user-centred design ap-
proach is key. Changing the behaviour and shifting the owner-
ship of packaging requires a good understanding of the users. 
But in order to develop a feasible and sustainable business 
model a business perspective needs to be incorporated into 
the approach when designing for the holistic system. Further-
more, as the main aim is to reduce the use of resources used 
for packaging of takeaway meals to ensure sustainability and 
circularity, design for sustainability and circularity is equally 
important. Thus it was decided that the three main perspec-
tives of designing the PSS would be; user centred design, 
business design and design for sustainability and circularity 
(see Figure 03.1.1).

for The Delivery and Takeaway Food Industry

process
03.2 Design Thinking, Agile and 
Lean Startup
To find the best design methodology and structure the initial 
plan of the design process, elements from Design Thinking, 
Agile Development and Lean Startup were investigated. 
Design Thinking supported by Agile principles and Lean 
Startup
Design thinking is a methodology applied for approaching 
complex and ill-defined problems (wicked problems) in an ef-
fective and creative way. The five stages of the methodology 
(emphatise, define, ideate, prototype and test) helps in under-
standing the unmet needs of the user to reduce risks associat-
ed with launching a product or service, generating innovative 
solutions and making it easier to learn and iterate faster. 
However, design thinking lacks clear directions on how to ap-
proach the problem-solving part from a project management 
perspective, where being efficient, competitive and costly 
plays a great role. Incorporating the four principles of agile 
can supplement the prior methodology by introducing con-
straints in time, prioritisation and focus in the design process. 
This synergy can facilitate an effective production and imple-
mentation of creative ideas. Furthermore, the business aspects 
of the agile-design thinking hybrid are supplemented with the 
Lean Startup methodology (see Figure 3.2.1). While the two

03/ Design Process
other methodologies focus on testing the product against the 
users, the Lean Start-up focuses on testing the product against 
the market to ensure that the product sells. The following 
sub-sections will provide a further description of the elements 
used in the agile and lean start-up methodology. 
The Agile Manifesto
The agile methodology is based on the Agile Manifesto draft-
ed by a group of software developers in 2001 (The Agile 
Manifesto for Software Development, n.d.). It emphasises on 
four principles that demand for the use of iterative methods 
for product development:
1.	
Individuals and interactions over processes and 
tools (research, define and ideate phases)
2.	
Working software over comprehensive documen-
tation 
3.	
Customer collaboration over contract negotiation 
(research, define and ideate phases)
4.	
Responding to change over following a plan (proto-
type, test phases)
These principles are appropriate in projects where the level of 
uncertainty is high, as it provides an agility to respond to un-
known unknowns with less impact on time and cost  (Darrin 
& Devereux, 2017). It is important to highlight that the sec-
ond principle is not as applicable as the others in this project, 
as it is a part of an academic report where documentation is 
required. In addition, working agile is often associated with 
software development, where it is easy to deploy and deliver 
working software. However, this is not a software project, but 
a design project as a part of a master thesis. A working proto-
type is valued over comprehensive documentation, but within 
the lines of reason.
Lean startup
The Lean Startup methodology builds on many of the ideas 
seen in management and product development including de-
sign thinking, agile development, lean manufacturing and 
customer development (Ries, 2011). However, the method-
ology differs by introducing the following five principles to 
scientifically manage and build a successful business and 
startup:
1.	
Entrepreneurs are everywhere (ability to design 
new solutions under uncertain conditions)
2.	
Entrepreneurship is management (ability to manage 
in a context of extreme uncertainty)
3.	
Validated learning (learn how to build a sustainable 
business)
4.	
Innovation accounting (measure progress, set up 
milestones, prioritise work)
5.	
Build-Measure-Learn (turn ideas into products, 
measure customer response, pivot or persevere)
The primary purpose of using Lean Startup in this project 
is not to create a startup, but to build a sustainable business 
model by following some of the principles described above. 
Principle 5 explaining the Build-Measure-Learn will espe-
cially be used to continuously adjust the developed solution 
according to the measured customer response. This will be 
done by creating Minimum Viable Products (MVP) through-
out the design process to test the elements in the PSS. 
Approaching the agile aspect by Scrum 
While the agile principles provide the overall guideline, the 
specific steps are found in different methods such as Crystal 
Clear, Scrum, Kanban, etc. The design process in this project 
is supported by aspects of the Scrum framework in a degree 
found relevant to promote a better response to changing stake-
holder requirements, improved team communication, morale 
and lastly also increased development productivity, in har-
mony with the Design Thinking principles and Lean Startup 
methodology. 
The overall activities in this design process are mapped out in 
a Gantt Chart (see Appendix A03.01) with milestones indicat-
ing the start of a new phase. Each phase is seen as a sprint with 
a maximum duration of five weeks. By the end of each sprint, 
a physical product is released in the sense of e.g. a literature 
study, written document, prototype, etc. This redefinition to 
include a physical product is an adaptation to the framework, 
as the sprints are usually restricted to the prototype and test 
phase in a software development context (Cooper, 2016). The 
daily work is organised by two Daily Scrum meetings to in-
spect and adapt progress towards the Sprint Goal. The first 
Scrum meeting is performed in the morning before initiating 
the work where the plan for the day is set, while the second 
one is performed by the end of the day as a wrap up.
The remaining activities are performed as described in the 
Scrum Guide, however, in a less formal way as e.g. the roles 
of being Product Owner, Developer and an adaptation of the 
SCRUM master role was implemented, as one member of the 
group was responsible for the time management and external 
communication each week.  (Schwaber, 2015). Furthermore, 
the sprints were intended to be more usable in the ideate, pro-
==PAGE:34==
26
The branch of business design is of main primary focus in 
the initial stages of the design process in order to ensure that 
the PSS is developed for a feasible market. Hereafter, the 
user-centred design approach has served as the main driver 
for the development of the solution. CE and sustainability 
strategies have been used towards the end of the design pro-
cess to help make final decisions on e.g. materials as well as 
evaluating and comparing the final solution against single-use 
packaging.

==PAGE:38==

04.1 Introduction
In order to develop a successful PSS it is important to look at 
the business perspective of the reusable product service sys-
tem by exploring the delivery and takeaway food industry. A 
wide range of different industries could match this category, 
ranging from online supermarkets to to-go coffee purchased 
from a cafe. The following distinctions have been provided, in 
order to make the intended system a bit more defined,
 
1.	
“Food” is defined as some kind of meal, not exclu-
sively drinks or groceries. 
2.	
The system has to include some  kind of transport. A 
hospital serving food to patients does not fit within 
this description, as the meals never leave the premise. 
Transport can mean both delivery and consumers 
collecting the meal themselves. 
3.	
The system has to include some kind of packaging. 
Plates carried from one place to another do not count 
as packaging.
 
The purpose of the market analysis is to identify markets 
which match these distinctions and evaluate the possibilities 
and challenges in creating a reusable packaging PSS solution. 
This analysis will provide a basis for identifying blue oceans 
within potential markets by looking at opportunities and key 
competitors. An approach for entering the selected market 
will then be presented. Lastly, calculations regarding the size 
of the market and feasibility will be presented. 
 
The market analysis has been conducted primarily through 
desk research, which has been great for finding published 
data on the markets investigated, analysing other markets and 
published information on key competitors. Information about 
market trends and specific data for the canteen market has 
been provided by the CEO from KK (see Section 01.2). In 
order to understand the business model of key competitors 
research “in the field” has been conducted as well, though 
these activities have been limited due to the pandemic. A 
competitor’s product service system has been tried out simply 
by downloading the app and visiting one of the collaborating 
coffee shops. 
04.2 Potential Markets
Based on the defined distinctions described in the previous 
Section, potential markets were identified in a brainstorm. 
The six potential markets for implementing a packaging PSS 
are listed on Figure 04.2.1. 
The markets have been identified in an initial discussion. The 
descriptions for each market (see Appendix A04.01), pros and 
cons (see Appendix A04.02) are based on prior knowledge 
and assumptions about the market. The purpose of the over-
view was to gain an understanding of what kinds of takeaway 
markets exist and which ones would be interesting to further 
analyse. 
Evaluation Matrix
The six markets were mapped in a matrix with feasibility on 
one axis and impact on the other, in order to evaluate which 
market provides the best opportunity, see Figure 04.2.2. Some 
of the markets were thought to have a limited impact, as it 
is estimated that a reusable packaging solution will not have 
a big environmental impact nor a high potential profit. One 
example is the meal box market, where a lot of the packaging 
is being brought back to the producers already. Other markets 
have a high impact, but low feasibility, as it would either be 
hard to find collaboration partners or the system is too com-
plex, such as the supermarket convenience meals, where the 
amount of involved stakeholders makes it hard to enter the 
market. 
The takeaway for restaurants market was estimated to have 
the biggest impact, but is also difficult to enter due to the com-
plexity of dealing with a diverse range of restaurants. The can-
teens are in a sweet spot with a combination of high feasibil-
ity, e.g. due to existing contacts within the canteen business, 
and high impact, as a lot of people have access to a canteen 
through their workplace. Therefore it was decided to further 
explore both the market of canteens and takeaway for restau-
rants. There are relevant trends within both of these markets,

04/ Market Analysis
such as increased focus on sustainability, which makes the 
two markets even more relevant to explore (see Appendix

the two markets could now be explored.  
04.3 Key Competitors 
Competitors and relevant businesses were explored through 
desk research. An  overview can be found in Appendix 
A04.04. 
Based on the research three key competitors were highlight-
ed; Kleen Hub and Dabba in Denmark and reCIRCLE in 
Switzerland and Germany. 
Kleen Hub is the only player in Denmark providing a reus-
able solution for takeaway meals with the vision of building a 
network in the restaurant and cafee industry (KleenHub.dk). 
Kleen Hub is still a fairly new concept, and has not yet dom-
inated the market in Denmark, though the business is grow-
ing rapidly, as they have expanded from very few locations to 
about 60 in the last couple of months. Kleen Hub is therefore 
viewed as a direct competitive threat. They provide both box-
es for takeaway and reusable cups, see Appendix A04.05. 
An even younger company, Dabba, has established a business 
based on selling lunch in reusable boxes, which they deliver 
by bike within the 8000 postal code around Aarhus (Dabba.
dk). Dabba is therefore viewed as a key competitor, as they 
are both delivering takeaway in reusable boxes as well as 
competing with the existing canteens. 
In Switzerland reCIRCLE has succeeded in making a sustain-
able business of providing a network of reusable packaging, 
with about 1500 partner restaurants (reCIRCLE.ch). reCIR-
CLE therefore provides a good case to learn from, but should 
also be viewed as a competitive threat, as they are likely to 
expand to the rest of Europe and has already expanded to 
Germany. More information about reCircle can be found in 
Appendix A04.06.
An overview of the three competitors can be seen in the info-
boxes presented on the next page.

The experiences with the KleenHub system and the overview 
of relevant competitors painted a picture of a developing mar-
ket where there is still room for innovation and improvement. 
An evaluation of the general competitiveness and potential 
value was, however, still needed in order to evaluate which 
market to choose for further development. 
04.4 Competitiveness and Value 
To analyse and define the competitiveness of the potential 
market segments within canteens and takeaway from restau-
rants Porter’s Five Forces Framework was used (as described 
by Kippenberger, 1998). The five factors are buyers power, 
suppliers power, threat of new entry, threat of substitution and 
intensity of rivalry, which are evaluated and listed in Appen-
dix A04.08. 
The market competitiveness is in general quite low for both 
of the market segments with the only real competitor being 
Kleen Hub in Denmark in the takeaway from restaurant mar-
ket. It is therefore concluded that a solution for both markets 
is feasible. However, a rise of competitors is expected within 
the near future, especially for the restaurant industry consid-
ering the growing sustainability focus and the roll-out of the 
new EU legislation in 2025, which intends to reward compa-
nies with sustainable design solutions and economic incentive 
(European Commission, n.d.). 
Companies that provide more single-use packaging, claiming 
to be more sustainable, are viewed as indirect competitors. An 
example is the Danish Re-Bag that has developed biodegrad-
able packaging, which has a reduced environmental impact 
compared to conventional bags left in nature (https://www.
re-bag.dk/). 
To gain more insights on the user experience of Kleen Hub the 
thesis group went to Coffee Collective, the first partner café 
(launched the 17th of February), to try out the system (see 
Figure 4.3.1 and Appendix A04.07). 
Here it was found that Coffee Collective had created their 
own monetary incentive for its customers to use Kleen Hub’s 
system by providing a 10% discount on coffee sold in Kleen 
Hub’s cups . Through an informal interview with the baris-
ta, it was found that Coffee Collective pays a “very small” 
amount for the service, and was happy to be able to provide a 
sustainable alternative to single-use cups. 
Tracking of containers worked by scanning a barcode with 
the app, which linked a container to the personal app-account. 
As cups could be returned in all of Coffee Collective loca-
tions, Coffee Collective had to distribute the cups internally 
between their different coffee shops. 
The barista did not like the design of the cups, as she thought 
it was way too big for the coffee portions. She also pointed 
out that the lid was very unfortunate, as it could not be sealed 
Dabba
Prepare and deliver lunch meals by bike to 
people working from home.
Consumers pay 55 DKK per meal, and 5,4 DKK 
per kilometer. Dabba only delivers meals within the 
postal code 8000.
Aarhus
05 	
Restaurants
==PAGE:42==
34
tainer design, but is connected to the service provided as well. 
The service can make it easier for consumers to buy takeaway 
from their canteen and thereby provide an easy choice for din-
ner, without having to feel bad about single-use packaging. 
There are significant benefits for the canteens as well, as 
implementing a sustainable PSS will be great for compa-
ny branding. The solution has the potential to increase the 
amount of takeaway sold from canteens and provide them 
with a great way to make a profit on leftovers. The PSS might 
even be a way to reduce expenses for packaging, as there will 
be no need to continuously buy single-use packaging. 
Both the canteen market and the restaurant market provide 
great opportunities for implementing a reusable packaging 
PSS, as investigated in the previous sections. The focus for 
the further design process was therefore planned out in a three 
step roadmap, which is described in the next section. 
As there is not yet any solution helping canteens sell take-
away in reusable containers (Dabba does not collaborate with 
canteens), developing such a solution would create a whole 
new value curve. As a part of a blue ocean strategy tool the 
four actions framework (Chan & Mauborgne, 2005) was used 
to investigate the new value curve. Factors that should be 
reduced, raised, created and eliminated to create value were 
mapped (see Figure 04.4.1). The exercise helped the team get 
an overview of which factors are relevant for a value prop-
osition, as well as certify that creating a reusable packaging 
system for canteens has great potential for being a blue ocean 
strategy. 
A reusable packaging PSS for canteens can provide benefits 
for both consumers (the people who buy takeaway from the 
canteen) and canteens. Creating a reusable container for take-
away makes it possible to optimise the quality of the contain-
er, as it is used several times. The reusable container can be 
tailored to enhance the eating experience of the meal, unlike 
most of the disposable packaging currently available. The val-
ue created for the consumer is not limited to an enhanced con-
Figure 04.3.1 - Display of Kleen Hub’s cups and 
system in Coffee Collective
==PAGE:43==
35
04/ Market Analysis
cluding bringing back the containers. In terms of behavioural 
design, initiating the new PSS in canteens rather than for take-
away restaurants is expected to be a lot easier.  The consum-
er’s ability related to the PSS is higher in the canteen market, 
as consumer will go to the canteen at work most days, which 
will make it easy for them to create a routine of e.g. bringing 
back the containers. This will also save them time and effort, 
and they will not have to think too much about planning. 
Therefore it is seen as a great advantage to start changing the 
mindset of consumers relationship with packaging (and re-
sources in general), in a setting where the ability to partake in 
the PSS is higher. 
Furthermore, it is an advantage to start developing a solution 
for canteens, as a collaboration with Kokkenes Køkken has 
already been established. One of their canteens can therefore 
be selected for developing an MVP and tactically serve as a 
beachhead market (Sull, 2004). Once proof of concept has 
been made, the solution can be rolled out to the rest of Kok-
kenes Køkkens canteens as well as at other canteen providers 
(see Figure 04.5.1). When the solution is mature within the

will require them to follow new steps of the given system, in-

Containers as a service
For this business model consumers do not buy the container 
up front but instead pay a monthly subscription fee in order to 
use the container service. The monthly fee varies between 30-
40 DKK, depending on how many customers are in the sys-
tem, e.g. for the smallest market at Edison Huset the month-
ly fee is 40 DKK.. The expenses are the same as for selling 
containers plus expenses for developing an app to support the 
service and salary for the personnel who have to keep the ser-
vice running smoothly, e.g. distribute the containers between 
canteens or restaurants.
 
Estimates for the size of the takeaway from restaurant market 
and canteen market were conducted. 
 
Takeaway market
15% of Danes between 15 and 74 years old eat takeaway at 
least once a week according to a survey performed by Coop 
Analyse (Coop analyse, 2009). There were 4,353,657 Danes 
in this age group at the end of 2020 (Danmarks Statistik, 
2021), resulting in 653,498 Danes who eat takeaway at least 
once a week.
 
Canteen market
2,303,400 Danes were full time employed in 2019 (Danmarks 
Statistik, Fakta om arbejdsmarkedet, 2021) . 34% of all full 
time employed (or students) are eating lunch at their canteen 
on most days during the workweek (Landbrug og Fødevarer, 
2016), which amounts to 783 165 Danes.
 
Kokkenes Køkken manage canteens equal to 1% of the can-
teen market according to CEO of KK Maj Toppenberg, so ap-
canteen market, the solution can be expanded to the restaurant 
takeaway market.
04.6 Market Size and Feasibility
Back of the envelope calculations were conducted in order 
to investigate the market potential of the canteens from Kok-
kenes Køkken, the canteen market in general and the restau-
rant takeaway market. All calculations can be found in Ap-
pendix A04.9 and A04.10.  
 
It should be noted that the following calculations are focused 
on the revenue and expenses for the business providing the 
PSS (e.g. if the master group decided to create a start-up). It 
was assumed at this point that this company will sell direct-
ly to the consumer buying takeaway (from either canteens or 
restaurants).
 
A lot of factors were unknown at this point, including the 
business model for the reusable container system. Two simple 
business models were therefore used for the calculations:
 
Selling containers
This business model is based on consumers buying the con-
tainers. The revenue for this model is based on the number 
of containers sold minus the production cost and the cost of 
maintaining a website as a selling platform. The price of the 
container is dependent on how many containers are produced 
(as fixed costs such as paying for an injection mould are dis-
tributed between more containers). The price ranges from 100 
to 150 DKK. It is assumed that consumers buy one container 
each within the first year. After the first year 5% buy another 
container each year (as they have lost the container/need an-
other one, etc.)

nificantly higher revenue by using a subscription model. The 
potential canteen market is, surprisingly, a bit bigger than the 
restaurant market. It should be noted that the calculations on 
market size are rough estimates. Even if the canteen market 
is bigger it is still desirable to reach the restaurant market as 
well.
 
In conclusion both the restaurant and canteen market are big 
enough to make a feasible business case for developing a re-
usable container PSS for takeaway. The market analysis can 
now be summarized. 
04.7 Summary
This chapter has explored the business perspective of devel-
oping a PSS system. Firstly, relevant markets within the take-
away food industry have been explored in order to identify the 
ones most suitable for developing a reusable packaging solu-
tion. Secondly, key competitors have been identified within 
these markets in order to determine the competitiveness of 
the market and develop a blue ocean strategy. This analysis 
formed the basis for a plan on how to enter the market through 
a three-step plan. Lastly, back of the envelope calculations 
have been conducted in order to determine the potential and 
feasibility of entering the targeted markets.
Creating a solution for the takeaway from the restaurant mar-
ket would undoubtedly have a big impact, but it is also a very 
complex system with a lot of different actors. The system is 
open ended, as the customer might never come back to the 
same restaurant. The takeaway from the canteen market is 
more overlooked but could potentially increase its relevance 
over time. The canteen system has the advantage of fewer ac-
tors and the fact that employees come back to the same place 
again and again. The main focus was therefore chosen to be 
the canteen market, which later on can be expanded to the 
restaurant takeaway industry. 
 
The most direct competitor within the takeaway from restau-
rant market in Denmark is Kleen Hub, who have already 
launched their reusable containers for takeaway. Competitors 
abroad such as reCIRCLE have reached further maturity with 
a substantial network of restaurants. On the canteen market 
the company Dabba is a key competitor, as they are selling 
lunch options for employees working from home in reusable 
containers.
Back of the envelope calculations show that there is a mar-
proximately 7,831 people frequent the canteens most week-
days.
 
In the following calculation it is assumed that 10% of the mar-
ket are willing to buy the reusable containers or subscribe to 
the container service.
 
Very rough calculations of the potential revenue of each mar-
ket were now conducted. 
 
The calculations show (unsurprisingly) that the bigger the 
market, the bigger the revenue. It might not be feasible in 
the short term to develop a solution only for customers fre-
quenting the canteens belonging to KK, as the cost will be 
bigger than the earnings from subscription payments. In the 
case of a limited customer base it will be wiser to sell contain-
ers instead, as the income up front will make it a sustainable 
business within the first year. The subscription model does, 
however, provide a much better business model for making a 
Calculations
The calculations represented here are very rough repre-
sentations of what the cost structure might look like for 
two different business models. All expenses are based 
on estimates. The production price for each container 
has been determined with guidance from Maj Toppen-
berg.
 
The two business models should be seen as very min-
imal concepts, which illustrates two ends of the busi-
ness model spectrum for the PSS – From a product 
oriented to a result oriented focus. Representing the 
two business models in the calculations provided an 
understanding on how selling containers and selling a 
subscription affect revenue on the short and long term.
The takeaway market was expected to be bigger than 
the potential for the canteen market, but the market 
size estimates show that the canteens might have more 
potential customers. How much takeaway is sold from 
canteens currently is not factored into the calculations, 
as one of the main goals for the PSS system is to pro-
mote and facilitate selling takeaways from canteens. It 
is therefore deemed more relevant how many consum-
ers are regularly using their canteens, as they are the 
potential customers. 
==PAGE:46==
38
ket potential for both the restaurant and canteen market. The 
estimated potential market is bigger for canteens than for the 
restaurant market, but both markets can potentially include 
more than half a million consumers. It would be beneficial to 
go from a product-oriented focus, where containers are sold 
directly to consumers, to a more use- or result-oriented focus 
where consumers pay a subscription to ensure a recurring rev-
enue flow. However, selling containers as a business model 
will be more feasible for a startup as a starting point.  
 
With the conclusion of the market analysis the rest of the proj-
ect can be scoped, which will be outlined in the following 
chapter.
