PROJECT 
SCOPE
==PAGE:50==
42
Based on the initial literature study and the market analysis 
the scope of this project is defined as: 
This project will explore how to replace single-use pack-
aging with a reusable product-service system. The project 
will first of all focus on developing a solution for the 
canteens of Kokkenes Køkken, while the long-term vision 
is to expand to the rest of the canteen market and later on 
the takeaway market for restaurants. 
The product-service system will include a reusable container 
and a system to support the use of the containers. 
The development arena of this project is mapped to get an 
overview of all relevant actors (see Figure 05.1). The three 
markets included in the three step roadmap are mapped as; 
Kokkenes Køkken (red circle), canteens (green circle) and 
takeaway restaurants (blue circle). The project scope (red 
dashed line) revolves around KK and the canteens in gen-
eral, where only the consumers of takeaway restaurants are 
included as actors within scope (as these are users that have 
valuable experience with takeaway). Furthermore, Danish 
legislation, food authorities and the assessment “Verdensmål i 
Værdikæden” are included in the project scope (view Appen-
dix A04.03 for further description of these actors). 
The main users of the scope that the further study will focus on 
are the canteens (also referred to as partners), their employees 
and their customers, also referred to as the consumers.
KK has a great interest in transitioning their canteens to a new 
product-service system both to mitigate the new legislation 
as well as improving their sustainable CSR profile. Exploring 
and analysing user behaviour will therefore mainly be focused 
around the consumers of the system, as it is expected chang-
ing behaviour for these users will be the greatest challenge.  
When assessing the final PSS solution in terms of environ-
mental factors, the scope for the product-service system is 
categorised as a PSS consequences scope, as the PSS solution 
will replace a current solution that is not a PSS. Thus, the pur-
pose of the environmental assessment will be a system-level 
assessment with the baseline situation without PSS as an ex-
ternal reference system (see Figure 02.3.2).

06.1 Introduction
Exploring the interactions, experiences and needs of potential 
users of a reusable packaging system is vital in order to later 
on develop a concept that truly fits the context. Therefore an 
exploration of the current context is needed in order to uncov-
er the context of a takeaway system from the perspective of 
both the canteen and the consumers. 
Firstly, the ecosystem illustrating the canteen industry is 
developed in collaboration with the CEO of KK in order to 
properly understand the flow of resources, information and 
products between the involved actors.  
The explorative study is then divided into two paths, each in-
vestigating one type of user group. 
The first type was the canteen personnel at Kokkenes Køkken, 
more specifically the personnel working in the canteen at the 
office building, Edison Huset. The work processes of the staff, 
the physical surroundings and existing takeaway solutions are 
documented using the contextual design approach described 
by Beyer & Holtzblatt (1998). 
 
The other type of user is the end consumer, who purchases 
the takeaway meal. Their experiences are documented using 
workbooks inspired by the generative design method de-
scribed by Sanders & Stappers (2012). Later on several gen-
erative workshops are conducted in order to explore wants, 
needs and behaviours in a new reusable packaging system. 
Insights from both paths are all gathered in affinity diagrams 
(one for the system and one for the container), to structure 
all insights. The affinity diagrams are used as a basis for the 
design specification. 
In parallel to the explorative user studies, an MVP test was 
developed and launched at Edison huset, as an element to 
measure customer response of a simple PSS, with the focus of 
adjusting the final solution accordingly.
06.2 Ecosystem 
An ecosystem was mapped in collaboration with the CEO of 
Kokkenes Køkken, with the purpose of gaining an overview 
of the different stakeholder interactions in the canteen and 
restaurant industry. Figure 06.2.1 (on next page) shows the 
ecosystem map with a primary focus on the canteen industry. 
The flows and transcriptions mapped between the different 
stakeholders are; money  (green line), information (dotted

06.3 Fieldwork at Edison Huset
Fieldwork at Edison Huset was conducted primarily using the 
contextual design method described by Beyer & Holtzblatt 
(1998) (see section 02.5 for an overview of the contextual 
design approach to participatory design). The fieldwork was 
conducted by observing and applying the master apprentice 
method to understand the breakdowns of the current work 
system. Afterwards, the five work models, described by Beyer 
& Holtzblatt, were used to gain a more in depth understanding 
of the sources to the breakdowns and issues in the system. 
Three different symbols are used in the world models to rep-
resent: lighting bolt (breakdowns), star (opportunities) and an 
exclamation point (things to be aware of). Company 1 and 2 
are companies that have their offices in Edison Huset. Finally, 
a swimlane diagram provided more in depth knowledge of the 
processes in ordering a takeaway meal in the current system, 
also including the related breakdowns.
Figure 06.2 shows an overview of the canteen staff at Edi-
son Huset. The site manager and head chef Teddy has been 
the primary contact within Edison Huset, while the other em-
ployees have contributed with insights related to their specific 
work role. 
A lot of insights has been gathered during the master/appren-
tice observations. Pictures from the fieldwork can be found in 
Appendix A06.02. The insights have been used to inform the 
five workmodels and the swimlane diagram. However, some 
general observations which do not inform a specific work-
model are relevant to note (see next pages). 
The swimlane model depicts the current takeaway process
Ecosystem
	
●
The canteen companies do not usually own any 
of the facilities that they use. They either rent or 
have a common agreement with the company or 
real estate owner.
	
●
The company/facility owner has the final say in 
the kind of crockery set and packages used (usu-
ally with a focus on price)
	
●
The lunch agreement is normally paid by the 
consumer via the company/employer. Extra food 
or beverages as well as takeaway is paid by the 
consumer directly to the canteen
	
●
Waste handling is managed by Daka and it is the 
company/facility owners responsibility to handle 
this. 
	
●
All accounting goes through KK
	
●
Canteens having an operational agreement rather 
than a management agreement have a higher 
incentive to sell takeaway meals
	
●
Currently different canteens within KK use 
several different platforms for ordering takeaway, 
these include: CanteenPro and Go Appified (Ap-
pendix A06.01)
	
●
Canteens often sell convenience meals 
	
●
Chefs are usually not tech savvy people. The 
platforms should be as user friendly as possible 
with a steep learning curve.

TAKEAWAY RESTAURANTS
==PAGE:58==
50
Entrance
There are several entrances to the canteen in Edison 
Huset, each with a screen for “checking in”. The can-
teen receives payment from the different companies 
within Edison Huset based on how many of their em-
ployees have checked in. 
As the check in counters have to be passed by all em-
ployees who are using the canteen it is a great location 
for promoting takeaway from the canteen. Employees 
are currently not exposed to any information about 
takeaway, as there are no signs or information about 
this option anywhere in the canteen.
Serving Area 
The canteen concept is based on SANS! (see Section 
01.2), which is why there is no buffeet but instead in-
dividually served dishes. The canteen environment can 
be chaotic around lunch time, which makes it difficult 
to promote new concepts such as takeaway. 
Single-use Packaging
Around 1000 single-use packages are stored within the 
canteen kitchen. The rest of the container storage is in 
the basement. There is not that much storage space and 
they do not have a specific procedure of handling boxes 
and containers. 
==PAGE:59==
51
06/ Explorative User Studies
Return Stations
Return stations for used plates and utensils can be 
found in two locations at the canteen. 
Dishes are gathered continuously during the lunch break 
and early in the morning on trays and put through the 
industrial washing machine, which runs programmes at 
85-90 degrees celsius.
Café
The cafe is currently the only place to collect takeaway 
meals. 
The capacity of the current takeaway setup is limited, 
as takeaway meals are stored in a small refrigerator. 
Pia and Stin work in the cafe in shifts from 7am till 3 
pm and 9am till 5 pm. The takeaway meals can, howev-
er, be picked up after 5 pm as the fridge is always open 
(trust based system). 
Convenience Meals
The canteen is trying to sell convenience food in glass 
containers, which do not need to be returned. The 
meals cost around 100 DKK. Consumers often return 
the glass containers anyway because they do not have 
space at home and do not think that they should go to 
waste. 
The glass containers can be fragile and break in the 
dishwasher or freezer.
==PAGE:60==

Cultural Model
There are several interesting relationships between 
stakeholder groups within the canteen arena. Each in-
dividual canteen has to take into account the overall 
agendas from Kokkenes Køkken as well as dealing with 
the companies they provide a service for. The cultural 
model shown here is based on interviews with several 
members of the canteen staff and therefore reflects their 
point of view and how they perceive the company cul-
ture within Edison Huset. 
Breakdowns
Many of the companies including Company 1 and 2 
expect the cafe staff and cleaners to go around and 
collect used utilities (cutlery, plates etc.) from the 
canteen. The employees are furthermore too lazy to 
put dishes on the cleaning racks in their own office 
kitchens. This might as well become a problem when 
returning used containers for a takeaway solution if 
the system boundaries are not clearly represented.

Artefact Model
Several types of packaging currently used for meals at 
Edison Huset are examined in this artefact model. The 
types of packaging seen here are used for meals that 
are not eaten in the canteen area, but somewhere else 
in the building e.g. in a meeting room due to a work-
shop. The head chef Teddy demonstrated the different 
types of packaging and recalled the feedback from the 
consumers. 
 
Breakdowns
The canteen personnel tried to serve the salads in flat 
cardboard containers. However, the consumers did 
not like the appearance of the salad in the containers, 
as it looked flat, boring and not fresh. Furthermore, 
the containers gave some consumers unflattering as-
sociations to nursing home food.

 Opportunities
The employees working at Edison Huset respond-
ed well to snacks served in reusable packaging at 
meetings. The impression of a sustainable packaging 
choice is a value in itself.  
Consumers find it appealing when the food is visible 
through the packaging. This was especially seen with 
the salads, as they looked much more appealing in 
the see-through cylindrical containers.
Some types of meals require specialized packaging 
(such as sandwiches, which are most easily served in 
plastic/paper bags).
The type of packaging used has a big effect on how 
the meal is perceived. Changing the packaging can 
make a dish much more flattering and popular.

Sequence Model
The sequence model depicts the current challenges of 
communicating the lunch and takeaway menu to the 
consumers. Communicating the menu to consumers 
is an important requisite for selling more takeaway, as 
most consumers want to know which dish they are re-
serving takeaway for. The process for sharing the menu 
is based on information from the head chef, who is the 
main responsible for creating and communicating out 
the menu.
Breakdowns
Sharing the menu on many different platforms is a lot 
of work, as it is currently not possible to share across 
all platforms at the same time. 
The menu is written on a black board in the canteen, 
but it is the only daily dish that is displayed there. 
Consumers will therefore not be able to rely on this 
information for takeaway, as it has to be ordered a 
day in advance before 12 o’clock.

==PAGE:72==
64
Together with KK it was decided to order a total of 60 con-
tainers to start with, where all takeaway meals would be 
served in a reusable container (not giving the consumers the 
choice to choose between single-use and reusable containers). 
A new email was created for the consumers to order from: 
takeaway-edison@kokkeneskoekken.dk, latest the day before 
at noon. The orders would be placed for collection in the café/
reception and should be returned at the cleaning stations in 
the canteen. 
To keep track of the containers borrowed and returned a QR 
code linking to a survey was placed at both the place of col-
lection and return (see Appendix A06.03). In the “borrow” 
survey customers were asked to write their email (the same 
as they used for ordering) and state how many containers 
they were borrowing. In the “return” survey customers were 
asked the same questions as well as how their experience was 
including a followup question (see Appendix A06.04 and 
A06.05). The emails of the customers using the system were 
later used for more qualitative interviews. 
Choosing the MVP container
To choose the best suited container for the MVP test, a test 
with seven different containers was made in Edison Huset 
(see Appendix A06.06). Participants were asked to vote for 
the container they would prefer to transport food in, eat from 
as well as which one they overall liked the most taking all 
aspects into consideration (see Figure 06.4.2).
30 customers came by the stand to give their vote. Scores can 
be found in Appendix A06.07. 
06.4 MVP Test
A minimal viable product (MVP) was developed in parallel 
to the explorative user study to gain a quick customer re-
sponse on the overall concept of selling takeaway meals in 
a reusable container system. The MVP test was conducted 
at Edison Huset as the first step in the 3-step roadmap (see 
Figure 04.5.1), with the aim of making a proof of concept at 
Kokkenes Køkken. This step is broken down into two steps; 
step 0, where the proof of concept is to be made via the MVP 
launched at Edison huset, whereafter step 1 is focused on ex-
panding the system to the rest of KK’s canteens (see Figure 
06.4.1). 
Furthermore, the purpose was to gain feedback on the solu-
tion (both the container and the system), gain insights on con-
sumers’ behaviour patterns and statistics as well as exploring 
the unknown unknowns.
The MVP Scope
The scope of the MVP test was based on the current ability 
of the master group and the canteen at Edison Huset. The aim 
was therefore to develop and implement a solution as simple 
as possible. It was decided together with Kokkenes Køkken 
to only initiate the test at Edison Huset, to avoid unnecessary 
complexity. Advertisement of the takeaway service and the 
reusable container system was launched on the 12th of April 
2021, as selling takeaway meals has not been an active ser-
vice provided by the canteen at Edison Huset. The idea was to 
initiate the test and collect ongoing feedback and implement 
changes accordingly, as described in the 5. principle in Lean 
Startup methodology (build, measure, learn) (see Section 
0.3.2). Therefore, the end date was not determined.

based on the 7 options
Furthermore the canteen staff were to choose their preferred 
type of container. Different arguments for choosing a round 
container over a rectangular were discussed, including that a 
round container would be more appropriate to serve food in 
and more familiar to eat from (as it resembles a round plate), 
whereas a rectangular container would be better for storing 
both in bags under transportation as well as in the fridge or 
shelves. In the end it was decided to buy two types of contain-
ers for the MVP test, a round and a rectangular one. By doing 
so, it was possible to easily perform an A/B test to investi-
gate the most preferred shape by the users (consumers and 
canteen). The two different shaped containers from the brand 
Frigoverre can be seen in Figure 06.4.4.. 
30 of each container was bought, and the consumers were giv-
en the free choice between the two shapes, testing the most 
popular shape based on the ones that were borrowed first. 
Furthermore, the kitchen staff also got to test which one they 
Results showed that most people do not necessarily prefer the 
same container for transporting as they want their food served 
in, as each context requires different functionalities. The most 
preferred container was the standard IKEA glass container 
with a plastic lid (see Figure 06.4.3). Arguments for voting on 
this container were based on its appropriate size (not too big 
nor too small), the fact that leftover food can be microwaved 
directly from the container and due to the familiar snap clos-
ing mechanism sealed with rubber strips that makes it seem 
secure and leakage proof.

==PAGE:74==
66
erative design approaches as described by Sanders & Stappers 
(2012) were used to uncover existing behavioural tendencies 
and experiences with takeaway and packaging to visions for 
future solutions (see section 02.5). Note that the focus for the 
generative user studies was the takeaway market in general 
and not the canteens market specifically, as most people have 
experiences related to takeaway (from restaurants), while sig-
nificantly fewer have tried to order takeaway from their work-
place canteen. 
Workbooks
Within generative design a key part of the process is sensitiz-
ing participants to think about their use patterns and experi-
ences before engaging in e.g. a workshop. Sensitizing is often 
done by asking participants to document their own experienc-
es on the topic of the study. It is custom in this type of material 
to make the focus a bit broader than the specific design con-
text (Sanders & Stappers, 2012). An example could be that 
sensitizing material for developing a new shaver would focus 
on morning hygiene practices. The sensitizing material devel-
oped for this project is therefore focused on experiences with 
takeaway and more specifically packaging for takeaway, but 
not necessarily in the context of buying a takeaway meal from 
a canteen. Choosing this broader focus makes it much more 
likely to find participants who can document their experience 
and makes sense in the broader scope of this project, as expe-
riences with takeaway in general will aid in the development 
of a product-service system which can be scaled up to include 
the restaurant market. 
Workbook content
A workbook was developed, which prompts the participants 
to describe their takeaway experience through choices, pic-
tures and open-ended questions. The workbook follows the 
guidelines for sensitizing material provided by Sanders & 
Stappers (2012), as it encourages self-documentation of facts, 
behaviours and feelings about takeaway and the participants 
were able to fill them out in their own time (they had a week 
prefer the most in relation to placing the food, storage, etc.   
Development of Advertising Material
The most necessary material for the MVP test to be developed 
was: the QR code posters - both for borrowing and returning, 
a pamphlet describing how to use the system, as well as a 
poster for general advertisement of takeaway, see Appendix 
A06.03 and A06.08.  See also the placement of the advertising 
material in the canteens in Appendix A06.09. 
To make the MVP test resemble a real commercial solution, 
a “brand” was developed. The master thesis group settled on 
a name “Rutine”, where a logo for the reusable container sys-
tem was developed based on a quick individual brainstorm in 
the group. Three different design logo versions were exposed 
to the group’s Instagram followers, who voted for the best 
logo, see Appendix A06.10. The winner logo was selected and 
made in Adobe illustrator as a vector file, see Figure 06.4.5. 
The “u” in “Rutine” was changed to an “O” due to the design, 
making the final brand name ROTINE.
Figure 06.4.5 - the chosen logo
06.5 Generative Design Ap-
proach 
The fieldwork conducted in the canteen at Edison Huset pro-
vided great insights about the current work systems in the ex-
isting canteens. The thoughts, feelings, behaviours and expe-
riences of the potential end consumer of a reusable packaging 
system was, however, still largely unexplored. Therefore gen-
Figure 06.4.4 - The two chosen containers 
==PAGE:75==
67
06/ Explorative User Studies
cial bubble. Therefore, the outcome of the workshop is also 
characterised by the type of participants - all three are in their 
mid-twenties, with backgrounds in design, engineering, busi-
ness and entrepreneurship.  
Even though the participants all knew each other, the first 
part of the workshop focused on creating comfort; the work-
shop was opened by the facilitator, stating the purpose and 
the agenda (see Appendix A06.15), then all participants short-
ly introduced themselves and stated their favorite takeaway, 
whereafter highlights from the participants individual work-
books were briefly discussed (see Appendix 06.16). 
The next part of the workshop focused on awakening a cre-
ative mindset, and breaking down any constraints in terms of 
drawing and brainstorming, this was done by a 1 minute exer-
cise where the participants had to draw a helicopter followed 
to do it) and in the comfort of their own home.
The inspiration for the content of the workbook came from 
other sources of self-reflection and documentation, such as 
travel diaries (one group member had an impressive collec-
tion), scrap books and friend books (most commonly used for 
documenting friendships and interests in childhood). All of 
these have a creative outlet in common, where the writers are 
able to express themselves. The creative expression is encour-
aged in the takeaway workbook by prompting participants to 
take pictures of their experience and express themselves using 
hashtags and emojis.
The workbook consists of 8 pages; Introduction, Tips and 
Tricks, Takeaway it is, The Arrival, Dinner is Ready, The Af-
termath, Now it is all Over and Thank You, guiding the partic-
ipants to describe their journey from the decision making to 
the cleaning in the end. An overview of all pages is shown on 
the next pages (see also Appendix A06.11 to A06.13). 
As the purpose of the workbooks also was to explore users 
behaviour in relation to takeaway and takeaway packaging, 
questions related to the three factors affecting user behaviour; 
triggers, abilities and motivation were incorporated (these 
factors are described in Section 02.4 Behavioural Design). 
Examples are listed below: 
•	
Triggers: “I decided to order takeaway when ...”
•	
Motivation and/or ability: “I’m having takeaway today 
because I’m …” or “What I considered before choosing 
my meal ...” or “I ate from … (my own plate/bowl or the 
packaging) because I …”
However, note that the answers to the stated questions will be 
the users own reflection upon the factors, making the answers 
their conscious perception of their behaviour.
Physical workshop 
After the participants had filled out the workbooks, ss in-
structed by mail (see Appendix 06.14), a physical workshop, 
prior to an online workshop, was held with the focus of gain-
ing insights from the conversations between the participants, 
while doing fun design exercises regarding the development 
of both a container and reuse system. The physical workshop 
was hosted by two of the group members (Astrid and Signe), 
which took turns in facilitating. Due to the covid-19 situation, 
only three participants were invited (the workshop took place 
in a conservatory with the capacity of 5 people), where all 
three were already a part of one of the group members’ so-
The Miro Platform
The workbook material has been developed in the Miro, 
an online visual collaboration tool that has been very 
valuable throughout the entire design process. Miro 
allows collaborators to work on a huge virtual white-
board with familiar elements such as post-its, different 
shapes, arrows, emojis and more. After discussing pros 
and cons it was decided that the participants should fill 
in the workbook directly in Miro. The expectation was 
that this might be difficult for some of the participants, 
as most of them were not familiar with using the online 
tool. To combat any difficulties an email with thorough 
instructions was composed (see Appendix A06.14) and 
the participants were told that they were welcome to 
call if any difficulties should come up. Some of the par-
ticipants in the 60+ age group were a bit hesitant to 
try out the platform. An alternative was provided in the 
form of a PDF version of the workbook, which could be 
printed and manually filled in or edited in other (more 
familiar) programs such as Powerpoint. All participants 
were, however, able to fill out the workbook in Miro 
in the end (with varying degrees of guidance). As the 
workbooks were digitally filled in, the material was 
ready to be processed afterwards. The workbook was 
tested both by the two other group members as well 
as a couple of other users before sending it out to all 
participants.

Online workshop
The physical workshop focused mainly on the container de-
sign (due to the slipped time schedule), which was ideal for 
making fun, concrete design exercises that fitted well with-
in a physical workshop format. A lot of good insights were 
achieved during the physical workshop exercises, however it 
was agreed that the online workshop should mainly focus on 
the system around the container, to gain more user insights 
concerning this aspect. 
Based on the learnings and the shift of focus, a new agenda for 
the online workshop was developed (see Appendix A06.20). 
Furthermore, the strengths and weaknesses of an online for-
mat were carefully considered when developing the content 
and structure. A new adaptation of the generative workshop 
by a 5 minute individual negative brainstorm of a reusable 
container (see Appendix 06.17). The participants were all 
brainstorming eagerly, resulting in fun and interesting nega-
tive ideas, e.g. a container made from endangered species or 
a container made from styrofoam (as one of the participants 
hate the sensory feeling of styrofoam). The participants were 
afterwards asked to co-create a “perfect reusable container” 
solution, by first doing individual (positive) brainstorms (see 
Appendix 06.17), which they used as a basis for discussion, 
where they agreed on their three most important specifica-
tions; be pretty, be eaten from and preserve temperature. 
These specifications were used as concepts in a morphology 
exercise. As the time schedule slipped, a few moderations to 
the agenda had to be made - and the participants only got to 
draw lines for the first concept, the pretty container, via the 
morphology. This concept was sketched (see Figure 06.5.2). 
 
Figure 06.5.2 - Drawing of the “pretty container” concept, made via 
morphology
The next part involved designing the system around the con-
tainer that they had designed. Due to lack of time, parts of 
the agenda were skipped - e.g. the exercise concerning be-
havioural elements, and individual brainstorms. The partici-
pants were asked to discuss elements important to the system. 
They were gradually given more props to help them in their 
concept development - including guidance to stages of the 
system relevant for the scope, as well as post-its with draw-
ings of humane and inhumane actors, to prime them to in-
clude relevant elements. The participants were then asked to 
“detail” their concept of the system, by illustrating it using 
pen and paper, post-its etc. Finally a 2 minute pitch was per-
formed by the participants, selling their full concept including 
the container and the system around (see Appendix 06.17). 
Despite sitting by a rocky table in a cold conservatory, the 
workshop was a fun and successful session, which created

●
Sustainability aspects are important, as it is one 
of the main drivers for participating in a reus-
able takeaway container system. (see Appendix 
A06.18)
	
●
One of the participants did not appreciate eating 
from plastic containers. Another pointed out that 
a lot of plastic in the packaging made the meal 
look less attractive (see Appendix A06.16)

Practical aspects of the container are important, 
such as the container being lightweight, seal

an important functionality when eating straight

in an appealing way. The different elements of 
the meal should be separated (see Appendix 
A06.17)

which often means balancing the container on the 
lap (see Appendix A06.17).

The container should stand out from “a boring 
IKEA container” (see Appendix A06.19)

approach as described by Sanders & Stappers (2012) had to 
be applied for the online format in order to make sure that 
the participants were creatively stimulated by the exercises 
and felt comfortable sharing their thoughts and ideas with the 
rest of the group even if they had only connected with them 
online. 
The online workshop was hosted via Microsoft Teams, with 
a total of 8 participants (see Figure 06.5.3 and 06.5.4). These 
were divided into two online “rooms”, having a facilitator in 
each. Both rooms were mixed gender and included at least 1 
participant that was 60+ years old and 3 participants in their 
mid-twenties. On each team at least one Design and Innova-
tion student was present (as they undeniably are some of our 
best friends, who are always engaged and eager to help). Both 
rooms got access to their own Miro board, where specific con-
tent had been prepared (see Appendix A06.21). 
The same basic structure of creating comfort and awakening 
the creativity within the rooms before starting to develop was 
used in the online workshops. The participants briefly got to 
touch upon the container design, by first doing an individual 
brainstorm, and then deciding on the most important func-
tionalities (these insights were used directly in the affini-
ty diagram later on). Afterwards, the facilitator presented a 
basic outline/premise for the system to help scope the solu-
tion space, thus staging the relevant discussions (see Figure 
06.5.5). The specific outcomes for both rooms can be found 
in Appendix A06.22 and A06.23. 
Hereafter, two rounds of specific questions, regarding e.g. 
stakeholders, motivation, delivery and return, were made for 
the groups to discuss. Based on these discussions the partici-
pants had to explain their system, using all the prompts they 
wanted to in Miro. 
Even though the two rooms had the same agenda, the out-
comes were very different. Room 1 followed the design agen-
da quite closely, while room 2 had many heavy discussions 
on what was actually the most convenient (e.g. to get the con-
tainer picked up by someone or to deliver it at some kind of 
station). The discussions were characterised by the different 
lifestyles - young people living in the city vs. middle aged 
living in a house in the suburbs. Especially because of the dif-
ferences in the sessions, many different insights were gained 
from both rooms. 
The physical and online workshops provided great insights 
into how the participants interact with packaging in their daily 
Workshop
Time aspect
	
●
The agenda was too optimistic - there were simply 
too many exercises in relation to the time given.
	
●
A good non-rushed brief of exercises as well as 
small talk between sessions are important to create 
a friendly and comfortable vibe (this took more 
time than expected).
	
●
Participants need time to explain their thoughts 
after e.g. brainstorming sessions.
Gaining the right insights
	
●
Especially one of the participants (the engineer) 
was quite constrained by what is physically doable 
and financially feasible when ideating. The pur-
pose of the workshop was not to make a final solu-
tion, but rather to reveal reflection and priorities. 
It was therefore important as facilitators to keep 
stressing that the participants should imagine that 
everything is possible, and focus on how the ideal 
solution would be. 
	
●
It became clear that getting the participants to 
brainstorm over “closing mechanisms’’ for the 
morphology exercise, did not bring much value, as 
the focus became too technical. The focus should 
mainly be on creating insights of the functional-
ities important for the participants, as users, and 
not getting them to solve engineering issues. 
	
●
Finding the right balance between leading the 
participants in the right direction and not limiting 
their creativity is important. When discussing 
the system the participants could have benefited 
from a bit more scoping, as they started to discuss 
cradle-to-cradle steps of the container - including 
where and how it should be produced. 
Using the right language
	
●
As all participants were Danish speaking it was 
not natural to make them ideate and converse in 
English - therefore the content for the next online 
workshop was developed in Danish, as all partici-
pants here were Danish as well.

Insights to the Participants Behaviour with Packaging
The workbook made the participants reflect upon their ex-
periences with takeaway, explaining why, how and when 
they performed certain behaviours. Based on the 19 filled 
out workbooks, motivations, abilities and triggers could be 
derived. The participants mentioned that the most common 
reasons for ordering takeaway was that they were craving 
takeaway food, or that it was more convenient to order a meal 
than make one, as they were either tired or knew they would 
come home late. What specifically triggered the behaviour is 
therefore not entirely clear, as it might have been a signal, 
such as a commercial post on Instagram that unconsciously 
triggered the final decision to buy takeaway. Some, howev-
er, mentioned that they only ordered takeaway as the proj-
ect group had asked them to, and being given this task might 
therefore be the biggest trigger of all. Gaining insights on the 
motivation and ability influencing the participants behaviour 
was in contrast a bit easier:
•	
Most participants (12 out of 19) were motivated to buy 
takeaway by the pleasure of treating themselves with a 
nice meal as well as the pleasure of satisfying a certain 
craving
•	
12 out of 19 participants chose to order takeaway either 
lives, what they value in a reusable container and how they 
would like a return system to look like. The workbooks that 
all participants had filled out beforehand worked great as a 
sensitising activity, as the participants had their previous ex-
periences with takeaway fresh in mind. The filled in work-
books did, however, have a lot of value as stand alone docu-
ments, as they provided a detailed view into 19 participants’ 
thoughts, feelings and habits related to takeaway. Sanders & 
Stappers (2012) state that sensitizing material such as work-
books can be a great source of insights and can be included in 
the analysis of data. It was therefore decided to take a closer

in how the meal is perceived, including the factors of interact-
ing with the packaging.
It is relevant to note that the types of food associated with the 
fast and convenient expectation often have highly specialised 
packaging, which is optimised for one particular type of food 
(such as pizza boxes, burger boxes, sandwich wrap, etc.). This 
kind of highly optimised single-use packaging would be dif-
ficult to adapt to a reuse system. The eating patterns from the 
fast and convenient expectation are interesting, as they reflect 
a relaxed everyday interaction with the meal. Takeaway meals 
from the canteen could easily fit into this setting as they have 
the same kind of ground settings. 
The type of food served in the canteens of Kokkenes Køkken 
is more similar to both of the settings in the experience expec-
tations, with a combination of hot dishes with a carbohydrate 
side and meals with a lot of smaller elements. The experience 
expectation is more in line with how Kokkenes Køkken want 
their meals to be perceived, as seen in the SANS! concept. 
The head chef Teddy stated that he wants the customers to 
experience the takeaway as exclusive. The nicely presented 
individual meal is interesting in this context, as the packaging 
is perceived as having value for the eating experience and is 
put directly on the Table. The ideal for sharing setting is in-
teresting, as this is probably how a lot of families eat when 
buying takeaway. 
In conclusion the reusable takeaway container should provide 
excitement and a wow factor as seen in the nicely present-
ed meal setting. The container has to be presentable enough 
for taking center stage at the Table when sharing a meal with 
friends and family, but at the same time support a dinner eat-
en on the couch after a long workday. Therefore, the further 
focus has been scoped to only include the experience expecta-
tions (see Figure 06.6.1).
due to convenience or being in a tired/lazy mood. This 
implies that the takeaway solution requires less ability 
than cooking themselves, in terms of time and physical 
effort. 
•	
Half of the participants (10 out of 19) ate directly from 
the packaging, as this was more convenient, which im-
plies that the physical effort it takes to transfer the food to 
a plate and clean it afterwards makes it less simple than 
just eating from the packaging. 
•	
11 out of 19 participants threw the packaging out in gen-
eral trash, where few stated that they had considerations 
(mostly in relation to whether it could have been recy-
cled). One participant wrote: “No. I just wanted to get 
rid of it so I could lay down”. This behaviour suggests 
that the single-use packaging requires very low ability in 
terms of physical efforts. 
Furthermore, many of the participants stated that they had 
never given much thought into the packaging before filling 
out the workbook (the workbook also asked the participants 
to thoroughly describe the packaging as well as how they han-
dled it afterwards). This empathises how great a challenge it 
is to change people’s mindset about packaging, as the current 
single-use packages are merely perceived as a convenient way 
of storing and transporting food. Interactions with the con-
tainer after it has fulfilled its purpose are limited and mainly 
unconscious, as it cannot provide any more positive impact.
06.6 Workbook Segmentation
Most of the data from all the filled in workbooks (see Appen-
dix B06.01) were transferred to an Excel sheet (see Appen-
dix B06.02), making it possible to sort the entries based on 
different criteria and look for patterns. Looking through all 
workbook material provided a good overview and an under-
standing of the elements that distinguish the experiences. The 
distinguishing factors were mainly based on priorities when 
ordering takeaway, how formal the setting was, which kind 
of meal it was and what the eating situation looked like. Two 
different expectations for the takeaway experience were iden-
tified – the fast and convenient vs. the experience. These will 
be presented in the next three spreads. 
Please look at all three spreads on the following pages before 
continue reading.
As seen from the segmentation of the workbooks the take-
away experience cannot be defined as just one thing. The ex-
pectations of the meal and the setting makes a big difference

The fast and convenient
No of workbooks 6/19 (4,5,6,9,11,14).
Considerations Price, specific craving.
Types of food Pizza, Burger, Sandwich 
This expectation is held by participants who just want some-
thing quick and tasty to eat. The takeaway is not used to mark 
a special occasion but is rather fitting into a busy lifestyle as a 
convenient choice. This expectation is common when eating 
alone, but can also occur when eating with a partner, especial-
ly if ordering takeaway is part of a regular routine. An afford-
able price is an important factor when choosing this type of 
takeaway as well as a specific craving. It is assumed that the 
users have a local restaurant that they rely on when wanting 
to meet this expectation.
The fast and convenient takeaway is eaten directly from the 
packaging (which is often some kind of paper wrap or card-
board box). It is common for this type of meal to be eaten on 
the sofa or even in the bed. There are typically no leftovers 
from the meal. All of the packaging is thrown out afterwards.
 
Six out of 19 workbooks belonged in this category, which has 
been sorted by filtering for ate from packaging and no cutlery 
or chopsticks (see Appendix B06.02). All of them are living in 
Copenhagen and are in their mid-twenties.

The nicely presented 
individual meal
No of workbooks 7/19 (1,4,6,13,15,17,18).
Considerations Price, specific craving.
Types of food Sushi, Middle Eastern, Ramen 
Participants who want to celebrate a special occasion or gath-
er friends or family expect more from their takeaway experi-
ence. They are more likely to consider health and they want 
to make sure that they are getting a high quality meal, which 
they often ensure by choosing a restaurant they have tried be-
fore or relying on recommendations.
The meal has to be presentable, as it is often shared with 
friends, family or a loved one. It is typically eaten at the din-
ing Table. Participants who expect a bit more from their take-
away meal experience are more likely to order bigger por-
tions, which creates leftovers.
 
13 out of 19 workbooks fit into this category (see Appendix 
B06.02), which has been sorted for extra items provided with 
the takeaway and used own plate and cutlery.
 
The two sorting criteria distinguish two different settings 
within this expectation – the nicely presented individual meal 
and the ideal for sharing meals. The two settings are different 
in the way the meal is eaten and how leftovers are stored. This 
spread showcases the nicely presented individual meal.
 
The meal in this category is well presented in the packaging 
and is therefore often eaten straight from it. Extra items are 
provided with the meal, such as napkins, dressing, chopsticks, 
etc., which shows that the restaurant has thought about the 
eating experience. The meal is individually served. The pack-
aging is presentable enough to be placed on the Table.
The packaging for this kind of meal is very often cardboard 
in a combination with plastic. The cardboard containing food 
remnants cannot be recycled and is therefore thrown out in 
residual waste. The leftovers are most often stored in the 
packaging, as it is not convenient to transfer into the users 
own container. All participants showing this behaviour were 
in their mid-twenties and living in Copenhagen. They were 
often eating together with friends.

asked about their habits and preferences regarding takeaway 
(described in the following paragraf) from the canteen even 
though they had already stated that they did not use the work-
place canteen for takeaway. In these cases the participants 
have either tried to answer the questions based on what they 
think they would do or typed in that they do not have experi-
ence with takeaway from canteens. 
The majority of participants buy takeaway from restaurants 
(almost 80% for both groups). Using takeaway as a treat and 
buying a reasonably priced meal are also popular reasons 
(18.9%). Most participants would share the meal with their 
family (41.9%) or eat it by themselves (21.6%). Almost half 
of participants (47.3%) agree or strongly agree that they are 
more likely to eat from the packaging when the meal is nicely 
presented within the packaging. 65% are more likely to eat 
from their own plate if they want to have a nice Table setting. 
Takeaway meals in general
Being leak proof is by far the most important quality in a con-
tainer, with 73% giving it a rating of 5/5 in how much they 
value this quality. Furthermore, most participants eat at their 
dining Table, with 85.6% normally eating dinner at the Table 
(the survey allowed participants to choose more than one op-
tion). In addition, 27% normally eat dinner on the couch. 
Motivations and ability for participating in a reusable 
container system
A survey was made to further investigate takeaway habits and 
priorities as well as validate the patterns revealed from the 
workshops and workbook material. 
06.7 Survey
A survey was sent out through social media and shared with 
the CEO of Kokkenes Køkken. The full results can be found 
in Appendix B06.03. Here, 275 participants answered the sur-
vey with most of them being in the age group of 25-34 years 
old (52.7%). This might be due to the fact that the survey 
was shared with the network available to the project group. 
The survey respondents primarily live with their partner 
(43.3%) or roommate(s) (24%), but some also live either by 
themselves (16%) or with their partner and children (13.5%). 
The majority of participants were either full time employed 
(44.7%) or students (39.6%). 
Buying takeaway from canteens
14.8% of participants without children buy takeaway from 
their canteen at work. This number was slightly higher for 
participants with children, as 21.7% of them buy takeaway 
from their canteens. The most common reason for ordering 
takeaway from the canteen is not having enough time to cook 
(as 31.1% of participants stated).
It should be noted that there was an error in the way that the 
survey was set up resulting in the survey respondents being

The survey respondents were asked to choose factors which 
would increase the likelihood of them participating in a re-
usable container system (they were allowed to choose more 
than one factor). The most influential factor is whether the 
container is easy to return, with 77.7% of respondents stat-
ing that this aspect would make them more likely to use the 
system. This implies the importance of simplicity; the time, 
physical effort and brain cycles it takes to return the contain-
er are very important aspects when ensuring that the user’s 
ability is high enough to perform the targeted behaviour of 
returning the container. The second most influential factor is 
not having to be stressed about returning a container (66.2%), 
this suggests that a negatively encouraging coercive interven-
tion model, where users will be punished for not returning the 
container in time is not preferred. The third most influential 
factor is the motivation for contributing to a more sustainable 
system (56.9%). Other popular factors are getting a discount 
on meals (52.3%), having the possibility to buy the container 
(22.7%) and not having to return the container (45.8%). 
06.8 MVP Test Luring with Free 
Takeaway
The MVP test was launched on the 12th of April 2021. No 
one had sadly ordered any takeaway after 3 weeks of launch. 
Therefore, it was agreed with KK to tempt and motivate the 
canteen customers to try out the system by offering them a 
free meal. During a lunch break 12 customers signed up for a 
free meal, and gave insightful feedback as to why they had not 
yet tried out the system.
When discussing the MVP feedback with the site manager, 
the need for having one platform for all communication and 
planning of the food was empathised. This was also seen in 
the earlier presented workmodels. As users have different 
needs when ordering takeaway - an idea emerged: all users 
should be able to subscribe for food either by food type or 
by weekday (one user mentioned that he would like to order 
every Tuesday, as it was his cooking day at home). It would 
be possible for the canteen to create food plans a couple of 
weeks in advance, making users cross off the days they want 
to order for. 
06.9 Affinity Diagram // Summa-
ry of Insights
To organise all insights gained during the explorative user 
studies, including fieldwork at Edison Huset and the gener-
Survey
There is a big potential for selling more takeaway 
from canteens, as buying takeaway from restaurants 
is very common.
Buying takeaway from canteens is something that 
fits into a busy weekday schedule, where there is not 
enough time to cook, as seen in the fast and conve-
nient expectation from the workbook segmentation. 
Takeaway meals from the canteens can, however, 
also be a way to treat yourself, which is an important 
part of the experience expectation. 
About half of the participants are in line with the doc-
umentation from the workbooks in regard to being 
more likely to eat out of the packaging when the meal 
is presented nicely. 
The survey confirmed that it is common to eat from 
your own plates when desiring a nice table setting.  
While most people eat at their dining table, quite a 
lot of people also eat dinner on the couch, which is 
also noticed in the generative user studies. This type 
of eating should therefore be accommodated in the 
container design. 
An easy return system is the most powerful motivator 
for getting consumers to participate in the system. 
Sustainability is an important motivator for using the 
system. 
Survey
The project group had hoped to reveal some kind of 
characteristics from the survey and workbook seg-
mentation, which could be used to develop personas 
that could act as a guiding star in relation to the user 
needs in the ideation phase. However, the survey and 
workbook segmentation did not reveal any specific 
habits related to any age or ways of living, meaning 
that the purpose of buying takeaway is not as simple 
as that. Nevertheless, the user insights are structured 
in an affinity diagram which are used to develop de-
sign specifications as described in the last section of 
this chapter.
==PAGE:92==
84
ative user studies, an affinity diagram was made (see Figure 
06.9.1). 
The diagram was divided into two parts; the first part includ-
ed all concrete insights focusing on the packaging functional-
ities, with the purpose of supporting the design specification 
for the reusable container (product according to the core ele-
ments of Mont’s framework). The second part focused on all 
other aspects relevant to a reusable container system (service, 
infrastructure, actor network), in order to support the design 
specification for the system (see Appendix B06.04). 
Affinity diagram Part 1 - packaging functionalities
The first part of the affinity diagram was divided into 7 overar-
ching categories: Durability, Aesthetics, Protecting the Food, 
Optimised for Storage, Optimised for Travel, Food Safety and

Most common reasons why users had not yet tried 
the system

Users had not seen the advertisement and did 
not know about the option (the advertisement 
material had therefore failed in broadcasting the 
offer)
•	
Users thought takeaway meant “to-go” food 
during lunch hours (the communication was not 
clear enough)
•	
Many users worked from home due to corona, 
so they either had not seen it or it did not make 
sense for them as they were only allowed in the 
office once a week
Reasons for actively not choosing to buy takeaway
•	
A well structured cooking routine at home
•	
Already subscribing to Aarstiderne or other meal 
services
What would make consumers use the system?
•	
The users should be able to check the menu, as 
many disclaimed that the type of food is very 
important to whether or not they want to buy the 
meal for dinner
•	
The menu should be available at least one week 
in advance, as some of the users have weekly 
meal plans for themselves and their family 
•	
Some users also mentioned that it was too much 
planning/too inflexible to order the day before 
at noon, as they were just not in the mindset of 
planning for the takeaway yet (the takeaway ser-
vice is not commonly known at Edison Huset yet 
or at least a part of the “culture”)
•	
The food should be preserved well and not shak-
en around in the container
•	
Some mentioned that the price was important - it 
should make sense for them economically and 
50 DKK seems like a good compromise
Regarding the box
•	
It was mentioned that many people bike to and 
from work, and it would therefore be a physi-
cal challenge if they should carry food for the 
whole family (4 containers for 4 meals). Weight, 
size and stackability of the container is therefore 
once again stressed as very important. 
•	
Most people preferred the round container to the 
rectangular, as it was more aesthetically pleasing
Furthermore, most of the users mentioned that they 
would like to use the reusable container system. They 
mentioned that they did not see it as a problem to re-
turn the container back to the canteen, as they would 
come back to the office and canteen everyday. The 
hypothesis that consumers in the canteen market have

06.10 Design specification 
The affinity diagrams ensured that the most important points 
from the research and analysis phase were gathered and struc-
tured in one place. A design specification could now be creat-
ed based on all the input along with practical considerations 
for the development of the system and container. As with the 
affinity diagram the design specification is divided into two 
parts, one for the system and one for the container. The design 
specification is a very comprehensive document. An extract 
is shown here.  
The Design Specification v1.0 (see Appendix B06.04) was 
later in the design process reviewed, with representatives 
from Kokkenes Køkken as a part of the co-creation workshop, 
see Section 07.2. 
The design specification formed the basis for the further de-
velopment of the product service system. The early develop-
ment phases are described in the following chapter Ideation 
and Concept development.

07.1 Introduction 
Before ideating and developing concepts for the solution,  a 
co-creation session with KK is held. The focus of the session 
is to evaluate the Design Specification v1.0 (see Appendix 
B06.04) seen from a canteen perspective, as well as conduct 
exercises that will allow the project group to deep dive into 
the different thoughts and visions the stakeholders might have 
for the solution. 
Based on both the co-creation session with KK and the find-
ings from the explorative user studies, ideation on both the 
system and the container is initiated. To evaluate and concep-
tualise ideas, several different methods are used, including a 
morphology exercise for both the system and the container. 
Ideating and conceptualising the system is mainly based on 
the broader takeaway system in the restaurants (as described 
as step 3 in the three-step roadmap) to ensure that the ideas 
are not limited to or restrained by the narrowed canteen per-
spective. 
Finally, three system concepts and six container concepts are 
chosen. App wireframes for the consumers are made for each 
system concept, and mock-ups are made for all container con-
cepts. These are described and presented in the Concept Cat-
alogue (see separate document).
07.2 Co-creation
Co-creation is a helpful tool to fully understand the user and 
their needs. It includes multiple actors and can be used in 
different stages of the design process (Mattelmäki & Visser, 
2011). An introduction to the co-creation methodology can be 
found in Section 02.5.
A co-creation workshop was conducted with employees from 
Kokkenes Køkken, all with different roles and areas of exper-
tise (see Appendix A07.01). The objective of the workshop 
was to apply the participants’ expertise and together create 
a vision of how the product and system should look and per-
form, as well as to create engagement in the change-oriented 
goals. The co-creation process should in the end interweave 
the participants ideas and skills together with the project 
group’s in an iterative way (Mattelmäki, 2008). 
A workshop plan was outlined to structure and define the 
overall purpose of inviting the participants into a workshop. 
The initial purpose was to include the participants in the de-
velopment process of the container and make them choose 
between different concept ideas. However, this was adjusted 
to support the design phase at the given time. With a brief 
look at the Gantt Chart to uncover the tasks to come in the 
future design process. It was decided to use the time with the 
participants on (1) understanding and uncovering their mo-
tivation and expectations for the project, (2) make them pri-
oritise the pre-defined design requirements and (3) make a 
customer profile and value map of the “to be” solution. The 
plan was structured to accommodate the three key stages in a 
co-creation workshop: create comfort, stage discussion and 
develop together (see Appendix A07.01 and A07.02 for the 
workshop plan).
Exercises
The workshop was conducted online on the visual collabo-
ration tool Miro, due to COVID-19 restrictions (see the in-
formation box in Section 06.5 for an elaboration of the tool). 
Using Miro has its pros and cons, as it creates the opportunity 
to support the exercises with probs. However, this does not

necessarily come as an advantage if the participants are not

The first exercise was therefore performed with the purpose 
of onboarding the participants in how to manage using Miro. 
This exercise was also used to create comfort, as they had to 
present a few facts about who they are, their favourite meal 
and why they are motivated for the project with their self-
made avatar, see Figure 07.2.1. The exercise was essential for 
the participants to get into the mindset of how to work with 
Miro, and it also proved to be a lot of fun for both participants 
and facilitators. 
The second exercise was the Verbal Mood Board also a part 
of the create comfort phase. The idea with this exercise was 
to get the participants in the creative zone and prepare them 
to perform the visual moodboard, which required an idea of 
what they would like the product to express or make them 
feel. Five minutes were given to write down different expres-
sions, followed by a discussion of the most interesting sub-
jects. The words provided guidance towards which style and 
expression the container should communicate in the visual 
moodboard (Appendix A07.03).
The third exercise consisted of creating a visual moodboard as 
the final part of creating comfort among the participants, see 
Figure 07.2.2. Here, the participants were given an individu-
al board to find pictures (on any search engine) that express 
the style or feeling they would like the container to include. 
The participants were also shown the moodboard of Luups 
Bakken as an inspiration before initiating the exercise (see 
Appendix A07.04) along with some inspirational pictures (see 
Appendix A07.05). 
The moodboards and their reflection provided a common 
visual understanding of the aspects that Kokkenes Køkken

The participants were set to validate the predefined design re-
quirements as an exercise to stage a discussion among the par-
ticipants, see Figure 07.2.3. The exercise was set off by mak-
ing the participants read all the requirements and then making 
them individually state whether or not they found it important 
for the design solution. This was done by placing a label say-
ing “keep” or “drop”. All requirements with a drop label were 
discussed in plenum to gain some insight on their reasoning. 
It was interesting to see that there was a common agreement 
in regards to some of the functional properties, such as the 
weight and whether or not it should be able to go in the mi-
crowave. The reasoning for this was that they did not want to 
found interesting and valuable for their brand. In its essence, 
the exercise was also about figuring out what kind of visual 
identity KK wanted to brand themselves by, which this PSS 
can help them with. Therefore, the moodboards were used as 
inspiration in the ideation phase, to create ideas and concepts 
for the container design as well as to detail the final container 
concept (see Section 11/)
The exercise showed that all participants wanted the product 
to include a natural expression in the sense of nordic design, 
where pure plastic was not an option. This also correlates to 
the earlier user test, where most people disliked the look of 
plastic as it does not express sustainability. The vision of the 
project is to create a sustainable PSS, which also entails a style 
and brand identity that should signal sustainability. One of the 
participants also focused on elements such as an eye-catching 
signature colour or a shape that could make the product stand 
out in a crowd. He gave the example of the first iPhone, which

time choosing only three criteria when they found them all 
equally important. Once the ice was broken and they started 
to choose their criteria, it was possible to see how their dif-
ferent backgrounds and expertise pulled them towards certain 
criteria. E.g. Maj, the CEO at Kokkenes Køkken, went for the 
more technical criteria, such as good insulation properties and 
enhancing the airflow of the container. Whereas Teddy, the 
head chef at Edison Huset, went for more aesthetical criteria, 
such as providing the most pleasant sensory feeling (aesthet-
ically pleasing and comfortable to hold), while enhancing the 
visual brand of the company. Rune, the operational manager, 
preferred robustness, aesthetic and the sustainability of the 
container. Asmus agreed with Rune, however, he also high-
lighted that the presentation of the food was really important.

cise got a bit rushed, due to loss of time and it only took five 
constrain which material could be used for the final solution 
by demanding a certain weight or microwave ability. They 
rather saw this feature as a “nice to have”, in other words as 
a criteria. This exercise also gave the participants insights to 
the different tradeoffs that the project group needs to make 
when creating the final solution, as including all aspects in the 
solution would be difficult. 
The results from this exercise were used to modify the Design 
Specification into a version 2.0 (see Appendix A07.06 and

The product should have a simple and elegant shape 
with elements inspired by nature in the sense of nordic 
design.

which requires an eye catching colour or shape.

The requirements moved to criteria were: 
Should be able to withstand -20 degrees
Should not be scratched when using metal utensils
Should be microwaveable
Should weigh between 100-500 grams
The requirements removed were: 
The user should rinse the container after use (as it 
could not be expected that the user would do so)
The prioritized criteria were:
Gain an overview of the containers in the system, the 
container has to permit airflow, and facilitate good in-
sulation (Maj, CEO of KK)
The container emits a good experience (aesthetically 
and comfortably when eating) together with a strong 
visual brand identity (Teddy, head chef at Edison 
Huset)
Robustness, sustainability, and aesthetics (Rune, oper-
ations manager)
Robustness, aesthetics and the possibility to nicely 
present the food (Asmus, quality manager)
The canteen experiences a lot of pains in the current 
system with communicating to the canteen customers 
from different platforms. The canteen would like to 
have one platform where they communicate the menu, 
sell meals and gather information about the orders
Consumer pains consist of not knowing the possibility 
of buying takeaway meals from the canteen, buying a 
meal in a container that does not keep the temperature 
and having to get rid of single-use packing after each 
purchased meal
The user should have a platform where they can keep 
track of the menu, their containers and the amount of 
CO2 emissions that they save by buying into a sustain-
able product-service system

The value map and customer profile provided the project 
group with two value propositions that were used in the 
ideation phase when creating business models for the prod-
uct-service system.  The participants used their expertise and 
decided upon the most important values. These insights are 
used in the conceptualisation phase (see section 07.03). Fur-
thermore, view the filled out Value Propositions in Appendix 
07.07.
The most common pains that Kokkenes Køkken experience 
are mostly related to the communication of the takeaway sys-
tem, such as too many communication channels, high variance 
in sales which is hard to plan for, and not having a common 
way for managing and tracking takeaway orders. Whereas 
minutes instead of ten minutes. The changes made to the de-
sign criteria can be viewed in A07.06 and Appendix B07.01.
In the last exercise, the participants were asked to develop 
a modified value proposition canvas, in order to define the 
value they would like to give to the consumer as well as to 
themselves, see Figure 07.2.5 for a snippet of the exercise. 
The exercise consisted of developing a customer profile and 
a value map for the consumers and Kokkenes Køkken in a 
fun and alternative way. The main goal of the exercise was 
to make the participants define the customer profile, visualize 
the value to be created, and create a possible product/market 
fit as described in the Value Proposition Canvas (“Value Prop-
osition Canvas,” n.d.).

for the consumers (employees at Edison Huset) the pains are

lowering the quality of the meal. All in all, this exercise pro-

mapping the value and creating a product/market fit.

07/ Ideation and Concept Development
Co-creation Session
A co-creation workshop differs from a generative 
workshop with its structure and aim to gain a spe-
cific output. The co-creation workshop had in total 
three goals: (1) understand and uncover the partici-
pants motivation and expectations for the project, (2) 
make them prioritise predefined design requirements 
and criteria and (3) make a customer profile and val-
ue map of the “to be” solution. All three goals were 
reached and insights were gained due to the game 
plan, which was structured before conducting the 
workshop. However, even though a fair amount of 
time was set off to accommodate for each exercise, 
there were still some slips in the time schedule. This

was expected as all participants were new to Miro 
and had extroverted personalities with a passion for 
the concept. The time schedule was adjusted continu-
ously to cater for the overall time plan, meaning that 
some exercises got a bit more attention than others 
- but this was expected when planning the workshop 
where each exercise got a priority in case time was 
short. 
Lastly, working in Miro had its pros and cons. How-
ever, the cons were easily mitigated by making proper 
preparation of the Miro boards and the exercises that 
the participants had to go through. The participants 
were primarily only asked to do simple tasks, such as 
move things around and not define new items.
Figure 07.3.1 - Output from the Magic Thing brain-
storming exercise.
==PAGE:108==
100
All group members brainstormed individually on the means 
to ensure that as much of the solution space was covered. All 
means were after the exercise gathered in one function-means 
tree. Some of the functions were split into two and some new 
ones were included. To decide on which concepts should 
be drawn in the morphology, another individual brainstorm 
was made. Afterwards the concepts were chosen by voting. 
Choosing and drawing concepts in the morphology was 
however a very time consuming and iterative process, as the 
functions-means tree was very elaborate by including both 
business, motivational and practical focuses of the complex 
system, forcing the group to go much more into detail with all 
concepts than necessary at that time being. See Figure 07.4.1 
for an overview of the drawn morphology concepts.

developed concepts covered the broader takeaway system in

After describing the six concepts it was decided not to proceed 
with the PSS Tomorrow, as it did not differentiate much from 
the MVP-test setup. Furthermore, Ultra Convenience was too 
dependent on strategic partnerships with e.g. Wolt, making 
the concept more appropriate as a future vision of what the 
PSS should be like. Community life, The Capitalist and Greta 
Thunberg were chosen as concepts to further detail and eval-
uate with users and stakeholders. Here, elements from Player 
1 could be incorporated.

Community Life
The Community Life concept is all about the social experience and your local community. The users will own the 
containers and swap containers at restaurants. Furthermore, being a part of Absalon Life, will provide you with 
recommendations from the chefs, and for new and fun experiences close to where you live. Help the 
environment and your local community by joining the community and be part of something bigger! The partners 
will buy a subscription, which provides them with the containers they need and exposure of their brand together 
with loyal customers.

Ability
Possible triggers

Creating loyal customers
Sustainable branding
Value is created through togetherness. By helping 
others and getting new exciting experiences close 
to where you live with people you love.
Social cohesion, by getting acceptance and being 
part of the community. Futhermore, to be part of a 
new social movement.
Social deviance. Being a part of the community 
makes it easier to enter the system.
Strengths
Facilitator and Spark, The app facilitates ﬁnding 
new spots in the local area and the 
recommendations from other users sparks an 
interest for new experiences.
Weaknesses

Greta Thunberg
This concept focuses on environmental sustainability above all. The user buys into the system by simply investing 
in a container. When buying takeaway the user brings their container along when they pick up their next meal - 
swapping the container for another one that has been cleaned by the restaurant, containing the meal. The 
business model excites the user to use their container again and again ensuring full utilization of resources.
Motivation
Ability
Possible triggers
Value for the Consumer
Value for Partners
This concept gives the best sustainable brand value, 
and will attract all consumers that are not willing to 
compromise on behalf of the environment.
Partners save money in the long run, as they won't 
have to purchase single- use packaging anymore.
A feeling of making a diﬀerence for the environment. 
The consumer gets concrete numbers on the 
amount of CO2 saved by changing from single- use 
packing to reusable packaging.
Anticipation The concept inspires users to make a 
diﬀerence for the environment by changing little 
things in their day to day behaviour.
Brain cycles Users who want to make more 
sustainable choices are now able to participate in 
this system without having to plan anything.
Strengths
Facilitators. Users who want to live more 
sustainable now have a concrete way to do it.
Weaknesses
Users are motivated to use 
the container many times, 
but buying a new container 
is the only way for Rotine to 
gain revenue.
Restaurants will have to 
have extra containers in 
order to swap with users
The user is deviating from 
social norms by actively 
changing their behaviour in 
order to make a change for 
the environment, which can 
be a challenge.
App registers the 
use via partner 
conﬁrmation at 
restaurant
No tracking is 
needed
No distribution logistics 
are needed. Partner 
restaurants simply order 
the amount of extra

Player 1
This concept use elements of gamiﬁcation to create a challenge driven environment that prompts the consumer 
to use the app and service. The Rotine app will include design elements such as badges, levels and score points to 
inﬂuence and motivate the consumer. Rewards, such as discount or a free meal are given to the consumer (on the 
restaurants bill) to create a lot of immediate activity and eventually lock the customers loyalty to the service.

within the system and are rewarded with 
points that can be used on unique takeaway 
experiences
Pleasure and hope. The gamiﬁed app will create a 
curiosity at the user. He/she will want to gain a 
(higher) reward for each accomplishment
Money. Users save money on their favorite 
takeaway by using the system and non- rutine is 
combatted by encouraging activities from day to

Ultra Convenience
Takeaway is all about convenience! The Ultra Convenience concept is based on creating a solution with as high 
convenience as possible, focusing on an easy transition from the use of disposable packaging to a reusable 
system. All choices are therefore based on what requires the least ability to enter the system. Rotine makes 
strategic partnerships, to ensure that the user won't have to enter a new platform to use the solution - all 
interactions happen via existing takeaway platforms such as Wolt.

07/ Ideation and Concept Development
Vision in Product Design (ViP)
To try and open up the solution space for developing an in-
novative container an interaction-centred design approach, 
called ViP was tried out. Here, the group followed the pro-
cedure as presented in the article Dancing with a Machine: A 
Case of Experience-Driven Design by Hekkert et al. (2003). 
p
p
product
user
past
present / future
context
interaction
product
context
context
Figure 07.5.2 - Vision in product design approach (Hekkert et al., 
2003)
ViP shifts the focus of interacting with products from what 
the product does to how it does it (Hekkert et al., 2003). The 
main objective is to redesign products based on a visionary 
description of the interaction - the case study presented in the 
article a redesign of a photocopier based on a dance. 
This design approach was applied on the container by first-
ly describing the interaction with single-use packaging. The 
following contexts were described as “eating takeaway of a 
single-use packing”:
•	
“Like a one night stand”
•	
“Quickly over”
•	
“Could be bad/could be good”
•	
“No expectations”
•	
“Quickly forgotten”
•	
“Somewhat random”
Afterwards the future context for the new container design 
was defined as a new PSS for packaging.  Hereafter, an indi-
vidual brainstorm on experiences that could be used as visions 
was done (see Appendix A07.12). After discussing how the 
different experiences might work as visions for the container 
design, six experiences were chosen as visions and further de-
scribed, see Figure 07.5.3. 
The three concepts were further detailed by filling out a Busi-
ness Model Canvas (BMC) for each of the concepts along 
with highlighting the benefits of choosing one of the con-
cepts from the canteen perspective. And lastly, a Swimlane 
Diagram was made for each concept (see Appendix A07.09 to 
A07.11) and app wireframes were made to test the concepts in 
a generative way (see Concept Catalogue).
07.5 Development of the Con-
tainer
The container concept was developed using several different 
ideation methods, including an individual brainstorming ses-
sion of the two different settings (ideal for sharing and nice-
ly plated) (see Figure 07.5.1). This was done using Vision in 
Product Design (ViP) as well as an individual Morphology 
exercise. Furthermore, simple moodboards were used as in-
spiration on style, material and surfaces to support the com-
munication of the ideated container drawings.  
Morphology
Developing the system concepts based on morpholo-
gy turned out to be an extremely challenging and cum-
bersome exercise, which was a surprise to the group, 
as morphology is an exercise performed many many 
times before. The morphology exercise demonstrat-
ed the high level of complexity that the reuse system 
has, and the group had a hard time balancing the right 
level of detail for the functions and means. Many of 
the functions depended on each other, and the group 
was forced to consider a lot of detailed scenarios to 
be able to make a decision. This was therefore an ex-
tremely time consuming and iterative process, which 
might have benefitted from either blackboxing some 
of the functions, or conducting some more research 
before diving into making decisions (this could espe-
cially have been relevant for the logistical part of the 
functions; collection/delivery, return and tracking). 
Furthermore, the morphology also made it clear that 
the group did not always have the same understand-
ing of the concepts, which was also a big strength of 
the exercise, as the group could align understandings 
and expectations in relation to the concepts.

A sketching session for each vision was done individually, 
were fun and interesting ideas emerged from this exercise (see

Morphology

Means for these functions were brainstormed and included in 
the functions-means tree (see Appendix A07.14). When trying 
to draw the concepts, it quickly became clear that all group 
members had their own perception and visions for the con-
tainer concepts, therefore it was decided to draw and visualise 
these individually (see Appendix A07.15). 
While still working individually the different concepts were 
ViP
The ViP exercise was really fun and interesting to 
try out. However, the group found it very difficult 
to ignore all of the gathered insights from the previ-
ous design phases and be creative without any con-
straints. Opening fully up for the solution spaces was 
therefore maybe a bit more limited than expected. 
However, at the end of the session some important 
aspects to include in the design of the container were 
discovered. 
•	
Visions such as “opening a box of crayons” 
made it clear that the experience when opening 
the container is important to consider. Opening 
the container should provide a “wow” effect and 
create excitement for the meal. It is therefore 
also important that the container shape allows 
for an appealing plating of the meal elements. 
•	
Ergonomic factors are important to consider in 
order to create a container that is inviting and 
comforting. 
•	
The container should have unexpected elements, 
while at the same time being familiar.
