08.1 Introduction
This chapter focuses on evaluating the different system and 
product concepts, developed in the previous chapter, with the 
purpose of finding a concept that suits both the stakeholders 
at Kokkenes Køkken and the consumers of the system. First 
of all, findings from the MVP test were considered, followed 
by a feedback session with KK as well as an enacting, telling, 
making workshop. 
08.2 MVP Test Evaluation
Before evaluating the concepts with stakeholders of the sys-
tem, the project group seeked to evaluate the outcomes of the 
MVP test to finally conclude and learn from the findings. 
After signing up 12 participants for free takeaway, the kitchen 
staff had finally received orders for takeaway. However there 
had been some problems with the QR codes for borrowing 
and returning the containers, and it was therefore not possi-
ble for the group to track the activity. Furthermore, one of 
the participants stated that he had ordered takeaway, and that 
his meal was forgotten, where there had been no staff around 
when he came to pick it up. This example insinuates that it 
is difficult to start a new routine and that the simple system 
(sending an email), might not be enough to ensure that the 
staff remembers to make the meal. 
A questionnaire was created to get feedback from the partici-
pants, here the main purpose was to get an idea of the willing-
ness of the customers to buy containers to enter the reusable 
takeaway system, as well as getting an idea of the price range 
(see questionnaire in Appendix A08.01).
After sending the link to the questionnaire three times, only 
one of the participants had answered; a male between 18-24 
years old, who was willing to buy a set of containers for 50 
DKK (free text answer), and not willing to pay 250 DKK. 
However, one participant’s feedback was not sufficient to 
make any final conclusions on the price nor sufficient enough 
for a final validation of the reuse system. Thus, the MVP test 
can not serve as a proof of concept, and a new test needs to be 
implemented in the implementation plan (see Section 12.6). 
The MVP test did however give some valuable insights to the 
solution, as well as validate the need for a user friendly sys-
tem supporting the sales of takeaway.
MVP Test
	
●
Physical advertisement like posters and pamphlets 
are not enough to catch users attention
	
●
Users of Edison huset are in theory willing to 
start using a PSS system for reusable takeaway 
containers as their ability to return the containers 
are quite high and they are motivated by e.g. the 
sustainable benefits. 
	
●
Users needs to be triggered or get extra motivated 
to enter the system for the first time - e.g. via a 
campaign with discount on or free meals 
	
●
The system for ordering takeaway needs to be 
user friendly for both canteens and consumers
	
●
The system needs to help its users in their 
workflow; e.g. reminders for making orders are 
essential
	
●
Robust technology for borrowing and returning 
containers needs to be implemented
MVP Test
Seen in retrospect, the MVP test has been way too 
big a mouthful to implement and monitor in parallel 
to the full design process. If the reusable container 
system for takeaway should have been properly val-
idated as a proof of concept in Edison huset, more 
time and effort should have been put into it, and oth-
er parts of the project should not have been made or 
at least heavily down prioritised. As the test did not 
fully play out in the periods where the group worked 
on other things, the test became more of a burden to 
try to make work, than actually being a valuable in-
put to the solution. Testing within Edison Huset has 
undoubtedly also been more of a challenge than other 
canteens might have been, as there was no takeaway 
culture in Edison Huset to begin with. 
That being said, the group did learn a lot from mak-
ing the MVP test.

08/ Concept Evaluation
08.3 Feedback from Kokkenes 
Køkken
The Concept Catalogue was sent to the participants from KK 
containing the three system concepts and seven container 
concepts prior to the feedback session as preparation. 
First, the three system concepts were presented which nat-
urally initiated a loose discussion after the presentation of 
each concept (see feedback in Appendix A08.02-08.04). The 
discussion continued as the stakeholders were asked to fill 
out a simple evaluation Matrix together, weighing up the pa-
rameters; brand diversity/uniqueness, motivation for users, 
motivation for consumers, motivation for canteens, business 
model and tracking/logistics (see Table 08.3.1 and Appendix 
A08.05, page 1-2). A common agreement was found with mo-
tivation for consumers having the highest priority and track-
ing/logistics having the lowest priority. The prioritisation of 
the different elements in the PSS clearly shows that KK is a 
company that first of all wants to motivate their customers in 
using their additional services. Without the consumers there 
is no business. 
The Greta Thunberg concept had the highest score followed 
by the Capitalist and lastly Community Life. As explained by 
one of the stakeholders, the Greta Thunberg concept is the one 
that describes KK’s vision the best. 
Containers
Secondly, the six container concepts were presented for the 
stakeholders by showing the Concept Catalogue along with 
mock-ups that could give them an idea of the shape, size and 
material use. The stakeholders were all impressed by the dif-
ferent concepts but did not immediately find one favoured de-
sign (see feedback comments in Appendix A08.06). 
However, after some thoughts they all three decided on a fa-
Figure 08.3.1 - Image from the feedback 
session with stakeholders
==PAGE:120==
112
When discussing different materials that could be used for the 
container, all three stakeholders agreed that the lid should be 
made from transparent hard plastic and not glass as it is too 
heavy, fragile and noisy when colliding during cleaning.
The bottom part should be made from Kareline (see Section 
09.4). Arguments for choosing this material are;
•	
it has a good sustainability profile, as it is made from 
50% sustainably sourced wood fiber and can be injection 
molded and recycled, thus creating a good storytelling 
•	
It feels and looks sustainable (opposite to 100% plastic 
(see insights from Affinity diagram in Section 06.9) 
•	
It is very aesthetically appealing
•	
it fits together with Luups Bakken, that is also made from 
Kareline 
•	
It fits well with their overall brand identity
•	
it is a functional material that dries quickly
•	
Furthermore a contact is already established with a Finish 
supplier of the material as well as a manufacturer in Jut-
land, that because of the Luups Bakke, has experience in 
injection moulding the material
vorite, where Maj liked the functional concept the most, and 
Teddy and Rune combined elements from different concepts, 
respectively Hay and Rotate and Rotate and Eating Experi-
ence (see Appendix A08.07 for fan favourites). 
The stakeholders were supposed to evaluate the different con-
tainer concepts based on evaluation factors, but due to time 
limitations the stakeholders only got to prioritise the factors: 
•	
Uniqueness/recognizability (5, highest priority)
•	
Chefs ability to plate (pleasing to look at) (4) 
•	
Optimal for transport with food (3)
•	
Optimal for storage/transport when empty (2) 
•	
Pleasing to eat from (1, lowest priority) 
Container
	
●
Uniqueness and high recognisability is of higher 
priority than the container being optimal for trans-
port or storage
	
●
Functional elements such as snaps, stackability 
and optimised storage is important, but can be 
compromised in favour of a unique design
	
●
Using different colours and or unique shapes is a 
good way to attract attention
	
●
The elevational part of the eating experience is in-
teresting it can be used as a creative way to divide 
and nicely plate the food
	
●
All stakeholders want a container that has a 
bottom made of Kareline material and a lid made 
of transparent hard plastic. Lastly, the two parts 
should be sealed with snaps like IKEA containers.
System
	
●
Greta Thunberg is the concept scoring the highest 
across the weighted parameters; brand diversity/
uniqueness, motivation for users, motivation for 
consumers, motivation for canteens, business 
model and tracking/logistics
	
●
Merging the sustainability aspect in Greta 
Thunberg with the money raised incentive in 
the Capitalist could make sense in the long run. 
E.g. showing CO2 emission savings, that could 
be shared amongst friends, while still providing 
discounts to make people use the service. 
	
●
The stakeholders highlighted two business models 
that they believed would be the best;
	
●
Consumers buying a container, which can get 
swapped when they buy a new meal. Containers 
might be placed on an account, meaning that con-
sumers will not have to store them at home. 
	
●
Consumers subscribing to the containers by pay-
ing a symbolic subscription fee. 
	
●
Kokkenes Køkken is inevitably motivated by 
business plans that include a high profit. 
	
●
Community life is a good concept, but not optimal 
for implementing in the canteen industry. Shar-
ing the amount of takeaway meals purchased on 
a weekly/monthly basis with your colleagues is 
probably not that flattering. However, elements 
such as saving favourite meals and preferences 
could be implemented.
==PAGE:121==
113
08/ Concept Evaluation
Madglad
Madglad is a community kitchen in the heart of Vester-
bro. They have been front runners in establishing a re-
usable takeaway packaging system. An interview with 
the owner of Madglad Trine was held to get insights to 
their experiences with such a system. 
 
Choosing the container
Madglad started their reusable container takeaway 
system in 2009.  
In the beginning people would just come with their 
own plate, but the health authorities could not approve 
of the system, as bacteria might be transferred from 
the plate to the rest of the restaurant. They considered 
making their own containers to implement in a take-
back system, but the boxes were too expensive for the 
clientele frequenting the restaurant, as they would cost 
75 DKK.
They have now settled on a standard container from 
IKEA, which accommodates the most important prior-
ities; leakage proof, compact, versatile, freezer friend-
ly.
 
Business model
Around half of the customers get their meal as take-
away. Customers get 2 kr discount on every meal when 
using the box. 25-40 people a day (approximately 1/3 
of the customers getting their meal as takeaway) are 
using the boxes. Madglad swaps the container for a 
new one when customers hand them in and wash them.
 
The cost structure breaks even, the 2 kr is equal to 
what they pay for single-use packaging and the guest 
pay 30 kr for the packaging, which is what the contain-
er costs. Trine does not believe that the 2 kr discount 
motivates people. It is the loyal customers who invest 
in this system, a lot of people come every week.
It is quite simple for Madglad to keep up the system. 
Guests have formed a community and help each other 
Figure out how the system works. Trine believes that 
the sense of being a part of a community makes her 
customers take responsibility for their own actions.
 
Changing behaviour
Madglad believes that the behaviour change is well 
on its way and that consumers are changing behaviour 
patterns quickly. They have not considered using an 
existing system like Kleen Hub, because they want to 
keep it simple and cheap. Any extra cost will result in 
a more expensive meal.
The customers at Madglad have not requested more 
information about the sustainability aspects. Trine be-
lieves that a part of the success of the system is that it 
does not have a moral component to it.
However, she also states that it would be interesting 
for Madglad to know when the system breaks even 
sustainability wise.
Trine believes that habits are the most important com-
ponent in establishing a system like this.

The enactment activities were based on a restaurant takeaway 
context (see reflection below). The participants were taken 
through the onboarding process (introduced to the system, set-
ting up an account), ordering a meal, interacting with unique 
features for each system concept (such as sharing experience 
with friends, getting a discount or evaluating sustainability), 
interaction with the restaurant and returning the container. At 
the beginning of each enactment the participant was intro-
duced to a member of the master group who played a friend 
who wanted to show the participant a new app for a reusable 
takeaway system. The master group member then guided the 
participant through the app and to the “restaurant area”, where 
another member of the group interacted with the participant as 
a restaurant employee. After having gone through all parts of 
the system the experience was evaluated with the participant 
and the other system concepts were explained.  
 An overview of the agenda can be seen on Figure XX (see 
detailed agenda in Appendix 08.08).
 
After the system enactment exercise and evaluation, the par-
ticipants asked to rate all container concepts after interacting 
with the prototypes. Participants were guided through a sim-
ple version of evaluation matrix, as they were asked to rate 
five aspects of the container design after importance and then 
rate each container concept. The five aspect were:
 
1. 	
Ideal for sharing
2. 	
Ideal for individual meal
3. 	
Optimal for transport
4. 	
Optimal for storage/transport when empty
5. 	
Wow factor
 
The five aspects represent important parts of the container de-
sign, as discovered through the explorative user studies (see 
Chapter 8) and represented in the affinity diagram (see Sec-
tion 08.7).
An overview of the workshop activities can be seen on the 
following pages.
Feedback on system concepts
Participants were asked to provide feedback for the system 
concepts during the enactment exercise as well as afterwards. 
The six workshop participants tried out the enactment exer-
cise with one of the system concepts (explained through one 
of the app prototypes) and were only shown the two other 
concepts after the enactment exercise. Each system concept 
was therefore evaluated in detail by two participants and 
briefly evaluated by the four remaining participants. The most 
important insights for each of the system concepts are stated 
08.4 Enacting, Telling, Making 
A workshop was conducted with potential consumers in order 
to evaluate the three system concepts and six container con-
cepts. The workshop agenda was inspired by the generative 
design approach (Sanders & Stappers, 2012) for evaluating 
concepts and ideas in the relevant context. In the framework 
described by Sanders & Stappers three types of activities are 
important in order to bridge the gap between the designers 
and potential users:
•	
Making activities (such as making prototypes)
•	
Telling activities (such as communication through story-
boards).  
•	
Enactment activities (such as role play with props)
In preparation for the evaluation workshop the following ac-
tivities were conducted within each of the three categories.
 
Making
Wired UI prototypes for the consumer app for the three sys-
tem concepts (Greata Thunberg, Community life and the 
Capitalist) were created in order to realistically convey how 
participants might interact with the system. The wireframes 
were intended to convey some of the unique features of each 
concept and provide an impression of what the UI might look 
like. The prototypes were wired in a way where the partici-
pant was able to physically interact with the prototype (click 
on selected buttons) but it was not fully functional, as not all 
elements of the prototypes were interactive. It was therefore 
necessary to guide the participants’ use of the prototypes.
Furthermore the mock-ups of the six container concepts were 
used as props for the workshop (see Section 08.5)
 
Telling
It was decided to incorporate the telling activity in the system 
prototypes. This was done by introducing a few wireframes 
explaining each process as a part of the “create account” pro-
cess within each app prototype. The intro frames were used in 
the enactment exercise as an aid for introducing the system to 
the participant trying out the app. A member from the master 
group was standing beside the participant and provided addi-
tional information and answered any questions while going 
through the wireframes.
 
Enactment
Participants completed an enactment activity at the workshop, 
which allowed them to experience key elements of the system 
concepts through role play and interaction with prototypes.

system by the participants. Several participants were, howev-

for the container (in this enactment the price was set to 100 
DKK) in case they did not return it.
Generally, the participants liked the overall concept of engag-
ing with local restaurants and sharing their takeaway experi-

value as a stand-alone app, as it could work as a social media 
platform for sharing food related experiences with friends. 
Another participant stated that she would not want to share

being judged by them.

The Capitalist
Participants were generally very focused on whether the dis-

They would like to have a free trial period for trying out the

enough takeaway for the membership to become an advan-
tage. A general comment for this system concept is that the 
benefits (such as discounts or points) should be linked to take-
away in order to make sure the discounts are actually valuable 
for the consumers. Another participant noted that the system 
would have to include a lot of restaurants in order to make 
the subscription model worth it and to not feel too restricted 
when choosing takeaway by being a part of it. Furthermore, if 
the system included cups for coffee, the participant would be 
more likely to join.
One of the participants states that the subscription model 
would make him feel more attached to the system and there-
fore make him use it more.
Another participant stated that she would rather buy the con-
tainer than rent it.

This system concept is based on consumers owning their own 
containers, which received mixed feedback from the partici-
pants. Owning the box can be convenient and makes interac-

An evaluation of the five factors revealed the following priori-
ty based on the average of ratings from all participants:
 
•	
Optimal for transport with food (5, highest priority)
•

•	
Wow factor (3)
•	
Optimal for storage/transport when empty (2)
•	
Ideal for sharing (1, lowest priority)
 
All of the container concepts were rated within each factor, 
where the rating 6 was given to the container that was ex-
pected to perform the best and the rating 1 was given to the 
container which was expected to perform the worst. The value 
for each concept was found by using the individually rated 
factors as a multiplier to the score (see Appendix A08.10, 
page 1-2).
 
The average values across all participants can be viewed in 
Table 08.4.1. 
The results show that the most popular container concept is 
the Functional, which is especially due to it having the high-
est score in the Optimal for transport with food factor. The 
second most popular concept is the eating experience, which 
has the highest score in regard to Ideal for individual meals. 
This concept also had the second highest score in regard to 
wow factor. 
Furthermore the eating experience scored the highest in the 
factor Optimal for storage/transport when empty, however 
this score might be influenced by the fact that this was the 
only prototype that was 100% 3D printed, thus having a draft 
that made it possible to stack it when empty. This prototype 
Workshop 
For the system concepts
There are drawbacks and advantages of all three busi-
ness models (subscription model, buying the container 
or renting the container).
A concept like The Capitalist needs to be widely imple-
mented in order for the concept to make sense.
Owning the container might be less stressful than rent-
ing it, as having to return the container within a dead-
line is considered a stress factor.  
Consumers will need to be reminded to bring their con-
tainer if they own them, as forgetting the container is a 
big concern.
Consumers who own a container will need an alterna-
tive if they forget it (such as the possibility to rent a 
container).
Displaying the environmental impact of the system can 
serve as motivation. Some participants expect this fea-
ture and request it when they cannot see it (this insight 
was also empathised in Section 06.7 survey).
Making the takeaway experience more personal by e.g. 
adjusting the platform based on personal preferences 
and displaying favourite meals is a good way to engage 
consumers.
Pictures of menu items makes the process of ordering 
takeaway more enjoyable.
 
For the container concepts
The most important functionality of the container is be-
ing able to safely and effectively transport the takeaway 
meal home.
Optimising the container for the individual meal is more 
important than being optimised for sharing a meal.
The snaps from The Functional container concept are 
perceived as a safe system for transport.
The Eating experience concept is viewed as the most 
ideal for the individual meal and for storage or trans-
port when empty.

09.1 Introduction
This chapter will go through the different considerations made 
when choosing the final system and product concept. The 
considerations are based on insights gained through both the 
explorative user studies, the MVP test as well as the feedback 
session with KK and the enacting, telling, making workshop 
described in the previous chapter 08/ Concept Evaluation.
The concepts are chosen based on which will perform the 
best as a PSS solution for canteens in Kokkenes Køkken, 
black-boxing (for now) the third step of the three step road-
map; expanding to the restaurant takeaway market (see Figure 
04.5.1). 
09.2 Choosing the Final System 
Concept
The three step roadmap illustrated on Figure 04.5.1 shows the 
strategic plan of reaching the desired end goal, which is to 
penetrate the restaurant takeaway market. The system con-
cepts (described in Section 07.4 and presented in the Concept 
Catalogue) was developed on the basis of this end goal. As 
participants of the enacting, telling, making workshop did not 
have much experience with buying takeaway from canteens, 
the reenactment was also conducted in relation to the broader 
restaurant takeaway industry. 
As the final system concept that should be developed with-
in the timeframe of this master project focuses primarily on 
canteens, the feedback given by Kokkenes Køkken is weight-
ed higher than the enacting, telling, making workshop when 
choosing between the concepts. 
Feedback from KK made it very clear that Greta Thunberg 
would be the most relevant and feasible concept to implement 
in their canteens, due to the simple business model as well as 
the focus on sustainability, and this concept is therefore cho-
sen as the basis for the final solution.
 
The overall user feedback on the Greta Thunberg concept was 
also positive, when having in mind that the users were asked 
to evaluate the concept seen from the perspective of buying a 
take-away meal from a restaurant. The only concern was on 
the aspect of forgetting the container at home when purchas-
ing a meal for swapping. Here, the users wanted the possibili-
ty of getting out of the situation in a comfortable way. There-
fore an option for renting containers, once the user has bought 
at least one container, is therefore added to the concept. Fur-
thermore, elements from the Community Life is added to the 
final customer platform to compliment the main sustainability 
focus and create a more gathered customer platform. 
Lastly, the evaluation feedback showed that a certain custom-
er base is required to make a good solution for the users to 
choose the Community and the Capitalist concept. This, once 
again, argues for the advantage of making a proof of concept 
with a simple solution such as Greta Thunberg at Kokkenes 
Køkken. This concept can therefore be used to test and learn, 
before expanding the solution to the remaining canteen and 
restaurant industry. The gained experience will most likely 
also help in making a strategic partnership with companies 
such as the delivery service Wolt (which is found very import-
ant for the consumers of the restaurant takeaway industry). 
The Chosen System Concept
The final system concept chosen is illustrated in the ecosys-
tem map (see Figure 09.2.1), and is further described below. 
The final system concept consists of a container and two user 
platforms: one for the canteen to upload menus, manage or-
ders, etc. and another for the customers to order meals, man-
age containers, etc. The canteens pay a monthly subscription 
a bit below the price already spent on single-use packaging 
along with an additional fee for the provided product-service 
system. The canteens have to continuously optimise their 
spendings and rarely purchase a product or service without 
cutting an expenditure somewhere else. The small discount on 
the containers are therefore given, as an incentive for the can-
teens to “save” money on the packaging while they in return 
receive a functioning system for handling takeaway meals in 
an environmentally sustainable way. 
The customers buy a set of containers to become a part of the 
system. Once the containers are bought, they have the pos-
sibility to order and pick up meals by swapping their own 
container with a new one. Users can return their containers to 
the system at any time before picking up a new meal, which 
is convenient if they do not want to store their containers at 
home. Should they forget to swap their container before pick-
ing up a new order, users have the possibility of renting a 
container, which will have to be returned within a certain time 
frame. 
09.3 Choosing the Final Con-
tainer Concept
After choosing the final system concept, the container concept 
was chosen. To do this all insights gained from the feedback 
==PAGE:129==
121
09/ Choosing the Final Solution
session with KK and from participants of the enacting, tell-
ing, making workshop (as described in the previous chapter) 
were gathered. These insights were, as opposed to choosing 
the system concept, weighted equally. 
Furthermore, the master group tried to individually rate the 
different container concepts using the same evaluation matrix 
exercise as the one applied on the participants in the enacting, 
telling, making session, to see how much the group’s score 
would differentiate from the workshop score (see Appendix 
09.01). Here, the functional concept had the overall highest 
score with the eating experience as a runner up equal to the 
scores of the participants. This highlights the common con-
flict found in wanting a product design to be highly functional 
but also unique and aesthetically pleasing. 
All concepts were furthermore held up against the design 
specification to see whether or not they lived up to the re-
quirements and criteria (see Appendix B09.01). Here, discus-
sions were made in regards to functionality, the different user 
preferences and whether or not some of the concepts could 
be merged (e.g. the lid of the eating experience concept had 
not been specified). Viewed from a high level perspective, 
all concepts essentially consist of a container, a lid and some 
kind of closing mechanism that connects the two parts togeth-
er. Elements from one concept can therefore easily be applied 
to another. 
In the end it was decided to choose the shape from the eating 
experience combined with the functionalities of stackability 
(bottom on lid) and snap closing mechanism from the func-
tional container (see Figure 09.3.1). 
The shape was chosen based on its high score on wow fac-
tor and with the reasoning that an alternative and interesting 
shape ensures uniqueness and recognisability. A compromise 
the stakeholders and the group were willing to take in relation 
to optimal shape for transport and storage. The snap closing 
mechanism was chosen based on the user insights gained 
from the generative workshops, choosing the MVP test con-
tainer (see Section 06.4) as well as the generative workshop. 
Here, all users made it very clear that proper sealing was of 
highest priority, where snaps were the element that most users 
trusted the most. Further arguments for choosing the eating 
experience are listed below.

Choosing material for the container
Research in the early design process found that there is a sig-
nificant difference between the materials that the consumers 
perceive as being environmentally sustainable and the materi-
als that are actually environmentally sustainable (see Section 
06.9). Many consumers stated that they did not like the looks 
of plastic as they associate it with being unsustainable. How-
ever, plastics such as PP, PE or PET are the most common 
types of plastic to be recycled in Denmark, and making du-
rable products in these materials therefore makes it possible 
to prolong and close resource loops, ensuring a sustainable 
circular economy. 
The feeling of sustainability is also an important aspect for 
KK, as they want the product to visually express sustainabil-
ity, also supporting a simple way of storytelling. This was 
clearly seen in the moodboards they created during the co-cre-
ation workshop, which included a lot of nature elements and 
simple nordic design similar to the vision of the SANS! Con-
Simple, feasible and elegant design 
•	
Fits well with the moodboards created by KK and their 
vision of creating a nordic product design. 
•	
Fits well with Luups Bakken and the already established 
SANS! Concept
•	
Users think it has the most ideal shape for individual 
meals (individual meals are found more important than 
sharing meals)
•	
KK like the idea of the elevational part, that provides the 
option for dividing the meal in a creative way, without 
being limited to dividing food
•	
Good to hold in one hand (an aspect found important in 
user studies)
09.4 Choosing Materials for the 
Container
With the overall product elements in place, further detailing 
of the product is made by evaluating and finally deciding on 
the materials of the container. 
The different materials in this Section are evaluated based 
on the design specification, feedback session with stakehold-
ers and workshop with users. The design specifications have 
throughout the design process been refined to suit the contin-
uously gathered user insights from workshops and feedback 
sessions with users and stakeholders (see the iteration of the 
Design Specification v3.0 in Appendix B09.01). The most rel-
evant requirements from for the material is highlighted below:
•	
Must be more sustainable than single-use packaging
•	
Must endure a minimum of 500 wash cycles
•	
Must be able to withstand industrial dishwashing at 90

Based on sessions with stakeholders from Kokkenes Køkken, 
workshops with users and the design specification, it has been 
chosen to use Kareline, as the material for the container part 
of the product. The wood plastic combination gives the ma-
terial a good tactile feel and look of wood rather than plastic. 
Thus the material resembles the look of nature (and sustain-
ability) as required by the users and stakeholders. A summary 
of the Kareline material is shown in Table 09.4.1 (Roots & 
Design | Kupilka, n.d.).
Choosing material for the lid and sealing
A comment that was repeated by all actors throughout the 
sessions, was that there must be a transparent element in the 
container, The chefs at Kokkenes Køkken want this to expose 
their nicely plated meals, while the consumers want it to see 
the meal that they are purchasing, without having to open the 
lid. This should rather be incorporated in the lid, as the food 
looks nicer from above. Polypropylene plastic (PP) is chosen 
as the material for this part, due to its strength and endur-
ance, which is necessary when incorporating functional ele-
ments such as the snaps. A summary of polypropylene plastic 
is shown in Table 09.4.1 (Polypropylen, PP-Plast | Det Store 
Plastleksikon, n.d.).

10.1 Introduction 
After choosing the concept for the service and the container 
(see Figure 09.2.1 and Figure 09.3.1 as well as the discussion 
and arguments for choosing presented in the previous chap-
ter), a further definition of the final product-service system is 
needed, before detailing the service platforms and container 
further. Thus, this chapter seeks to explore and define all ele-
ments that feed into the final product-service system solution 
ROTINE. 
The solution is defined through an updated description of the 
business model canvas and value proposition, which is also 
evaluated in relation to behavioural design strategies used. 
Furthermore the service blueprint model is updated from the 
previous version made (see Appendix A07.10 Swimlane Dia-
gram, Greta Thunberg) to define and gain a final overview of 
the details of the system. 
Finally the PSS Morphology was used to define the PSS from 
a high level perspective. To summarise the final definition of 
the product-service system, this is described using Mont’s 
Model. 
10.2 The Updated BMC and Val-
ue Proposition
The initial Greta Thunberg concept was updated in a new 
Business Model Canvas (see Figure 10.2.1). 
The most notable changes to the BMC are found in the cost 
structure, which was updated based on a thorough discussion 
with KK’s CEO. Here, where the following business elements 
decided during the discussion: 
•	
The canteens should pay a subscription fee 
•	
The consumers have to pay 120 DKK for a set of con-
tainers (at least two containers are required for a meal 
including e.g. a hot dish and a salad) in order to enter the 
system. 
•	
The amount of containers going out of the system has to 
be at least the same as the amount of containers going 
into the system, in order to sustain the flow and amount 
of containers at the canteen. The consumers can return 
their used set of containers whenever they pick up a new 
set of meals, but will have to return it before picking up 
the new meal. 
•	
If the consumer forgets to bring their meal or does not 
own enough containers for the amount of meals ordered, 
they will have the option to rent a container for 10 DKK. 
However, the container(s) have to be returned within 14 
days for them to avoid buying out the container for the 
full price.  
Additionally, two value propositions were described based on 
the elements in the BMC. The product-service system prom-
ises the following values to respectively the canteen and the 
consumers. 
ROTINE helps canteens who want to be sustainable frontrun-
ners and mitigate upcoming packaging legislation, by provid-
ing a system for selling takeaway in reusable packaging that 
elevates the gourmet food experience. Furthermore ROTINE 
provides an efficient platform that makes it easy for canteens 
to communicate to customers and manage orders.
ROTINE helps consumers at Kokkenes Køkken who want to 
treat themselves with high quality takeaway meals, without 
feeling guilty of unsustainable disposable packaging. This is 
done by offering a convenient reusable packaging system that 
makes it easy to make a sustainable habit in the busy everyday 
life.
10.3 Behavioural Strategies 
Used
The defined product-service solution uses different be-
havioural design elements and strategies.
Decisive intervention model
When implementing the ROTINE system in a canteen, this 
will be the only option, as single-use packaging should be 
100% replaced. Therefore a decisive intervention model is 
used. However, as seen in the case of Edison huset, some 
canteens implementing the ROTINE system will have no 
well-established takeaway culture. The goal of the solution is 
therefore also to get even more users to buy takeaway from 
their canteen. Therefore the three factors (motivations, abili-
ties and triggers) influencing whether users will be a part of 
the system or not has been carefully considered. 
Persuasive intervention model
Generally the solution is targeting the conscious state of 
mind, by positively encouraging users through an informa-
tional strategy. The users are persuaded to use the system by 
informing them about the sustainable benefits. Furthermore 
the sustainable tracking in the app and website will continu-
ously inform the users of the environmental performance of 
the ROTINE system compared to single-use packaging, en-
suring continuous persuasive interventions. 
==PAGE:135==
127
10/ Defining the Final Solution
Motivations, abilities and triggers
The sustainable tracking intends to motivate the users, while 
also the functional and aesthetical design of the container 
aims to create a pleasurable experience for the users. 
Furthermore the system aims to make it simple for users to 
enter the system, thus heightening the ability of using the sys-
tem. The time, physical effort and brain cycles it takes to use 
the system and return the container is very simple, as the users 
can easily bring the containers along any day at work, making 
it a part of their daily routine. 
When users have first entered the system, triggers are used to 
e.g. remind them when their favourite meal is on the menu. 
Furthermore notifications via the app are used to remind users 
to bring their containers when they have purchased an order 
or have rented containers.
Elements from a coercive intervention model
Users buy into the system by purchasing a set of containers, 
which they can keep until their next order. The up-front cost 
might be intimidating, however users found it more simple to 
make a one time purchase than paying for a continuous sub-
scription fee. This business model was chosen also, as it was 
found that users do not like coercive business models, where 
they will be punished for not bringing back the container in 
time (see Section 08.4). 
However some tradeoffs needed to be made in terms of the 
need for a more structural element of the business model. If 
users forget to return the container before picking up a new 
order, they need to have a simple alternative. Therefore the 
users have the option of renting a container for 10 DKK, (this 
is a symbolic price that will also remind the users to bring 
their own containers to avoid paying the fee again). To ensure 
that users return these containers back into the system a co-
ercive model is therefore used, “punishing” users by making 
them purchase the containers if not returned within 14 days. 
10.4 Updated Service Blueprint 
The journey of the service recipients (consumers and canteen 
employees) and the related service operations are mapped out 
in an updated version of the Greta Thunberg Service Blue-
print (see Figure 10.4.1 and 10.4.2). The blueprints were 
mapped out to cover the two main aspects of the user journey: 
(1) signing up and ordering a meal as a new customer and (2) 
returning the container and collecting a new takeaway meal. 
Consumers need the option to pick up their meal and return 
their used containers at all times and not only within the work-
ing hours of the canteen staff (from around 06:00 - 14:00). 
Furthermore robust technology needs to be implemented to 
track containers within the system (as found in the evaluation 
of the MVP test, see Section 08.2). Therefore it is decided to 
implement technology, which complies with the internet of 
things (IoT). This includes a smart fridge for storage of ready 
made meals and an identification chip moulded into all con-
tainers. To open the smart fridge, consumers scan the NFC tag 
on the fridge via their ROTINE app, ensuring that the system 
recognises them as a user and ensuring that they have ordered 
takeaway. The containers are returned to the system by scan-
ning the container at the return stations, thus tracking the con-
tainers and ensuring that they do not go missing. 
The communication protocol for opening the smart fridge 
was initially based on the company identification cards (RFID 
technology) of the consumer, with the assumption that all em-
ployees would have this in order to access the company build-
ing. However, after a feedback session with KK it was made 
clear that company ID cards are not necessarily used in all of 
their canteens. Therefore, it was necessary to explore other 
solutions when consumers used or returned a container. Both 
Bluetooth and NFC chip technology were investigated to get 
a further understanding of their differences. The research 
showed that the NFC technology would be more appropriate 
for the system as it is more secure and in a higher quality 
when sending small data packages on short distances (“NFC 
vs. Bluetooth: What are the 5 Key Differences?,” n.d.).

in Figure 10.5.1.

paring the strategic characteristics with the eight archetypical 
PSS business models described by Tukker (2004). Here, the 
comparison shows that the developed product-service system 
is a combination of all three categories (product-,  use- and 
result-oriented) as the solution caters for both the canteen and 
the consumers as listed below:

impression is crucial for making the PSS work. 
Regulatory
The product-service system has to cater for two types of 
regulatory requirements. Firstly, there are the national and 
international incentives to reduce waste such as the Circular 
Economy Action Plan as introduced in the /01 Introduction 
chapter. The canteens need to accommodate for the packaging 
responsibility that is to come sooner or later before it becomes 
a regulatory requirement. 
Secondly, the takeaway system needs to oblige with the food 
safety regulations by e.g. using food approved materials.
Cognitive
The PSS provides a platform for the canteens to handle all 
elements of managing takeaway orders in a gathered solution. 
This makes it easier to create and share the menu with the 
consumers as well as easily gain an overview of the ordered 
meals. For the consumers the PSS provides a simple way of 
ordering takeaway meals from the canteen. With the consum-
er platform is it also possible to create a habitual takeaway 
pattern. 
Normative
Normative settings which the PSS hope to create are con-
sumer habits like rinsing the container before returning it, or 
cleaning it if it is not swapped for a new one within the first 
days after use. Furthermore, norms such as only picking up 
the amount of meals purchased are expected.   
Customer satisfaction
Customer satisfaction is achieved through the design of the 
product, which fulfills user requirements such as aesthetics 
and functionality.
The platform facilitates ordering of takeaway, making it easier 
to purchase takeaway through their canteen. Furthermore, the 
platform intends to motivate both consumers and employees 
by continuously tracking the amount of resources and CO2 
emissions saved. 
Business viability
The business viability is ensured as the subscription fee for 
using the ROTINE system is cheaper in the long run com-
pared to single-use packaging. By subscribing to the ROTINE 
system, canteens ensure to meet future legislation on packag-
ing. Furthermore the service facilitates the implementation of 
a new business model for canteens, that have not yet found a 
way for selling takeaway. 
as a supplement to their already purchased set of containers 
(use-oriented)
The canteen pays for the predefined result of providing con-
tainers and a service platform to sell takeaway meals in  (re-
sult-oriented)
The product-service system is of a complex nature as it con-
sists of multiple solutions to substitute the reference system: 
single-use packaging. However, it brings a lot of value to oth-
er  aspects such as introducing a takeaway service platform. 
10.6 Summary // Mont’s Model
The definition of the final solution is summarized through 
Mont’s Product-Service Systems Framework. The design, 
environmental and institutional features of the PSS are de-
scribed in through the 10 elements (Mont, 2007). This is done 
in order to ensure that all parts of the PSS have been taken 
into account (see Appendix A10.01).
Product
The products that are included in ROTINE are containers with 
ID chips, smart fridges and scanners for returning containers. 
Service
The services include a digital platform (website and app) 
where the canteens can manage their orders and the consum-
ers can place their orders as well as a return system based 
on IoT to distribute the information flow going through the 
NFC-reader and the containers ID chips. 
Continuous improvements of the platforms are required for 
the PSS to work fully functioning. The services also include 
an initial distribution of the containers along with a continu-
ous operations and replacement management, in case the con-
tainers break. 
Infrastructure
The required infrastructure for canteens are: 
•	
Industrial kitchen and cleaning facilities to clean the 
containers
•	
Storage space for storing the containers when they are 
not in use
•	
Computer to access the canteen service platform
The required infrastructure for consumers is: 
•	
Smartphone to enable access to the smart fridge
Actor-network
The actor-network consists of Kokkenes Køkken and their 
canteen staff and the end-consumers. Establishing a good first 
==PAGE:145==
137
10/ Defining the Final Solution
Environmental soundness
The container is designed for emotional durability and to-
gether with a service system designed with a focus on us-
ers, this creates an incentive to use the containers again and 
again. This way resource loops are prolonged, and ultimately 
resources used for single-use packaging are saved. Materials 
used are recyclable.

11.1 Introduction
After defining all the system elements of the PSS, the most 
important elements of ROTINE are detailed;  the two service 
platforms (app and webpage) as well as the container. During 
this phase a feedback session with the three stakeholders Maj, 
Rune and Asmus from KK was held to ensure that the right 
choices for both the service platforms as well as the shape of 
the container was made. 
The final solution is presented in a Solution Catalogue (which 
is a separate document). 
11.2 Development of Final Sys-
tem Platform
The development of the system platform was based on the 
decisions made for the final system concept (see Section 09 
Choosing the Final Solution). The final platform is therefore 
based on the Greta Thunberg concept with elements from 
Community. Previously mock-ups for the consumer app have 
been made in order to test the three system concepts in the 
generative workshop, these have served as inspiration for the 
final app for the consumers (see Concept Catalogue). The 
website serving as an internal platform for the canteen needed 
to be developed from scratch.
User stories and user story map
First step in developing the two platforms was to map out 
the essential user stories for each of the two user segments 
(consumers and canteens). The user stories is an agile tool 
(see Section 03.2) that helped specify what goals the two 
user groups wanted to achieve by using the platforms. The 
user stories are based on all the research and testing that has 
been done prior to developing the final concept. An overview 
of user stories can be seen in Table 11.2.1 and 11.2.2 below 
along with references to relevant research for each story.
 
The user stories made the basis for user story maps to be ap-
plied for the consumer app (see Appendix A11.01) and the 
canteen’s internal website (see Appendix 11.02). The user 
story maps have three layers. The top of the map represents 
the goal of the consumers or canteens, which is based on the 
user stories. The second layer consists of the activities the 
platform should perform in order to fulfill the goal. The last 
layer is the task layer, which breaks down the activities into 
concrete tasks which have to be performed by either the user 
of the platform or the background system. The tasks form the 
basis for developing wireframes for the system, as they form a 
concrete list of the actions that the system has to support. The 
user story maps show a comprehensive view of all the func-
tionalities of each platform. Wireframes have been developed 
in selected parts of the system in order to provide a sense of 
the functionality and experience of using the app and website 
(see Figure 11.2.1-11.2.3 and Appendix A11.03 & A11.04).
 
Feedback session with Kokkenes Køkken
Mockup wireframes were shown to Maj, Asmus and Rune 
from Kokkenes Køkken as a part of a feedback session, where 
both initial wireframes illustrating the canteen platform and 
prototypes for the final container concept were presented. The 
presentation material can be found in Appendix B11.01. An 
overview of the feedback provided can be seen below.
Feedback from KK
General feedback
	
●
The three representatives from Kokkenes Køk-
ken were generally excited about the functional-
ities the new system provides for them.
	
●
The platform encourages the chefs to continu-
ously update the system, as they create the menu 
within the system. Rune pointed out that this is 
probably much more effective than the current 
systems, where it is up to the reception to update 
the takeaway menu (which is often forgotten).
Features feedback
	
●
Having the option to add a “leftover” meal (if 
there is more food than expected left over from 
lunch) is something that Kokkenes Køkken was 
really excited about, as it can help prevent food 
waste and further enhance sustainable branding.
	
●
Using the platform as a tool for creating and 
sharing menus is really valuable. Creating a 
shared library with recipes across the canteens 
in Kokkenes Køkken is very useful, especially 
if the recipes are searchable. Rune pointed out 
that it would be great if recipes can be searched 
based on which ingredients are in season. The 
recipes should be tagged with a month, as what 
is in season changes on a monthly basis.
	
●
Maj noted that future updates of the platform 
could include showing CO2 footprint for each 
dish on the menu, as this is information that the 
canteen will be interested in displaying to the 
consumers in the future.

Kokkenes Køkken wants to provide a convenient takeaway 
system for their customers (important gain of new system in 
the value proposition for the consumers)
As a consumer I want to be inspired by what the canteen has 
to offer in order to keep my interest in the system
See Section 08.4 (Enacting, Telling, Making)
See Appendix 08.04 Feedback on Community Life

seeing a lot of pictures of menu items.

so I can make sure that I have what I need and avoid any 
unforeseen fees
See Section 08.4 (Enacting, Telling, Making)
It became very clear after the enactment workshop that the 
participants wanted to be informed about exactly what they 
would be paying for and that they need to be informed about 
how the renting system works up front.

11.3 Development of Final Con-
tainer
The detailing of the container was based on the set of specifi-
cations made when choosing the final container (see Section 
09.3 and 09.4), see Figure 11.3.1. As the organic shape of the 
container had not been further specified, this was explored be-
fore choosing a final shape to detail. Mock-ups and drawings 
were brought to a the feedback session with three stakehold-
ers from Kokkenes Køkken, to choose the final shape before 
drawing this in CAD-software. 
Exploration of the Organic Shape 
The organic shape was further explored to ensure the right 
aesthetical look as well as the right functionality of the final 
container. 
First sketches of the outer shape were drawn in 2D (see Fig-
ure 11.3.2) with suggestions to how the elevational part might 
look (dashed contours). Different inspirational pictures were 
found to support the exploration (see Appendix A11.05). Af-
ter an internal voting session within the group, 8 shapes were 
selected. 
These were quickly mocked-up by laser cutting in cardboard. 
Some of the shapes were detailed in relation to how the ele-
vation might look, but as this took a long time to mock-up, it 
was decided to buy some playdough instead, which could be 
Top incl. snaps: Transparent PP
Bottom: Kareline (PP and ﬁberwood)
Silicon rubber sealing
Elevation
Organic shape 
Stackable top and bottom
Figure 11.3.1
Production
Production of the container was discussed with a con-
sulent from Dutec Plast A/S. Here it was confirmed 
that the snaps in the container should be moulded to-
gether with the lid in the same material and colour, 
as this is the smartest and cheapest way. Injecting the 
snaps and lid in one component is cheaper as only one 
mould is needed and no assembly is required. Further-
more it is assumed that the component will be less 
likely to break, as there will be no joints. It is possi-
ble to inject more than one colour or material for one 
component, however this would cost 20-40% more 
per component. 
Furthermore the possibility and price of injecting a 
chip into one of the components was discussed; if us-
ing the right setup and chip, this should not be too 
expensive (Dutec has robots that can easily handle 
this). The consultant referred to the following page 
for finding the right chip (Prosign.dk, n.d.)
The thickness of the container was discussed with the 
consultant as well, as the group was in doubt whether 
2, 2,5 or 3 mm would be best. The consultant advised 
using 3 mm, as this will feel more exclusive than 2 
mm, and the roundings/fillets would be better.

Drawing the Final Container in CAD
After choosing the right shape for the container, the process 
of drawing this in CAD software began. First the ratio of the 
three circles were calculated to ensure the right ratio of the 
two compartments, as well as estimating the right diameters 
to end up with a volume between 750-800 mL, with a height 
of 50 mm (see Table 11.3.1). This method was mostly used 
as a starting point, as more or less volume would be added 
between the circles. Furthermore, a draft and fillet as well as 
the elevation would be added to the model, also influencing 
the volume. 
 After estimating the sizes the drawing began in CAD. First 
the ground shape was drawn and extruded with a 13 degrees 
draft and a fillet in the bottom was added, whereafter the mass 
volume of the solid body was calculated by the software. The 
size was iteratively adjusted until the volume was closer to 
800 mL (the volume should be slightly bigger, as some of 
the volume would go as material). The final measures of the

12.1 Introduction
This Section seeks to evaluate the final PSS solution ROTINE 
(see Section 10/ and the Solution Catalogue). First the busi-
ness viability is evaluated through assessing the total cost of 
ownership (TCO) as well as the return of investment. After-
wards an environmental evaluation of the final solution is 
done through the CE framework and an sLCA. Evaluating the 
PSS further, an analysis of the potential rebound effects asso-
ciated with incorporating the PSS at KK is completed. Lastly, 
the three step roadmap is detailed into an implementation plan 
that includes further work as well as potential barriers which 
may occur in the future development of the PSS.
12.2 TCO and Return of Invest-
ment
Total Cost of Ownership (TCO) calculations were conducted 
in order to get an overview of the costs and cost structure 
for developing the system and producing containers. The 
TCOs were furthermore used for estimating the feasibility of 
the business model, comparing the system to costs related to 
a single-use packaging system and identifying the return of 
investment point. All calculations can be found in Appendix 
B12.01 - TCO excel sheet.
 
TCO for four scenarios
It is assumed that members of the system will buy takeaway 
twice a week on average throughout the year. 
Every customer will buy a set of two containers for 120 DKK 
to get access to the system (as described in the final business 
model, see Section 10.2). To keep the system running, includ-
ing having containers to swap and rent, 1.5 times the amount 
of containers that have been bought by customers is assumed 
to be necessary for the canteens to have in stock. E.g. If 50 
customers have bought a set of containers (100 containers), 
the canteen will have 150 containers, meaning that 250 con-
tainers are needed in the system in total. All calculations are 
based on the scenarios investigated in the market size calcu-
lations in Section 4.6.
 
The scenarios are (see Section 4.6): 
1.	
Edison huset (50 customers) 
2.	
10% of customers from Kokkenes Køkken (783 
customers)
3.	
10% of frequent customers in canteens  (78,315 
customers)
4.	
10% of frequent takeaway customers (65,349 
customers)
Note that scenario 3 has a slightly larger amount of custom-
ers than scenario 4, which shows that reaching 10% of the 
canteens market (step 2 in the three step roadmap) is actually 
slightly more profitable than reaching 10% of the restaurant 
takeaway market (step 3 in the three step roadmap). Reaching 
both markets is of course still desirable.
The system is evaluated on an 8-year basis. After 8 years it is 
assumed that most of the ROTINE boxes will have to be re-
placed, as they will have gone through close to 500 use cycles 
Figure 12.2.1– TCO for one ROTINE box in scenario 2 after 8 years.
==PAGE:165==
157
12/ Evaluating the Final Solution
(see Section 09.4 Design Specification v3.0).
 
The Total Cost of Ownership (TCO) calculations are based 
on assumptions, guidance from experts and estimates (see 
assumptions in Appendix 12.01). The included costs of the 
TCO are primarily upfront investments for ROTINE, such 
as production of moulds for ROTINE box (both bottom and 
top), production of containers, and development of app, web-
site and IoT integration. Furthermore, the recurring costs will 
include maintenance of the service platforms etc., marketing, 
and spare parts. The distribution of containers will be man-
aged by Kokkenes Køkken, and is therefore not a part of the 
TCO. The TCO for scenario 2 can be viewed in Figure 12.2.1, 
where the total cost of ownership is calculated to 728 DKK 
per box for 8 years (the TCO for scenario 3 and 4 are respec-
tively 66 DKK and 67 DKK. ).
 The largest cost associated with the ROTINE box is primarily 
the development and maintenance of the app (where the first 
version is assumed to cost 500 000 DKK to develop, followed 
by a total of 100 000 DKK for continuous maintenance during 
the 8 years). However, this will only be the case for scenarios 
where few ROTINE boxes are produced, as the total cost of 
producing the containers will become the highest expense for 
the bigger markets. TCO, cost and earnings overview of the 
other scenarios can be found in Appendix 12.02, page 1-4.
 
TCO of ROTINE box compared to single-use packaging
The TCO of the ROTINE box can be compared to the cost 
of single-use packaging. This is done by calculating the to-
tal price of the single-use containers that would be used per 
member during eight years. As it is assumed that each member 
will buy takeaway two times a week on average the amount 
of single-use containers needed would be 832 for eight years, 
which would cost almost 5000 DKK (as one container con-
taining both lid and bottom costs 6 DKK (according to the 
CEO of KK)). The price per single-use container will not 
change in relation to the number of consumers..
 
The TCO of a ROTINE container is on the other hand depen-
dent on the amount of members in the system. Table 12.2.1 
below clearly shows that the TCO of one box is very, very 
high when there are few members in the system (scenario 1). 
This is due to the high fixed costs for the app development, 
tools for production, infrastructure, etc. (as seen in the pre-
vious Section). The TCO drops significantly when there are 
more members and boxes in the system, as the high fixed 
costs will be split between a lot of containers. The TCO of 
the container is multiplied by 2.5, in order to make a fair com-
parison between single-use containers and the ROTINE box 
(as it was assumed that 1.5 extra containers are needed in the 
system for every container purchased by a customer).
Table 12.2.1 shows that the TCO is higher for the ROTINE 
system compared to single-use packaging when there are very 
few members of the system. This changes dramatically when 
the system expands, as the TCO for each container becomes 
much lower and is now favourable compared to the cost of 
single-use. The difference between the two systems is most 
significant in scenario 3 and 4, where the TCO of the RO-
TINE box times 2.5 amounts to less than 5% of the total cost 
of single-use containers.
Revenue and return of investment
The income in the ROTINE system comes from containers 
sold directly to members of the system and the subscription 
paid on a monthly basis from canteens (or restaurants selling

biggest part of the income by far is made on the subscription 
paid by the canteens/restaurants. This trend can be seen on 
Figure 12.2.2 below, where the total revenue for the system in 
the course of eight years can be seen for scenario 2.
Figure 12.2.2 clearly shows that the income from containers 
is insignificant compared to the income from subscriptions.
 
The TCO and other calculations presented in this Section 
largely supports the initial conclusions from the market size 
calculations found in Section 4.7. Selling the product service 
system to a very limited customer group, e.g. focusing on 
only one canteen, is not a viable business. This is especial-
ly due to the high costs associated with developing, updating 
and maintaining the app platform for the customers. A recom-
mendation based on these calculations is therefore to make 
a much simpler setup when launching the system to a very 
limited market. This could be done by developing a website 
instead of an app to the customers, as this is much cheaper.
 As expected from the market size calculations the biggest 
source of income over time is through the subscription paid 
by the canteens rather than the sale of containers.
What the ROTINE box should cost can be discussed, especial-
ly as the price of the container is not an essential part of the 
income structure. The psychological and behavioural consid-
erations regarding the price of the container are important to 
consider in this case. The 120 DKK for a set of two ROTINE 
boxes might be a barrier for entering the system for some con-
sumers. On the other hand, a very cheap price might make 
consumers perceive the container as a low quality product, 
and consumers might be less likely to treat the box with care 
or to remember to turn in borrowed boxes. Thus, too cheap a 
price goes against the wish of achieving emotional durability 
(see Section 12.3).  
takeaway). Any income from renting containers has not been 
accounted for in the following calculations, as it is difficult 
to estimate the amount of container rented and as the income 
from renting containers is very small (10 DKK per container). 
As stated earlier members pay 120 DKK for two containers 
when entering the system.
 
The reference system with single-use packaging was used to 
estimate how much the monthly subscription should cost for 
the canteens and takeaway restaurants. Two containers and 
two lids are always used per takeaway meal, resulting in a 
single-use packaging cost of ≈12 DKK per meal (according 
to the CEO of KK). The monthly subscription for each can-
teen is set to a bit less than what the canteen would spend on 
single-use packaging if each member of the system bought a 
takeaway meal two times a week. 
It should be noted that the average number of customers in 
a canteen is estimated to be 300 people, resulting in around 
30 members of the system in each canteen (assuming 10% of 
customers are willing to partake in the system). As there are 
only 30 members in each canteen the subscription price per 
canteen is lower than in Edison Huset, where it is assumed 
that 50 people are willing to partake in a pilot test. 
 
Table 12.2.2 shows that if the ROTINE system is only used by 
a small number of members, the business is not viable, as the 
income will never exceed the expenses. The return of invest-
ment will happen after two years if the system is expanded to 
all of Kokkenes Køkken and 10% are willing to participate 
in the system. If the system is successfully expanded to all of 
the canteen market or the takeaway market the return of in-
vestment will happen after one year. The table shows that the

12/ Evaluating the Final Solution
The price point has to be investigated in the future work in or-
der to determine when the price is low enough for consumers 
to enter the system but high enough to make them treat the 
box with care and make sure they return used boxes.
Now the business viability of the final solution has been in-
vestigated and important insights have been found. The Sec-
tions to come will deep dive into the environmental viability 
and profile of the final solution and highlight potential barriers 
to overcome.
12.3 Circular Economy 
The framework described by Bocken et al. (2016) can be used 
to evaluate the final container and system concept on how 
well it fits into a circular economy as described in the liter-
ature study in  Section 02.2. Bocken et al. describes design 
and business strategies for narrowing, closing and slowing 
resource loops in order to transition from a linear to a circular 
economy (see Section 02.2). The main design and business 
strategies which have been applied in the development of the 
container and system design are listed below. 
Design strategies for the container
The main strategy to slow the resource loop for the container 
is designing long-life products, as the container is designed 
for attachment and trust (Bocken et al., 2016). The design of 
the container has been carefully considered, ensuring that user 
Figure 12.2.2 – Revenue for scenario 2 based on income from selling containers 
and subscriptions (red) and only selling subscriptions (green).
TCO
The TCO is based mostly on assumptions, which acts 
as a potential source of error. The production price per 
container should also be discussed as production pric-
es are reduced when a higher amount of units/items is 
produced.
The calculations are based on the assumption that each 
member of the system will buy takeaway two times a 
week on average. This estimate might be a bit high. If 
members buy takeaway less than two times a week the 
TCO per ROTINE box will be less favourable compared 
to single-use packaging, as the amount of single-use 
containers it replaces becomes fewer. The ROTINE box 
will, however, still be cheaper compared to single-use 
packaging for scenario 2, 3 and 4 even if members of 
the system buy takeaway only once a week on average. 
All in all, the TCO provides a good overview of how 
many participants would be necessary to run a healthy 
business. However, it is important to keep in mind that 
it was based on a lot of assumptions. There will defi-
nitely be more costs related to implementing the prod-
uct-service system, which would have to be elaborated 
in further work by getting offers on the different costs. 
==PAGE:168==
160
will therefore own the access to two containers, but the exact 
containers will be switched out with each use.
 
The ROTINE system provides many of the benefits associat-
ed with the Access and performance model as described by 
Bocken et al. (2016). The consumers can enjoy a meal from a 
reusable container without needing to wash it or even store it 
(as they can hand it in at the canteen for later use). If the con-
tainer breaks or has become too worn they can simply hand it 
in and get a new container.
 
The business model provides an incentive for producing con-
tainers that will last a long time, as the longer the container 
lasts the better the revenue. It is therefore also in the interest 
of the business to repair broken parts of the container (e.g. the 
rubber ring) in order to extend the use of the container. There 
is also a financial incentive for collecting worn or broken box-
es in order to remanufacture them.
The circular economy strategies applied in the development 
of the container and system provide a high level characteri-
sation of sustainability considerations. The following Section 
will dive deeper into the environmental impact of the system 
by conducting an sLCA. 
12.4 sLCA
A simple Life Cycle Assessment (sLCA) based on the litera-
ture from Section 02.2 is conducted in this Section with the 
objective of comparing the developed PSS with the existing 
solution of single-use packaging in Edison Huset (also re-
ferred to as the reference system). The assessment is scoped 
as a PSS Consequence, with the focus on analysing the envi-
ronmental changes caused by introducing a PSS to the refer-
ence system (see Section 02.3). Furthermore, the study focus-
es on assessing the CO2 emissions saved, as the developed 
PSS tries to extend the use of the product and material (Kjaer 
et.al., 2017). 
The sLCA is made based on elements from the seven step ap-
proach as described by McAloone and Bey (2008). The seven 
step approach has been adjusted to only include the steps rel-
evant to supplement the assessment. The following four steps 
are included and applied in the described order: (1) describing 
the  use context, (2) gathering an overview of the environ-
mental impact, (3) identifying hotspots and (5) quantifying 
the environmental impacts. 
Detailed identification of the product to be analysed
needs are satisfied creating emotional durability through its 
functionality, aesthetics, and uniqueness. Furthermore, the 
choice of material for the bottom part of the container (Kare-
line) is, among other reasons, chosen to distinguish the con-
tainer from regular plastic products which were found per-
ceived as cheap and unsustainable (see Section 6.9 Affinity 
Diagram).
The design for attachment and trust has been guide by the 
explorations in experience design conducted through the ViP 
design method in Section 07.5. Elements from the exploration 
have been incorporated in the final design, such as organic 
shapes to ensure a pleasant interaction with the container. The 
wow effect of opening the container has been achieved by in-
troducing the elevation in the bottom of the container, which 
makes it possible for the canteen to place different elements of 
the meal nicely and creates an unexpected element. 
Furthermore, the container is designed for disassembly to pro-
long the use phase. The most vulnerable part of the container 
is assumed to be the rubber ring inserted in the lid, which 
could either break or become unhygienic after many uses. The 
rubber ring can therefore easily be removed from the lid, as it 
is secured by a press fit with no glue or other adhesive. As the 
containers are handed in at the canteen regularly the canteen 
personnel will be able to spot any rubber ring that needs re-
placing and do so without having to discard the lid. 
The Kareline material used for the bottom of the container is 
a great choice for closing resource loops, as the containers can 
be collected, remelted and used for producing new contain-
ers. Old containers can be collected directly from the canteens 
(who will send the containers back when they discover that 
they are too worn to continue in the system). As all contain-
ers are made out of Kareline the continuous recycling of the 
material can continue in a closed loop, ensuring a high quality 
primary recycling (Bocken et al., 2016). A closed recycling 
system as a part of a PSS is in fact a prerequisite for recycling 
Kareline, as it can not be recycled in the conventional system. 
Circular business strategies
As stated in Section 02.2 (CE literature) the most relevant 
business model is the Access and performance model, which 
is basically the CE term for a PSS (Bocken et al., 2016). The 
business model for the ROTINE system is in line with this 
model, as consumers are buying access to the use of the RO-
TINE boxes. In the description of the system it is stated that 
consumers buy two containers when entering the system. 
Though it is true that they pay for two containers, the real 
value of participating in the system is not from the transfer of 
ownership when purchasing the boxes, but rather by swapping 
the boxes when purchasing the takeaway meal. The customers

reference flow for the reusable container does not include a 
rotation of the containers in the PSS. For this 11 reusable con-
tainers are required for the PSS to fully function, assuming 
that the reusable containers are returned the day after they 
have been used. 
(2) Gathering an overview of the environmental impact and 
(3) identifying hotspots
An overview of the product life cycle of the single-use pack-
aging is made to properly understand the significant envi-
ronmental impacts of the product, through a MECO-matrix, 
identifying the most hazardous environmental impacts in the 
four categories of Materials, Energy, Chemicals and Other 
(see Figure 12.4.1).  
Three hotspots identified for single-use packaging are relat-
ed to material extraction, energy used on extracting materials 
and disposal of materials. These three areas are not affected 
nearly as much for the product life cycle of the ROTINE box, 
as the lifespan is substantially  prolonged.
In contrast, the Rotone box affects the use phase, as it requires 
energy and water consumption to  continuously clean the con-
tainer. However, it can be argued that the created impact does 
not have a high enough effect to even out the three hotspots 
The objects of the sLCA study are the single-use packaging 
and the reusable container that are to be compared through-
out the product life cycle from cradle to grave. Their mate-
rial properties are listed in table 12.4.1 below. Note, that it 
was not possible to find all specific material properties in the 
GRANTA Edupack tool used for modelling the sLCA. Minor 
adjustments have therefore been made in order to conduct the 
assessment. This must be taken into account when concluding 
on the results. 
(1) Describing  the use context
The use context is described by a functional unit in order to 
gain a common reference frame,  which can be used to com-
pare the developed PSS with the single-use packaging. The 
obligatory properties of being able to contain and seal 750 mL 
has been the basis of the functional unit stated below.
Container of 750 mL meal for consumption 5 days a 
week for 8 years. The container must be reclosable and 
appropriate for transportation.
The reference flow for the single-use packaging is 2,080 
bottoms and lids, while the reference flow for the reusable 
containers are 4.16 bottoms, lids and silicone sealing (when 
assuming all three parts have the same lifespan). See calcu-

The second comparison investigates the environmental impact 
of the two systems in relation to the functional unit and refer-

ing in the product system is better than the reusable container 
in the product-service system. The calculation of the water 
and energy consumption for washing the reusable containers 
in a dishwasher are assumed to run once a week (assuming 
that 5 containers can fit onto one industrial dishwasher tray) 
for three minutes for 8 years. In addition, it is assumed that 
the consumers use 1 liter of water per used container to rinse 
it before returning.

comparisons.  
Results
Results from the first comparison shows that one reusable 
container has a carbon footprint of 0.84 kg, while one sin-
gle-use packaging has a carbon footprint of 0.148 kg. This 
identified for the single-use packaging. 
(5) Quantifying the environmental impacts
The simple life cycle assessment is modelled in the GRANTA 
Edupack tool. See Appendix 12.04 and 12.05 to view the cho-
sen materials, manufacturing process and End of Life (EoL) 
potential in the software programme for both containers. Note, 
that all chosen materials are virgin materials, meaning that re-
cycled materials are not included in this assessment. Further-
more, the single-use packaging is at EoL sent to combustion 
to follow the Danish recycling guidelines (materials polluted 
with food that cannot be washed off should not be recycled). 
And lastly, the numbers used in the transportation phase are 
based on rough estimates by looking at the distance between 
the different production sites, as the assessment is solemnly a 
simple life cycle analysis. View Appendix A12.06 to gain an 
overview of the assumptions made to model the sLCA.
The single-use packaging has been compared to the reusable 
container in two ways. The first comparison investigates the 
amount of CO2 emission contributed by each container, with 
focus on finding the amount of times the reusable contain-
er has to be used in order to break even with the single-use
