means that the CO2 emission of one reusable container is 
equal to around 6 single-use packaging (see Figure 12.4.1). 
In other words, the consumer would have to use the reusable 
container 6 times in order to break even with the analysed 
single-use packaging system. These results are based on not 
including the EoL potential. The reusable container will be 
equal to only two single-use packages if the EoL is included. 
See all calculations in Appendix B12.02 and the associated 
GRANTA Edupack evaluations in Appendix B12.03. This 
number needs to be further investigated in a real Life Cycle 
Analysis, as it seems a bit too optimistic that the EoL can in-
fluence the results that drastically. 
Results from the second comparison shows that the reusable 
containers in the PSS have a carbon footprint of 50.7 kg, while 
the single-use packages have a carbon footprint of 293.3 kg 
without including EoL Appendix B12.02 - sLCA. This means 
that the single-use packages have a 5.8 times higher impact 
on the CO2 footprint compared to the PSS. The impact dif-
ference increases to 10.8 when including EoL, as the bottom 
and lid in the reusable container are sent to recycling and the 
single-use bottom and lid to combustion (see Figure 12.4.2). 
Note that 2.080 single-use containers are required to fulfil the 
functional unit, while only 11 reusable containers in the PSS 
are required, as described in the reference flow. Furthermore, 
the saved amount of CO2 emission will only increase propor-
tionally with the amount of orders per week. Meaning that if 
there were 50 orders a week, it would require 110 reusable 
containers vs 20800 single-use containers which corresponds 
to CO2 savings which are around 10 times higher.
12.5 Rebound effects 
After looking at the positive environmental impact of the 
sLCA, a brief assessment of negative unintended environ-
mental impacts (Rebound Effects) that might occur is made. 
As mentioned in Section 02.3 these impacts might occur due 
to either behavioral or systemic responses of changing the 
takeaway system from single-use packaging to a reusable 
PSS (Kjaer, Pigosso, & McAloone, 2017). The rebound ef-
fects highlight the impacts related to the implementation of 
the system for the economic-, social-, and environmental 
sustainability. The assessment of the impacts can be seen on 
Figure 12.5.1.
Economic impact
It is expected that the implementation of the ROTINE box 
system will have a positive impact on the economy for can-
teens, as using the ROTINE system will be cheaper in the 
sLCA
The conducted sLCA is mainly based on assumptions 
in the phases of manufacturing, transport, use, disposal 
and EoL potential. This gives a certain degree of uncer-
tainty to the results presented e.g. it is assumed that the 
reusable containers can endure a use cycle of 500 times, 
based on the requirement made in the Design Specifica-
tion v3.0. The reusable container can most likely endure 
more use cycles (in comparison Kleenhub states that 
their containers can endure 1000 use cycles (Kleen Hub 
| Smart Coffee Cups and Food Boxes, n.d.)).
Furthermore, the EoL potential has a high influence on 
the amount of single-use packaging required to break 
even with the reusable container. The EoL potential 
should therefore be blackboxed in this sLCA, as the 
processes in EoL most likely include some sources of 
error.
In addition, the calculations in the sLCA are only made 
for one container and not a set of two, as the PSS solu-
tion actually consists of. This is reasoned by the fact 
that the same amount of containers (reusable or not) are 
required for the same amount of meals. Therefore, this 
does not have an effect on the results.
Furthermore, another important sustainability effect of 
replacing the reference system with the ROTINE PSS 
system, which has not been taken into account, is that 
the ROTINE box will most likely not end up as litter in 
nature, which is a great problem with single-use pack-
aging. Considering this aspect, the implementation of 
the PSS solution is even more preferable. 
In conclusion, the sLCA gives a good overview of the 
environmental performance of the reusable contain-
er and the PSS compared to the single-use packaging. 
However, a lot of assumptions were made throughout 
the assessment, which should be further validated in a 
proper LCA before publishing the results to the stake-
holders and consumers. That being said, an LCA study 
has not been made in this project, as it requires meet-
ing a lot of preconditions that are not within the project 
scope.
==PAGE:172==
164
Environmental impact
The usage of water will be higher due to the washing of the 
ROTINE boxes at the canteens. Furthermore, users of the sys-
tem will, if adhering to the norm, rinse them after use and re-
turn them, leading to more water used. However, a secondary 
benefit will be that people will buy meals which could have 
been wasted. Furthermore, if takeaway meals can help con-
sumers with providing portion served meals in their busy ev-
eryday life food waste might be saved as well. This is likely, 
as KK has proven to save food waste with the implementation 
of their SANS! Concept. 
Furthermore, another important sustainability effect of replac-
ing the reference system with the ROTINE PSS system is the 
fact that the ROTINE box will most likely not end up as litter 
in nature, which is a great problem with single-use packaging. 
Considering this aspect, the implementation of the PSS solu-
tion is even more preferable. 
Lastly, a huge material waste might happen if the system is 
not properly utilized, as the containers might end up in a stor-
age room. Therefore these considerations must be taken into 
account when detailing the implementation plan. The next 
Section will dive deeper into how to mitigate the potential 
barriers that can arrive in implementing the PSS solution, 
through well structured planning of a roadmap.

long run compared to single-use packaging (one of the ben-
efits from CE business models is to earn money on the same 
resources again and again). Therefore the cost of producing a 
meal for the canteen will become cheaper resulting in a higher 
profit per meal. Furthermore, as prices for single-use plastics 
are expected to increase even more in the next few years, the 
profit will increase even more. The extra profit earned makes 
it more likely that companies (canteens and takeaway restau-
rants) will be more inclined to spend money on sustainable 
initiatives as this is the main focus of the danish legislation.
Furthermore, if ROTINE becomes a great success this will 
have a negative influence on the economy for single-use pack-
aging manufacturers. 
Social impact
Consumers are expected to increase their consumption pattern 
for takeaway from canteens, as ROTINE makes it easy to get 
good and healthy food to an affordable price (especially at 
canteens like Edison Huset that do not have an established 
takeaway culture). 
This new consumption pattern might result in a decrease in 
the consumption of other takeaway food (e.g. unhealthy fast 
food) creating a positive impact on health. 
Furthermore, this will also increase the sustainable profile for 
both the canteen, the employees, and the house.

The hope is to create enough incentive and motivation from

Acceptance: The most common barrier, also described in the 
Section 02.3, is acquiring customer acceptance. Throughout 
this study it was found that accessibility and convenience are 
the most important characteristics to have in place in order to 
gain acceptance from the customers of takeaway systems (see 
Section 06.9 Affinity Diagram). Therefore, the platform has 
to meet most of the customers needs, and this will have to be 
tested in the roadmap.
Furthermore, the kitchen head chef at KK also made it clear 
at the last feedback session, that he would not like it if we 
changed his workways quite drastically, without properly ex-
plaining the benefits of doing so. So the final system would 
also have to be tested and evaluated with him and some of the 
other chefs. 
12.7 Implementation Plan
In this Section the implementation plan will be described 
as well as the potential barriers that need to be mitigated to 
implement the final solution successfully. Furthermore, this 
Section will revolve around how the final solution will be 
incorporated and which activities are necessary to enter the 
beachhead market (Kokkenes Køkken), the canteen market 
and lastly, the takeaway restaurant market.
Potential Barriers
Norms: Behaviours are hard to change, especially when the 
reason behind the existing behaviour is related to conve-
nience; consumers are used to ordering takeaway, where it is 
possible to throw the dirty packaging out after use instead for 
cleaning it and bringing it back into the system. As found in 
the literature study, the PSS will demand users to invest time 
and effort into the PSS, which could potentially lead to rejec-
tion (Section 02.3). Therefore, it is necessary to change the 
habits of the employees at Edison Huset and at other canteens 
as well to ensure the success of the ROTINE system. 
Furthermore, employees at Edison Huset have not been order-
ing takeaway because they were lacking the knowledge about 
==PAGE:177==
169
12/ Evaluating the Final Solution
and evaluation will be needed, which is why step 0.5 is the 
launch of a MVP 2.0, see Figure 12.6.1. 
Furthermore many activities are necessary along the timeline 
to reach the final goal which is expanding the ROTINE box 
system into the canteen- and takeaway restaurant industry.   
Some of the work that has been completed as a part of the 
design process needs to be reevaluated before launching the 
PSS. 
Whether the financial structure with a combination of con-
sumers primarily buying and secondarily renting containers 
is the right approach can be discussed and explored through 
further user studies. The price of the container should be re-
evaluated with potential users in order to determine the ideal 
pricepoint where users are willing to buy the containers but at 
the same time careful not to loose them.  Results and response 
from the MVP test would have been beneficial in evaluating 
the business model from the user perspective, but as conduct-
ing the test did not go as planned further tests will have to be 
made.
The sLCA should be seen as a rough comparison between the 
new solution and the reference system. In order to fully de-
Partnerships: The partnerships are really important, how-
ever, KK seems really invested and interested in the system 
under development. The most important thing to overcome is 
to find a reasonable price point for the subscription between 
KK and ROTINE, as this is the primary income. The discus-
sion about the subscription prices will therefore, be really 
important for the further work of making the product-service 
system feasible. Afterwards partnerships will be necessary for 
the other canteen in order to really make an environmental 
difference and expand to other markets. 
Lastly, restaurants with loyal customers will be crucial if we 
are to expand the business into the takeaway restaurant mar-
ket, see the 3 step plan in the roadmap, Figure 12.6.1.

to define the overarching strategic objective associated with 
each step while acting as a tool for communication (Product-
plan, n.d.)

tivities were touched upon to make the business viable, which 
was the system itself and the adaptation of it. Therefore, tests

13.1 Discussion 
Many choices have been made along the design process, 
which have influenced the final solution. Some of these deci-
sions have been made when deciding the focus for this thesis 
before the official start of the project. Here it was decided to 
focus on creating a reuse product service system for takeaway 
as an alternative to the current disposable packaging system. 
Other approaches to change the current system could have 
been applied, such as e.g. looking into optimising the recy-
clability of the disposable packaging. The PSS approach was 
chosen because it was seen as the most innovative solution 
for creating an entirely new way to think about packaging by 
incorporating user centred design approaches.
 
In the following Sections some of the most important choic-
es and factors that influenced the design process will be dis-
cussed.
 
Research
All research for the canteen market has been conducted in 
the canteen at Edison Huset, which is managed by Kokkenes 
Køkken. Edison Huset is therefore used as a model for all 
canteens in Kokkenes Køkken and even the rest of the can-
teen market. It is believed that the workways observed in the 
canteen at Edison Huset are representative for other canteens, 
at least when looking at the big picture, but features that are 
uniquely seen in Edison Huset might have influenced the re-
search conclusions. Small adjustments of the final solution 
might therefore be necessary if implemented in other can-
teens.
 
A lot of the research conducted in Kokkenes Køkken has been 
informed by the head chef Teddy Rose, as he is a part of many 
work processes related to the takeaway system. The other 
members of the canteen staff have contributed to the research 
within their field of expertise. The research conducted in the 
canteen may have been influenced by Teddy Rose’s subjective 
interpretation of how the system works. This subjectiveness 
would however be balanced by observations in the actual con-
text, as members of the master group conducted interviews 
and observed in the canteen.
 
As all other parts of society, the canteen at Edsion Huset has 
been influenced by the pandemic. The main influence in re-
gard to this project is fewer people frequenting the canteen. 
Takeaway orders for the canteen have been very limited, as 
employees were just starting to return to the canteen at the 
start of this project. This makes it difficult to document the 
extent of the current takeaway system accurately, as more em-
ployees might have placed orders outside of the pandemic. A 
more popular takeaway system would only be beneficial to 
the implementation of the solution.
 
The user centred perspective in the initial research comes 
from the generative design approach, where participants were 
asked to fill in workbooks on their latest takeaway experience. 
The material in the workbook has been constructed in order to 
let participants freely and creatively express their experienc-
es. Despite this intention the material might still have primed 
them through the questions and prompts. Questions like what 
they did with their packaging at the end of the meal could lead 
some of the participants to reflect upon sustainability aspects, 
which they might not have thought about otherwise. This can 
be both an advantage and a disadvantage, as it is desirable to 
get participants to reflect upon their interaction with packag-
ing, but undesirable to affect their view on it.
It can be discussed whether the experiences documented in 
the workbook reflect a typical takeaway experience for the 
participants. Some of the participants might have ordered 
takeaway because they wanted to document it for the research 
and not as a part of a regular routine. Even if this is the case 
the thoughts, feelings and behaviours documented in the 
workbooks are deemed to be a valid representation of a typi-
cal takeaway experience.
 
The participants in the generative design research were re-
cruited from the networks of the master group members. 
Recruiting people who already had a connection to the mas-
ter group made it much easier to gain access to descriptions 
and pictures from the participants’ own dining Table, which 
strangers might not have been willing to share. Being in close 
communication with participants made other aspects easier as 
well, such as instructing participants in the use of the Miro 
platform and how to fill in the workbook. Participants familiar 
to the master group were deemed to be more willing to par-
ticipate in several time-consuming research activities, such as 
filling out a workbook and participating in a workshop. The 
limitations to recruiting participants within friends and fam-
ily was the limited access to different age segments. Most of 
the participants in the generative design research are therefore 
between 20 and 30 years old, which may have influenced the 
results. The survey conducted later on (Section 06.7) include 
more age groups, though the majority of participants were 
still in their mid 20ies to 30ies.
The underlying structure of the survey turned out to have an 
error, which led some participants through questions on their 
experiences with buying takeaway from their canteen when 
==PAGE:183==
175
13/ Discussion, Conclusion and Reflection
they in fact had not tried buying takeaway from the canteen. 
The answers they provided are therefore based on assump-
tions about what their behaviour would be if they were to buy 
takeaway from their canteen. The answers are deemed rele-
vant for this project, but the error should be kept in mind.
 
Ideation, concept development and evaluation
Maj, Rune, Asmus and Teddy have been the primary repre-
sentatives for Kokkenes Køkken and the canteen business in 
general at both the co-creation session and at later concept 
evaluations. Their personal approaches to the canteen busi-
ness have probably affected the outcome of these sessions, as 
other stakeholders might have provided very different feed-
back. The four representatives have been chosen because they 
represent important aspects of the canteen business, from the 
CEO who has the overview of the entire business to the head 
chef, who knows what works in the canteen kitchen.
 
The evaluation with potential users was based on the restau-
rant takeaway context, as the system prototypes were based 
on ordering takeaway from restaurants. The feedback from 
the session might have been very different if the prototypes 
had been presented as a part of the canteen context, as a lot 
of the feedback was related to the complexity of the restau-
rant takeaway system. On the other hand, the takeaway from 
restaurant context made it simpler for the participants to relate 
to the system, as they had prior experiences with this context.
The potential users were introduced to all of the container 
concepts, but not all concept prototypes were detailed to the 
same level. This might have had an influence on their eval-
uations of the containers. The prototypes did, however, not 
stand alone, as the container concepts were explained to the 
participants as well.
 
Both the Functional and Eating experience container con-
cepts performed well in the evaluation with potential users 
and stakeholders from the canteen. The Functional concept 
could have been chosen for further development, but in the 
end the Eating experience concept was selected, as it repre-
sented a more innovative choice for the container, which suit-
ed the branding of Kokkenes Køkken well. Elements from 
the Functional concept have been implemented in the final 
solution in order to optimise the Eating experience concept, 
e.g. the clamps which hold the lid tightly sealed during trans-
port. Whether potential users will perceive the final container 
as a successful combination of the two container concepts will 
have to be evaluated in further user testing.
 
Some aspects of the final container concepts have only been 
validated with Kokkenes Køkken. This is true for the final 
form, as it was chosen at an evaluation session with represen-
tatives from the canteen. Potential user might have had other 
inputs for the final container shape. That being said, potential 
users have interacted and provided feedback on the initial pro-
totypes for the Eating experience concept, which had a similar 
shape to the final concept.
 
The choice of Kareline as the material for the base of the con-
tainer is mainly based on inputs from Kokkene Køkken as 
well. Their experiences with this material from the Loops tray 
have been incorporated in the evaluation. The fact that the 
material fits well with the brand identity of Kokkenes Køkken 
has been valued highly. The material choices for the differ-
ent concepts were shown to potential users at the evaluation 
workshop, but a formal evaluation was not conducted. It can 
be discussed whether a more formal evaluation with poten-
tial users would have influenced the choice of material, but 
in this case the input from Kokkenes Køkken was prioritised, 
as the material would have to be in line with their brand and 
approach to sustainability. The recyclability of Kareline is de-
pendent on keeping it in a closed recycling loop, where the 
containers are sent back to the manufacturers when broken 
or too worn to continue use. The fact that Kokkenes Køk-
ken already has implemented products made out of Kareline 
(the Loops tray) makes it likely that the recycling system will 
work for the ROTINE boxes as well. If it turns out not to be 
feasible to send the boxes back to the manufacturer it can be 
discussed whether a material that can be reused in the conven-
tional system would have been more appropriate.
 
There might be unforeseen costs related to the development 
and implementation of the final system, which have not been 
accounted for in the TCO calculation and which might change 
some of the results. The scenarios and assumptions used for 
the calculations might also need to be adjusted in the future. 
The TCO calculations should be regarded as rough estimates 
for whether the business model is feasible or not. Even if 
some of the calculations will have to be adjusted, most of the 
results represent pretty significant differences between the fi-
nal solution and the reference system.
 
This project has balanced between a holistic view of all the 
relevant elements related to the takeaway and canteen sys-
tem and trying to come up with detailed solutions for both 
system and container. The final solution should be seen as a 
steppingstone towards developing this system rather than a 
fully detailed concept.
==PAGE:184==
176
totyped and presented in a Concept Catalogue, which was 
shared and evaluated with stakeholders from KK. Further-
more, concepts were evaluated through an enacting, telling, 
making workshop. In the end a concept for both the system 
and the container was chosen. 
The final product service solution ROTINE has been devel-
oped and is presented in the Solution Catalogue. ROTINE in-
cludes a reusable container, with a system platform consisting 
of a website for canteen employees to manage menus, meals, 
orders and containers as well as an app for consumers to ex-
plore and order takeaway. The platform aims to motivate us-
ers by continuously updating the amounts of resources saved 
when using the system. 
Consumers buy into the system by purchasing a set of two 
containers, which is a one time fee. Canteens pay a monthly 
subscription covering both the service and products. Consum-
ers swap their containers whenever they are ordering a new 
meal, and can keep the containers for as long as they want in 
the meantime. Consumers also have the option to rent a set of 
containers. 
The ROTINE container is designed for circularity and emo-
tional durability. With its organic shape the container has a 
uniqueness and wow-factor that ensures recognisability. The 
elevational part in the bottom of the container facilitates a 
proper plating of food. 
The container’s bottom part is made from Kareline (50% PP 
and 50% sustainably sourced wood-fiber) which is recyclable 
in a closed loop system and the lid is made from transparent 
PP, which is recyclable in the danish system. Snaps are in-
cluded in the lid to ensure a proper sealing. A chip, which is 
molded into the container as well as NFC technology through 
the consumer app are used to support logistical activities, 
such as managing meals and locating containers. 
The total cost of ownership has been assessed and from this 
it is concluded that PSS is a viable business with a return of 
investment within two years when implemented in all can-
teens of KK. 
The environmental performance of the container is compared 
to single-use packaging through an sLCA. From this assess-
ment it was concluded that the container is more sustainable 
after 6 use-cycles and can endure up to 500 cycles, resulting 
in up to 83% CO2 saved (without EoL).
Before implementing the PSS solution to KK’s canteens and 
later on and expanding to the rest of the canteen industry and 
finally the restaurant takeaway industry, further work needs to 
be done, including further development of the container and 
13.2 Conclusion
As the environmental issues are becoming more and more 
predominant, the need for developing sustainable solutions 
and minimising the use of resources is paramount. During 
the covid-19 pandemic the takeaway market has dramati-
cally increased, which has resulted in enormous amounts of 
resources being used on single-use packaging. If the sustain-
able development goals, including the 12th goal sustainable 
consumption and production are to be met, new solutions that 
facilitate a circular use of resources needs to be developed and 
implemented. 
This report has investigated how Developing a Reusable 
Product-Service Solution for The Delivery and Takeaway 
Food Industry, with a User-Centred Design Approach can be 
done. Besides focusing on an user-centred approach the proj-
ect has been focusing business aspects as well as designing 
for sustainability and circularity. A collaboration with the can-
teen company Kokkenes Køkken was established. 
A literature study was done prior to the design process, in-
vestigating how methodology such as sustainability, CE, PSS, 
behavioural design and participatory design could be included 
in different phases of the design process. 
A market research was conducted to investigate potential 
markets and to evaluate the competitiveness and business po-
tential. A three step roadmap was mapped, to ensure a feasible 
penetration of the market, where the first step included proof 
of concept in one of Kokkenes Køkkens canteens, followed 
by an expansion to the rest of KK’s canteens and other can-
teens. The third step included the expansion to the rest of the 
delivery and restaurant takeaway industry. Thus the scope of 
the project was set to developing a solution for takeaway food 
for KK’s canteens, where the main users of the development 
arena were defined as the canteens, their employees and their 
customers (consumers). 
Expolarive user studies, including field research in Edison 
huset and generative design activities, was conducted to gain 
an understanding of the holistic system on several levels and 
to empathise with its users. Insights into breakdowns and op-
portunities, thoughts, experiences and behaviors were gath-
ered, mapped and categorised in an affinity diagram, which 
was used to form the first draft of the design specification for 
both the product, the service and the infrastructure of the PSS. 
In the ideation and concept development phase the require-
ments and criteria were evaluated with stakeholders from KK 
at a co-creation workshop. Ideation was done through several 
different brainstorming activities, and concepts for both the 
service and the container were drawn through morphology. 
Three system concepts and six container concepts were pro-
==PAGE:185==
177
13/ Discussion, Conclusion and Reflection
the final solution.
 
It might have been beneficial to focus more on the canteen 
context when developing the initial system concepts. The ini-
tial concepts were created with the restaurant market in mind 
in order to make sure that the system would be scalable and 
that the evaluation with potential users could be based on their 
experience with the restaurant takeaway market. Adaptations 
for the canteen market were then suggested. An earlier focus 
on the canteen market would have made it easier to use the 
provided feedback directly in the further concept develop-
ment, as the final solution is focused on the canteen context.
 
The planning for this project has tended to be overly opti-
mistic. Ambitions for conducting a lot of different research 
and design activities at once have sometimes overshadowed 
a more stringent focus, which would make the whole process 
less complex. An example of this is the effort to apply lean 
work methods in the form of an MVP test, which was set up 
in Edison Huset at the same times as other research activities 
were conducted. The lack of focus on the MVP test made it 
difficult to continuously check up on whether the test was run-
ning smoothly and communicate with the canteen employees. 
The test might have been successful if there had been more 
time dedicated to being present at the canteen.  
 
Completing the project during a global pandemic has not 
been without challenges. The pandemic made it very difficult 
to find an office for the group to use and meant that access 
to production facilities for e.g. creating prototypes has been 
limited, especially at the start of the project. That being said, 
adjustments have been made in order to make the best out of a 
difficult situation. The collaboration tool Miro has been a core 
part of the workflow throughout the project, as it allowed for 
online collaboration and could be used for on the wall anal-
ysis. Miro and Microsoft Teams have been key tools when 
facilitating online workshops, as material and selected boards 
could be prepared in advance and participants could access 
the material and contribute at the same time. The shift to an 
online workshop
format has not been without challenges, as participants had to 
be introduced to the new tools.
 
Two of the master group members unfortunately tested posi-
tive for Covid towards the end of the project. This was a chal-
lenging time, as all members had to stay in isolation and the 
productivity of the two sick members was at very low point 
during this period, which increased the time pressure on the 
final part of the project.  
the system platforms. 
As the ROTINE solution is further developed, this will help 
facilitate a circular use of resources, thus help reach the 12th 
sustainable development goal. Furthermore, the final PSS fa-
cilitates canteen’s sale of takeaway, either creating a whole 
new business for canteens or significantly improving existing 
solutions to ultimately upscale the sale of takeaway. 
13.3 Reflection 
The focus for the project has undergone iterations along the 
way. Creating a solution for the restaurant takeaway market 
was initially seen as the main focus, where the canteen market 
mainly served as an initial arena for trying out and refining the 
solution. Throughout the design process the complexity of the 
restaurant takeaway market became apparent and the focus 
was therefore shifted to creating a solution for canteen market 
with the possibility to expand to the restaurant market in the 
future. As a result of the shift of focus the delivery aspect of 
takeaway is something that will be considered in the future 
work, as delivery of takeaway is not a part of the investigat-
ed canteen takeaway system. A more stringent focus on the 
canteen market from the start would have made it easier to 
navigate the design process.
Participatory design methods have been chosen as the main 
approach for developing a user centered design. Other ap-
proaches, where the designer has an expert mindset and the 
user is viewed as a subject of the research rather than an active 
contributor, could have been applied instead. 
 
The master group wanted to gain a thorough understanding 
of many different aspects related to takeaway from canteens 
and restaurants. Therefore, several approaches have been 
used to investigate the system, including extensive market 
research, generative design, contextual design methods, ob-
servations and surveys. Furthermore, a wide range of methods 
has been used in the idea and concept evaluation phase, in-
cluding co-creation, input from users through workshops and 
evaluations with Kokkenes Køkken. The vast amount of input 
gathered from all of these methods was very challenging to 
implement in the development of the final concept. Efforts 
have been made in order to condense all inputs into tangible 
guides for the design such as the design specification. The 
extensive research did help create an informed final concept, 
which has been based on inputs from both potential consum-
ers and representatives for the canteen market. The extensive 
research phase and time used processing all the insights was 
a priority, which resulted in less time available for detailing 
==PAGE:186==
178
shops and provided thorough feedback on concepts even with 
very short notice. They have also answered questions that the 
master group had throughout the project quickly and effec-
tively.
 The collaboration between members of the master group has 
been very successful. The fact that the group consisted of four 
members made it easy to effectively split up the workload, 
as the group was able to split up into pairs and conduct sev-
eral activities in parallel. This has been done throughout the 
research and design process, where main responsibility for 
different aspects have been divided, which allowed members 
to work with the methods and approaches they felt most pas-
sionate about. The pair constellations have shifted throughout 
project, so all members have had the chance to work closely 
together.
 
The collaboration between group members have been 
strengthen through practices adopted from the SCRUM ap-
proach. Daily stand-ups have proved very valuable, as the 
group needed to establish a joint overview of tasks in order 
to work effectively. An adaptation of the SCRUM master role 
has been beneficial as well, as this role (which shifted be-
tween group members every week) made sure that one mem-
ber was in charge of keeping the process on track timewise 
and communicating with external stakeholders.
A ritual for the end of every milestone helped the group eval-
uate the process. The starfish model exercise summarised 
which work practices should continue in the following phase 
and which should be left behind. The exercise was used to 
evaluate the social dynamic in the group as well, in order to 
identify ways to strengthen the collaborations and dissolve 
potential conflicts. Additional efforts to strengthen the rela-
tionship between group members has been prioritised in the 
form of weekly dinners, silly traditions and even new forms 
of communication.
 
In order to communicate the research and design process to 
stakeholders an Instagram account was created. The account 
has served as a daily reminder to document activities related 
to the process and has proven to be an effective tool when an 
opinion outside the group was needed in order to decide on 
things such as the logo. An unexpected advantage of the ac-
count has been that the effective communication with friends 
and family led to easier recruitment of participants for re-
search, as many showed an interest in the project after fol-
lowing the Instagram account. The account strengthened the 
communication between the master group and the head chef 
at Edison Huset Teddy Rose, as Teddy followed the account 
closely and thereby remained updated on project activities.
 
The collaboration between the master group and Kokkenes 
Køkken has been effective throughout the project. Represen-
tatives from Kokkenes Køkken have participated in work-
